# RETURN OF THE CITIZEN
## Triumph of the Individual in an Age of Collective Insanity

*By Will Schulz and Mike Suppes*

---

# Preface

This book began as an argument between friends.

We were talking, one of us from an individualist starting point and the other from a civic-republican one, about a question neither of us could stop asking: what has happened to the American citizen? Not the American voter, who shows up every four years and is endlessly surveyed and tracked. Not the American consumer, whose preferences are the object of the largest data-gathering apparatus in human history. The citizen — the person who inhabits a public world with equals, who can still be addressed in the second person plural, who owes something to that world and is owed something by it in return. We agreed that the citizen had gone missing. We agreed that the disappearance was a catastrophe. We did not agree on why.

One of us tends to describe the problem in the language of the individual: people have been absorbed into ideological cults, severed from the reasoning minds that make them free, captured by mass movements whose grammar is the grammar of the mob. The fix is the recovery of the single person, standing on his or her own reason, thinking openly and arguing honestly with others who do the same.

The other tends to describe the problem in the language of the polity: people have been stripped of the associations that form character, of the institutions that tell them who they are, of the public space in which the virtues are practiced. The fix is the recovery of the civic — the rebuilding, from the ground up, of the shared life without which the individual is only a rumor of itself.

These two framings do not obviously agree. They have been arguing with each other for forty years in the academy and for longer than that in American life. The classical liberal who reads the civic republican hears the faint footfalls of Vermeule and the administrative theocracy. The civic republican who reads the classical liberal hears the hum of Amazon and the loneliness epidemic. Each camp can produce a respectable library of books demonstrating that the other is the real danger.

We have concluded that both of us are right and that the apparent disagreement is the book. The citizen is degraded by atomization and by mass movement at once — because they are the same phenomenon seen from different angles. Hannah Arendt and Eric Hoffer saw this in 1951. The lonely person, the person stripped of a world, becomes raw material for the movement that promises a world. You cannot save the individual without restoring the polity, and you cannot restore the polity without respecting the individual. The tradition that understood this best is the one whose name is on the cover of this book. The citizen is an individual who belongs.

We have tried to write the book that sees both halves at once. We have also tried, as much as two writers can, to earn the right to the arguments we make. In practice this has meant spending a long time with thinkers neither of us would naturally cite. We have read Patrick Deneen carefully enough to recognize what is true in his diagnosis and what is dangerous in his prescription. We have read John Rawls carefully enough to see why his critics are not bad-faith traditionalists. We have read Robert Putnam's data closely enough to feel their weight. We have read Eric Hoffer and Hannah Arendt end-to-end, which is not the same as quoting them, and it has changed what we think about what is happening now. We have tried to treat every camp we engage with the way we would want our own position treated if it were the one on the other side of an argument.

A few notes before we begin.

On the word *cult*. We use it, in the pages that follow, to describe a specific kind of ideological formation whose features are well-documented — by Robert Lifton, Margaret Singer, and Steven Hassan, and by Hoffer and Arendt before them. We do not use it to describe every political movement we disagree with. We apply it, when we apply it, symmetrically: to the far left and to the far right, to secular ideologies and to religious ones, to the "woke" tribe and to the populist tribe, to identity radicalisms and to tech eschatologies alike. The point of the analysis is not that the other side is a cult and our side is fine. The point is that the conditions of modern life produce the kind of person who is susceptible to mass formations, and that this susceptibility is the common property of everyone reading this book, including us.

On partisanship. We are citizens of no party. This is not a claim of neutrality — we have political opinions and the attentive reader will be able to infer some of them — but a claim about what we think this book is for. A book that scores points against a named political figure makes a smaller claim on the future than a book that tries to say something durable about human beings in public life. We have tried to write the latter.

On three volumes. This is a long book, and we do not apologize for it. A question this old and this hard is not going to yield to a fast argument. The three-volume structure — foundation, diagnosis, renewal — is the only honest architecture we could find. If the reader picks up only one volume, we hope it will be Volume III, which is the one that costs something to write and, we think, something to do.

On who this book is for. It is for the citizen in the old sense: the person who reads seriously, thinks for herself, and is still open to being wrong. It is not for the partisan who wants ammunition, and it is not for the cynic who has already concluded that the public world is over. We believe it is not over. We believe most Americans, across every line that currently separates them, want something better than what our public life has become. This book is our attempt to say what that something could be, and how a person might begin to build it.

We have made every argument we could make as honestly as we know how. The rest is with the reader.

*Will Schulz and Mike Suppes*
*April 2026*


---

# Introduction
## The Citizen Missing from the Room

There is a figure who used to stand at the center of political life in the countries that call themselves republics. He — and, belatedly, she — appears under many names in many traditions: *polites*, *civis*, *burgher*, *citoyen*, *citizen*. The figure is always roughly the same. The citizen is a person who belongs to a political community of equals, who participates in its common life, who is bound by its laws, who bears responsibility for its character, and whose personhood is in some deep sense completed by the participation rather than merely exercised through it. The word does not simply mean *resident* or *voter* or *taxpayer*. It means, at root, *one who shares in the polis* — one who is a partner in the common thing.

This figure has gone missing from the room.

You can feel the absence in the texture of public life. We talk as consumers talking to other consumers, through platforms designed to measure attention and extract it. We talk as partisans to enemies we have never met, through systems that reward contempt and punish sympathy. We talk as therapy clients to therapists who never answer, pouring our grievances into feeds that never reply. What we almost never do is *address each other as citizens* — as people who share a polity, who owe each other an explanation, who are jointly responsible for the common thing we are building or wrecking.

This is a book about why the citizen disappeared, what the disappearance has cost us, and how — concretely, patiently, with the tools we already have — the citizen might return.

## The Puzzle

We live at what is, by almost every measurable standard, the best moment in the history of our species. Global extreme poverty has fallen by more than a billion people in a generation. Childhood mortality has collapsed. Information that a medieval monarch could not have bought at any price is free to anyone with a phone. Violent crime in most rich countries is a fraction of what it was in the 1970s. The great killers of the twentieth century — famine, war between great powers, smallpox — are either extinct or in retreat.

And yet.

Suicide rates among young Americans have climbed relentlessly for two decades. The U.S. Surgeon General has declared an "epidemic of loneliness." The percentage of Americans who trust their neighbors to return a lost wallet has fallen. The percentage who know their neighbors' names has fallen. The percentage who believe democracy is essential to a good life has fallen. Trust in nearly every major American institution — Congress, the press, the church, the university, the criminal justice system, the corporation — has fallen, in most cases by half or more, over the past fifty years. Each of the two largest political parties now regards the other, in survey after survey, not as rival but as existential threat. And the movements attracting the most intense loyalties — on the illiberal left and the illiberal right, in the identity crusades and the technological eschatologies — are movements whose core promise is that the regime which produced this abundance is corrupt beyond repair and must be torn down.

This is the paradox that has to be explained. A society that has delivered unprecedented material goods has produced a citizenry that is unhappy, isolated, furious, and drawn to political movements built on the premise that the society is irredeemable.

Every serious book in the last ten years has tried to explain this puzzle. Patrick Deneen says it is because liberalism succeeded. Robert Putnam says it is because the civic associations collapsed. Yuval Levin says it is because the institutions became platforms. Jonathan Haidt says it is because the phones rewired the brains. Michael Sandel says it is because the merit regime humiliated the losers. Chris Bail says it is because the algorithms distorted the public mirror. Ezra Klein says it is because partisan identity ate every other identity. Each of these explanations has empirical weight behind it. None of them, alone, is enough.

This book's answer is both simpler and older. Those accounts are describing, from different angles, a single phenomenon the twentieth century was the first to name clearly: the hollowing-out of the person and the rise of the mass.

## The Two Halves of a Single Problem

Hannah Arendt and Eric Hoffer, writing independently and publishing in the same year, 1951, saw the same thing. The totalitarian movements of their century had not recruited from the ranks of the politically engaged and the associationally thick. They had recruited from among the atomized — the unemployed, the dispossessed, the lonely, the people who had lost their place in a world. Arendt put it with characteristic bluntness: loneliness is "the common ground for terror." Hoffer, in plainer American prose, observed that the true believer is running *from himself* as much as toward a cause — "losing one's individual distinctness in a compact collective whole" — and that mass movements are therefore interchangeable at their psychological core. Whether the movement is fascist, communist, nationalist, religious, or revolutionary is a matter of local accident. The human raw material is the same.

This was one of the twentieth century's most important insights, and it has been largely forgotten. Most contemporary analysis treats the rise of mass politics and the collapse of civic life as two different problems. They are one problem. The person stripped of family, neighborhood, congregation, union, club, and vocation is the person available for capture by the tribe. You do not get cults in a society with thick local life. You get cults in a society where the local life has been stripped away and replaced with nothing.

The implication cuts in both directions.

If you take only the first half of the argument — the individual side — you get what a great deal of well-meaning contemporary writing looks like. You urge people to think critically, open their minds, seek diverse viewpoints, resist the extremes. This is not wrong, but it is radically incomplete. It is advice given to a lonely man standing in a parking lot. The advice does not reach him, because the conditions that made him susceptible in the first place — the absence of anyone in his life to think with, to argue with, to be accountable to — have not changed. Critical thinking is not a cognitive style you can download. It is a practice that requires a community of other minds.

If you take only the second half — the civic side — you get the kind of pious communitarian writing that sends every reader to plant a garden with his neighbors. This, too, is not wrong, and in the last chapter of this book we will ask something not far removed from it. But the civic recommendation, offered alone, misses that the person who will have to do the planting is a modern individual, formed by modern freedom, and that any proposal to rebuild civic life on ancient foundations has to reckon with the fact that the ancient foundations were made of inequalities — of sex, of class, of religion, of race — that we rightly will not resurrect. The modern citizen has to be a citizen *and* an individual. The tradition that understood this best, intermittently and without the name, is the one this book tries to recover.

## Four Traditions and What Each One Gets Right

Before we can recover that tradition, we have to walk through the company it keeps. Four major intellectual traditions have laid serious claim to the word *citizen* in the last two centuries, and the first volume of this book is a tour through each. The very short version:

**Classical liberalism** — from Locke through Kant and Mill to Rawls and Fukuyama — begins with the individual. The person comes before the polity; rights are held against the state; the purpose of political order is to secure those rights so that each person can pursue his own conception of the good. Classical liberalism's central achievement is moral: the recognition that every human being is an end in himself, that no one is merely a subject, that the political community exists for its members rather than the other way around. Its central danger is that, in bracketing the question of the good, it can leave its adherents without a shared language for anything that matters most to them, and thus without the resources to resist the tribes that do offer such a language.

**Civic republicanism** — from Aristotle and Cicero through Machiavelli and Harrington to Arendt and Pettit — begins with the polity. The person is completed by participation in public life; liberty is best understood not as absence of restraint but as the condition of not being at the mercy of an arbitrary power; a self-governing people requires a certain kind of character, which is what "virtue" meant before the word turned moralistic. Civic republicanism's central achievement is its account of freedom as *non-domination*, which is indispensable for understanding the unfreedom of the modern worker, the modern debtor, and the modern user. Its central danger is that, in emphasizing the common good, it can slide into the imposition of a *particular* good, and thus into a soft or hard authoritarianism.

**Communitarianism** — Sandel, MacIntyre, Taylor, Walzer, Bellah — arose in the 1980s as a direct response to Rawls. Its claim is that the liberal self, the one choosing its values from behind a veil of ignorance, is a philosophical fiction and a sociological catastrophe. Real selves are constituted by traditions, communities, and relationships they did not choose. A politics that pretends otherwise produces the alienated, atomized individual who ends up in a mass movement. Communitarianism's central achievement is its diagnosis of why liberal societies generate their own worst enemies. Its central danger is that, taken too far, it loses the capacity to criticize the communities it reveres.

**Post-liberalism** — Deneen, Vermeule, Ahmari, Hazony — is the most recent and most aggressive of the critiques. Its claim is that liberalism has not gone wrong but rather succeeded: what it set out to do, it has done, and what it has done is the wreckage. The post-liberals do not want to reform liberalism; they want to replace it. The most honest thing we can say about post-liberalism is that its diagnosis is often piercing and its prescriptions are often frightening. We engage it in this book because a generation of readers will engage it, and the book that pretends otherwise will be talking past its audience.

Our own position, built across Volume I, is neither a fifth tradition nor a synthesis by committee. It is closest to what Richard Dagger has called "republican liberalism" — the thesis that the liberal achievement and the republican achievement are not rivals but complements, and that the citizen as a type requires both. A citizen is an individual, with rights against the state and a conscience that is his own, *and* a member of a polity, bound in a common life with equals whose good is also his good. Neither half without the other. That is the thesis this book defends.

## What the Argument Asks of the Reader

We are going to ask three things.

First, we are going to ask you to read past your own camp. If you begin the book as a libertarian individualist, we will ask you to read Sandel and MacIntyre charitably. If you begin as a communitarian, we will ask you to take Mill and Berlin at their strongest. If you begin as a post-liberal, we will ask you to read Fukuyama's *Liberalism and Its Discontents* without preparing rebuttals. If you begin as a progressive, we will ask you to take the populist diagnosis seriously — not its prescriptions, but its underlying grievance. If you begin as a conservative, we will ask the same about the left critique of economic power. This is not a plea for centrism. It is a plea for attention. The book that wins its arguments by ignoring the strongest version of the opposing case has nothing to teach anyone who already held that opposing case, which is to say it has nothing to teach most of its potential readers.

Second, we are going to ask you to entertain the possibility that the problem is at once larger and smaller than you think. Larger, because it is not a matter of which party wins the next election or which policy passes the next congress. Smaller, because the answer — whatever shape it eventually takes at scale — has to begin in lives no bigger than yours and ours, in institutions no more glamorous than the school board and the neighborhood association and the bowling league. Volume III will argue that there is no other scale at which the repair can start.

Third, we are going to ask you to consider that the mood of the moment — the cynicism, the defeatism, the belief that the system is beyond fixing, the secret or not-so-secret hope that someone will come along and burn it all down — is itself a symptom of the disease rather than an accurate perception of the prognosis. A people that believes itself to be doomed will be doomed; a people that believes itself capable of common action will, eventually, act. The single most important political act, in a moment like ours, is to refuse the story that nothing can be done.

## A Brief Tour of the Structure

**Volume I — The Citizen.** Seven chapters on the philosophical foundation. The oldest question; the four traditions in turn; the American quarrel between the Federalists and the Anti-Federalists as the first great local version of the same argument; and, in the concluding chapter, our own reconstruction — republican liberalism — as a usable answer that carries forward into the rest of the book.

**Volume II — Broken Systems in an Age of Abundance.** Seven chapters on what has actually happened. The paradox of plenty; the return of mass-movement psychology; the collapse of social capital; the institutional decay; the polarization findings; the platform public sphere; and the hollowing of work and merit. We engage each of the major explanations on offer, note where they disagree, and argue for a synthesis in which the loneliness thesis — Arendt's and Hoffer's — is the connective tissue.

**Volume III — The Citizen Revitalized.** Seven chapters on what to do. Why most solutions fail. The surprisingly large empirical literature on what actually works to depolarize and rebuild. Critical thinking as civic discipline. Institutional renewal from the inside out. Deliberative democracy in practice. The personal practices of a public life. And, finally, a picture of what a country of returning citizens would look like — not as utopia, but as a place we could plausibly build from here.

And a conclusion, which is the book's moral argument in its barest form: *what we owe each other*.

## A Personal Word

The two of us who wrote this book did not start from the same place. The book itself is an argument we have been having with each other for several years. The reader will be able to feel, in some chapters, one of our voices more than the other. We have not tried to erase this. A book about the citizen, written by two citizens, arguing in public toward a common thesis, is closer to the thing it argues for than a book of seamless corporate prose would have been.

The citizen is not a nostalgic figure. The citizen is a figure of the future, or of nothing at all. This is a book about how to choose the first.

Let us begin.


---

# VOLUME I
## The Citizen

*Political philosophy, history, and the relationship between the individual and the state, from first principles.*

---

# Chapter 1
## The Oldest Question

Somewhere in the world tonight a woman will close her laptop, push back from a kitchen table, and try to explain to herself what she owes the country she happens to live in. She has had a hard day. Her job is not quite what she was promised it would be. Her rent has gone up again. Her parents, a thousand miles away, are getting older in a place she is not sure she will move back to. On the screen in front of her, before she closed it, two strangers were screaming at each other about a politician neither of them has met. She closes the laptop not because she has reached a conclusion but because she has reached a limit. And in the silence of the kitchen, for perhaps the first time that week, an old and embarrassing question rises unbidden: *what does any of this have to do with me?*

It is an embarrassing question because we have been trained, in the moral language of our time, to have instant answers to it. She should vote. She should be informed. She should follow the news. She should buy from ethical companies and avoid unethical ones. She should be "civically engaged," a phrase that has come, in our generation, to mean almost nothing at all. But none of these answers touches what she is actually asking. She is not asking what a responsible consumer does. She is asking what she *is* — whether the nation of three hundred and thirty million strangers whose laws she lives under has any real claim on her, and whether, conversely, she has any real claim on it. She is asking the oldest question in political philosophy, and she is asking it alone, at a kitchen table, which is roughly where most Americans now encounter it.

The question is this. What does a human being owe the political community, and what does the community owe the human being in return?

It is the oldest question because every serious political thinker in the Western tradition, from Plato to the present, has had to answer it before getting anywhere else. It is the hardest question because a political order cannot exist without an answer, and yet no answer is obviously right. And it is the question our age most needs to ask again — not in the vague and moralizing register in which it is usually mumbled, but clearly, in the manner of people who understand that everything else depends on what they say next.

This book begins from the premise that the question has not been settled. It has been answered, and answered seriously, and answered incompatibly, at least four times in the history of our civilization. Each of those answers has sophisticated defenders still writing today. Each of them, taken alone, produces a recognizable political style and a recognizable set of pathologies. And the public life of the early twenty-first century has been warped, in part, by our reluctance to admit that the question is still open — by a series of attempts to evade it rather than confront it. The citizen has gone missing, we argued in the introduction, because we no longer know what a citizen is. That is not a rhetorical claim. It is a philosophical one, and we mean to defend it at length.

But first: a word about why the question is hard, and why the four answers that have been given to it are worth lingering over before any of them is chosen.

***

There is a temptation, in any book of this kind, to skip to the part where the author tells you what he thinks. That is the temptation of the pundit, who has strong opinions and a column to fill, and it is the reason most books about politics decay within ten years of publication. We are going to resist it. A question that has been live for twenty-five centuries does not deserve to be settled in a paragraph, and a reader who has come this far deserves to see the question worked before she sees the answer. More practically, we have found that every contemporary political argument we have encountered — our own included, before we started writing — turns out to rest on an unexamined answer to the oldest question. The culture-war argument between a conservative and a progressive on cable news is very rarely an argument about the policy on the table. It is almost always an argument, two or three layers down, about what a human being is in public and what a political community is for. Neither debater typically knows that that is what they are arguing about, which is why the arguments never end.

So let us try to say, as plainly as possible, what the four serious answers have been.

The first answer is that the human being is completed by the political community. On this view, associated with Aristotle in the *Politics* and carried forward through Cicero and Machiavelli into the contemporary work of Hannah Arendt and Philip Pettit, the polis is not an add-on to a pre-existing individual. It is the environment in which the capacities that make us human — reason, speech, judgment, friendship, justice — are developed and exercised. A person outside the polis, Aristotle wrote, is either a beast or a god. He meant it literally. The polity is not a service provider; it is the place where a creature of our kind becomes what it is. The citizen, accordingly, is not a holder of rights but a participant in ruling and being ruled in turn. Freedom, on this picture, is not the absence of interference. It is the condition of being a partner in a common life under laws one has some hand in making.

The second answer is that the human being is prior to the political community, and that the community exists to secure the rights the individual already holds by nature. On this view, associated with John Locke in the *Second Treatise* and carried forward through Kant and Mill into the contemporary work of John Rawls and Francis Fukuyama, the person comes first, and political authority is legitimate only to the extent that it can be derived from the consent of persons. Rights are held *against* the state. The state's central business is not to form citizens but to leave them alone — or, more precisely, to secure the conditions under which each can pursue his or her own conception of the good without being dominated by the conceptions of others. The citizen, accordingly, is a rights-bearer, a consenter, a subject of equal concern and respect. Freedom is non-interference, or something close to it. The political community is, in the severe phrase of the tradition, a framework rather than a fate.

The third answer is that neither of the first two is quite right, because each misdescribes the kind of being the human being actually is. On this view, associated in its modern form with Alasdair MacIntyre, Michael Sandel, Charles Taylor, and Michael Walzer, the real self is not the reasoning agent of liberal theory nor the public-minded citizen of civic republican theory but something more deeply constituted: a creature made by traditions, languages, and relationships it did not choose, pursuing goods it inherits rather than goods it invents. A political order that pretends otherwise — that tries to be neutral among ways of life, that treats the self as the unencumbered chooser of its own values — produces, in practice, atomized individuals who no longer know who they are and who therefore become available for capture by any movement that will tell them. The citizen, on this account, is a particular human being embedded in particular communities with particular commitments, and the political task is not to abstract from those particularities but to honor them. This is the answer of the communitarians.

The fourth answer is the most recent and the most disquieting. Associated with Patrick Deneen, Adrian Vermeule, Sohrab Ahmari, and Yoram Hazony, and foreshadowed by Christopher Lasch, it holds that the liberal project has not gone astray but has *succeeded* — that what it set out to produce, it has produced, and that what it has produced is the wreckage around us. The unencumbered self, the global market, the administrative state, the hollowing of family and congregation and neighborhood, the loneliness and despair that now register in the suicide statistics of every Western country — these are not failures of liberal modernity but its fruits. The answer follows: liberalism cannot be reformed from within, because its internal logic is the problem. It must be replaced by something older and thicker, some recovery of a substantive common good the liberal order was designed to evade. The citizen, on this picture, is the bearer of a tradition that the current regime is actively dissolving, and whose recovery will require something more than a seminar.

We will spend a chapter on each of these traditions in what follows, and a chapter on the particularly American quarrel in which all four have been refracted for two centuries. We will not name a winner prematurely. What we want the reader to see, at the very start, is that each of these answers has been given by serious people, defended by serious argument, and confirmed in practice by serious political cultures. None is a straw. Each captures something the others struggle to capture. And the moment at which we start choosing among them — which we will eventually have to do — is a moment of real philosophical commitment, not the moment at which we finally stop asking the question.

***

Why does it matter that we get the question right before we get too fond of any answer?

Because most contemporary political argument is defective at exactly this point, and the defect is not a matter of taste. When a modern American politician says "the American people deserve better," he is not making an argument; he is assuming an answer to the oldest question. He is assuming that "the American people" exists as a singular moral subject, that it has interests distinguishable from the aggregated preferences of its members, that it is owed something by someone, and that the someone is generally not himself. When a modern American commentator says that voters are choosing "against their own interests," she is assuming an answer too — one in which the interests of an individual are detachable from his membership in a place, a class, a faith, a story, and in which her own view of those interests is the view from nowhere. When a modern populist says that the elites have "betrayed the real country," he is assuming yet another answer — that there is a real country beneath the legal one, that it has a moral standing the legal one lacks, and that he speaks for it. None of these speakers has done the philosophical work to earn his assumption. Each is shouting into a public square in which the foundational question has been bracketed, and the bracketing is mistaken for neutrality.

The effect, across a generation, has been to reduce the citizen to a series of pictures, each of them partial and each of them distorting. The citizen has become a consumer, whose sovereignty consists in choosing among goods others have produced and whose political life is exhausted by the act of preferring this product to that one — and, by analogy, this candidate to that one. The citizen has become a voter, a unit measured every few years and otherwise left alone, addressed primarily through algorithmic advertising and dismissed between election cycles. The citizen has become a taxpayer, a source of revenue whose moral claim on the system is proportional to the size of his contribution, which is the picture the libertarian right and the technocratic center share without wanting to admit it. The citizen has become a victim, a recipient of harms whose political identity is organized around the wrong done to him and whose legitimate claims are indexed to that wrong, which is the picture much of the contemporary left has inherited without examining. Each of these pictures is an answer to the oldest question — a very thin answer, an answer small enough to fit inside a slogan, but an answer — and each of them, put into political practice, produces a characteristic deformation of public life.

The consumer-citizen cannot build anything, because he cannot conceive of a good that is not private. The voter-citizen cannot sustain anything, because his participation is too thin and too episodic to form habits. The taxpayer-citizen cannot love anything, because his relation to the polity is a cost-benefit calculation that returns, as cost-benefit calculations do, a negative answer in most of the cases that matter. The victim-citizen cannot act in concert with anyone who is not himself a victim in the same register, which is to say, cannot act politically at all. What these deformed pictures have in common is that they all locate the citizen outside the political community, looking in — as customer, as spectator, as contributor, as complainant — rather than inside it as a member. And they do so, we think, because the deeper question of what it means to be inside has been abandoned. You cannot recover a practice you have ceased to describe.

This is why we insist on starting here. The argument of this book is that every serious contemporary diagnosis of our public life — the loneliness, the polarization, the institutional rot, the platform pathologies, the rise of mass movements on left and right — is downstream of a forgotten philosophical question. Get the question back, and most of the contemporary confusions begin to sort themselves out. Leave the question buried, and you are condemned to treat symptoms whose root you have agreed not to see.

***

Consider the word itself. *Citizen* is not a neutral English term, though it functions as one in ordinary speech. It is the Anglicization of the French *citoyen*, which is the Frenchification of the Latin *civis*, which is the Latinization of a concept the Romans inherited and transformed from the Greek *polites*. At every stage the word has carried two meanings in tension — membership in a political community and participation in its common life — and the tension has been productive rather than accidental. If the etymology is worth pausing over, it is because the English version of the word has lost the tension almost entirely. "Citizen" in contemporary American English means something close to *person with papers*. In its oldest and most demanding sense, it meant something much nearer to *partner in a common thing*.

The Greek *polites* is built on the root *polis*, the self-governing city. A *polites* was not merely someone who lived inside the walls; metics and slaves lived inside the walls, and they were not *politai*. A *polites* was a full partner in the ruling of the city — someone who served on juries, voted in the assembly, held offices by rotation, fought in the phalanx, and inherited the founding stories of the place as his own. Aristotle, in Book III of the *Politics*, defines the citizen precisely as one who "shares in ruling and being ruled" — not a subject of rule, not merely a rights-bearer, but a participant in the reciprocal authority of a free community. The definition is relentlessly participatory. A citizen who does nothing is a citizen in name only; a citizen who is ruled without ever ruling is not yet a citizen at all. And the community itself, on Aristotle's account, is not a mere alliance of individuals for mutual advantage. It is a *koinonia*, a communion, organized around a shared conception of the human good and sustained by the friendship of its members. A *polites* is not a customer of his polis. He is, in a deep sense, part of what the polis is.

The Latin *civis* carries the same structure but inflects it differently. The Romans were not Athenians. Their city was not a face-to-face democracy; their political imagination was shaped by the long history of the republic, by law, by contract, by the structural checks that ran between Senate and people, consul and tribune, patrician and plebeian. Cicero, in *De Re Publica*, gave the tradition its most enduring formulation. The *res publica*, he wrote, is not any gathering of human beings any way associated, but an *iuris consensu et utilitatis communione sociatus* — an assemblage united by a common agreement about law and by a common sharing in advantage. The phrase is untranslatable in its density, but its two pillars are worth holding. First, the republic is a *res* — a *thing*, a public thing, something that has being apart from any one of its members. Second, it is the property of a people, the *populus*, defined not as an aggregate but as a moral community constituted by its agreement about justice. A *civis* is not just a resident of Rome. He is a co-owner of the *res publica*, with obligations to it and rights within it, and his political life is the exercise of that co-ownership. When the *res publica* falls into the hands of a single man, as Cicero knew it was falling in his lifetime, it ceases to be a *res publica* at all — no matter what anyone continues to call it. The vocabulary already includes a warning against its own degradation.

By the time the word reaches the French Revolution, it has acquired a still more charged edge. The *citoyen* of 1789 is not an inheritance from the past but a claim against it. To be addressed as *citoyen* rather than *monsieur* was to be addressed as a moral equal, a partner in the new order, a bearer of rights that could not be alienated by throne or altar. The word carried the democratic revolution inside it. And yet the revolutionaries who used it drew, often quite explicitly, on the Roman and Greek vocabulary they had read in their *lycées*: the *citoyen* was the *civis*, the *civis* was the *polites*, and all three were members of a self-governing people who had taken their freedom seriously enough to fight for it. The word, across the whole arc of its history, refuses the modern contrast between individual and community. To be a citizen is to be a particular kind of individual — one whose personhood is partly constituted by belonging to a free commonwealth, and whose freedom is partly the freedom to help shape what that commonwealth will become.

This is not an antiquarian point. The reason the word matters is that in carrying forward its etymology we are carrying forward the weight of the oldest answer — the civic-republican answer — woven into the term itself. When a modern American hears the word *citizen*, even if he does not know any of this, some trace of it reaches him. He feels, obscurely, that a citizen is not merely a resident and not merely a voter, that the word is asking more of him than either of those pallid terms would. He is right. The word is asking more of him. It has been asking more of him for twenty-five hundred years.

The modern difficulty is that the rest of our political vocabulary has been emptied of the corresponding demand. We speak of *users*, *consumers*, *stakeholders*, *constituents*, *followers*, *subscribers* — a whole lexicon in which the political subject is constructed as recipient rather than partner. The citizen survives in this company as a ceremonial relic, invoked on Independence Day and forgotten the rest of the year. And the invocation does not save him, because a word that is not practiced does not carry the concept it names. The Roman senators continued to convene after Augustus had drained the republic of its substance. They continued to call themselves senators. It did not help them, and it did not help the republic. The office outlived the thing.

***

Here the central tension of this book begins to show itself. The case we have just made — that citizenship is a participatory, public, communal thing, with its roots in Aristotle and Cicero rather than in Locke and Kant — is a case that leans hard in one direction. And the direction is only half the truth we want to tell. A tradition that reveres the polis and says nothing about the individual is a tradition that has forgotten what the modern moral revolution has taught us: that every human being, before he is a citizen, is a *person*, an end in himself, whose dignity does not wait upon his political contributions and cannot be revoked by his community. The Greek polis was, among other things, a society of slaveholders. The Roman *res publica* was a society in which the humanity of women, of non-citizens, of provincials, was systematically undercounted. The genius of the modern age — the genius of Locke and Kant and Mill and the long liberal tradition that has flowed from them — has been the insistence that the person cannot be reduced to his civic role. There is a Kantian dignity in every human being that we are not free to disregard in the name of a common good. There is a Millian liberty of conscience and of expression that no majority has the standing to extinguish. There is a Rawlsian conception of equal moral status that has become, for any decent modern polity, a floor beneath which it cannot sink.

To write as though we could simply revive the classical conception of citizenship without carrying those modern achievements forward would be to write nostalgia rather than argument. It would be, in fact, to misread the classical tradition itself — because the classical conception was always tangled up with the inequalities of its time, and what we admire in it is precisely what survives the stripping away of those inequalities. What we need is not a return to the polis of free adult male property-owners but an extension of what the polis *meant* to everyone. The ancient insight that the human being is completed through participation in a common life has to be married to the modern insight that the human being has a standing prior to any common life. Neither insight, alone, is adequate. Each, alone, produces the pathologies that the other tradition rightly fears.

We approach this marriage, the two of us who are writing, from different sides. One of us instinctively begins with the individual — with the reasoning person who must not be absorbed into any mass, whose conscience must not be colonized, whose freedom to think and to disagree is the condition of every genuine political act. The other instinctively begins with the polity — with the common life in which character is formed, without which the reasoning individual is a fiction and the disagreements are tribal noise. We have spent several years arguing with each other about which beginning is the right one. We have come to the conclusion, and we will argue it through this volume, that the apparent disagreement is the book: the two halves are not rivals but necessary complements, and the tradition we will end up defending — what Richard Dagger has named *republican liberalism*, and what we will extend — is the one that holds them together.

The name is unlovely but precise. *Republican* points to the older tradition of participation, civic virtue, and liberty as non-domination — the tradition in which the citizen is a partner in a common thing. *Liberal* points to the modern tradition of individual rights, freedom of conscience, and equal moral standing — the tradition in which the citizen is an individual whose dignity is not at the disposal of the community. The two words, held together, describe a human being who is at once a member and a self, a partner and an agent, a participant and a rights-bearer. A citizen, on this picture, is an individual *who belongs*. He belongs without ceasing to be an individual. He is an individual who does not confuse his freedom with his isolation.

Stating the thesis this bluntly is already to preview what the rest of Volume I will have to do. We have to show why neither of the component traditions, on its own, can carry the weight. We have to show why the other two serious answers — the communitarian and the post-liberal — are indispensable critics of the liberal and the republican strands, and why, for all their power, they cannot be the final word either. We have to show that the marriage is philosophically possible and not just rhetorically convenient. We have to show, finally, that this is the answer most closely matched to the American tradition itself — a tradition whose distinguishing feature, from the Federalist and Anti-Federalist debates forward, has been the refusal to choose cleanly between the individual and the common thing. That is the work of the six chapters that follow.

***

One more commitment is worth declaring, because it will determine everything that follows. We do not intend to begin from policy, and we do not intend to begin from party. We do not intend, in this volume, to recommend a reform of campaign finance or a change to the primary system or a new media regulation. Those arguments have their place — we will make some of them in Volume III — but they do not belong at the beginning of a book like this one, and the reason is not a matter of prudence but of method. A political program that is not grounded in a clear conception of the citizen will, over time, produce outcomes the program's authors did not intend. The history of the twentieth century is in part a history of political programs whose authors had no considered answer to the oldest question, and who were therefore unable to recognize the humanity they were grinding under when their programs met reality. A movement that knows what it wants to do but cannot say what a person is in public life will eventually find itself doing things to persons that it cannot justify to anyone, including itself.

So we begin, instead, with first principles. We begin by asking what a citizen *is*. We will not, in doing this, pretend to stand outside our own moral commitments; we are Americans and children of the Western tradition, writing for a readership that is largely the same, and the whole project is shot through with inheritances we could not disown if we wanted to. But we can at least try to make the commitments visible rather than smuggling them in under the cover of policy proposals. If we are right that the citizen has gone missing, then the first task is to describe the figure whose absence we are naming. Everything else — the diagnosis in Volume II, the program in Volume III — follows from that description.

The chapters that come next will take the four traditions in turn. Chapter 2 will enter the republican inheritance, from Aristotle's *Politics* through Cicero's *De Re Publica* and Machiavelli's *Discourses* to Arendt's space of appearance and Pettit's non-domination — the long argument that the citizen is completed by public life and that liberty consists in not being at the mercy of another's will. Chapter 3 will present the liberal revolution, from Locke and Kant through Mill and Berlin to Rawls and Fukuyama — the argument that the person precedes the polity, that rights are held against the state, and that the bracketing of substantive conceptions of the good is not an evasion but a moral achievement. Chapter 4 will present the communitarian reply, from Sandel's critique of Rawlsian neutrality through MacIntyre's *After Virtue* and Taylor's *Sources of the Self* — the argument that the unencumbered self of liberal theory is a philosophical fiction that, when believed, produces sociological catastrophe. Chapter 5 will take up the post-liberal turn, from Deneen through Vermeule and Ahmari and Hazony, and will engage its claim that the liberal order has succeeded in exactly the ways that matter most, and that its success is the problem. Chapter 6 will argue that the American founding debate, between the Federalists and Anti-Federalists, is the first great local version of the quarrel among all four, and that Tocqueville, Lincoln, and Dewey are the American voices in which its deepest resolutions have been glimpsed. And Chapter 7 will offer the synthesis we have named — *republican liberalism* — in the form we intend to carry into the rest of the book.

That is the shape of Volume I. It is meant to be the foundation on which the diagnosis of Volume II and the program of Volume III can stand, and it is meant to be more than preparation. A philosophical foundation that answers its question is already, in some sense, a victory. The question, to restate it one last time in the simplest form we can find, is this: *what does it mean to be a person in public with other people?* Not what does it mean to be entertained by them, or to trade with them, or to defeat them, or to blame them, but to be *with* them, in a common thing, under laws we are both bound by, toward ends we share because we have agreed to share them. The woman at the kitchen table knows, in her bones, that this is the question her evening has forced upon her. She has been told for most of her adult life that she does not need to answer it — that all she needs to do is consume, vote, pay, complain, and hope. She is beginning to suspect, in the silence after the closed laptop, that the telling was a lie. She is right. She is going to need a better answer, and so, all of us, are we.

Let us see whether one can be found.


---

# Chapter 2
## The Republican Inheritance

The word *citizen* has never been a neutral word. It sounds neutral to us — bureaucratic, almost — because we meet it mostly on passport applications and jury summonses, in the language of paperwork. But its lineage is not neutral at all. *Citizen* is a republican word. It was coined to name a particular kind of human being in a particular kind of political community, and the tradition that coined it had commitments most of its present users would be surprised to discover they have inherited. To say "I am a citizen" is, whether one knows it or not, to speak a line from a very old play. The play has many acts and many authors, and some of its scenes we rightly refuse to stage again. But modern politics in the countries that call themselves republics is written in its grammar, and a book about the disappearance of the citizen has to begin by saying what was there when the citizen was present.

This chapter is that saying. It is a walk through the civic-republican tradition — the oldest continuous strand in Western political thought and the one most directly evoked by the noun on the cover of this book. We take the tradition in order, from Aristotle to the present, and we try to let each of its major voices speak in its own register before we judge. At the end we extract the tradition's core commitments, we make the strongest case we know how to make for it, and we name the dangers we think it carries. Our claim, to show our hand at the start, is not that civic republicanism is the tradition we should now revive in pure form. No serious person should want Athens back. Our claim is that the tradition named something real about what it is to be a human being in public, and that without recovering what it named, the word *citizen* loses its weight and becomes a synonym for *resident with documents*. The disappearance of the citizen, which this whole book is about, begins with the forgetting of what the word was originally for.

***

The tradition has two branches, in productive argument with each other for about twenty-five centuries, worth keeping distinct at the start. The first we will call, following the conventional labels, *neo-Athenian* or *civic humanist*. It runs from Aristotle through the Florentine humanists to Hannah Arendt, and its central claim is that direct participation in public life is not merely instrumental but intrinsically valuable — that to act and speak among equals in the open space of a political community is a fundamental human good, maybe the highest of the properly human goods, and that a life which never rises to such action is missing something private life cannot supply. The second branch we will call *neo-Roman*, after the label that Quentin Skinner and Philip Pettit have made canonical. It runs from the Roman republican writers through Machiavelli and Harrington to Pettit himself, and its central claim is subtler and in some ways more portable: that freedom consists not in the absence of interference but in the absence of *domination* — in not living at the mercy of an arbitrary power that can, if it wishes, interfere with you, whether or not it chooses to do so on any given day. The benevolent slave-owner who lets his slave do as he pleases does not thereby make the slave free. The slave is unfree because he lives at the master's discretion, and discretion is what non-domination refuses to accept. Both branches insist that self-government is the political form in which freedom is possible; both insist that self-government requires a certain kind of citizen, formed in certain kinds of institutions. But the first branch is more heroic and the second more juridical, and modern republicans differ sharply on which emphasis to carry forward.

We will carry both, because both are true. What follows is the lineage.

***

Aristotle is the beginning, and everything that follows is in some sense a footnote to him, often a footnote disagreeing with the text above the line. The *Politics* opens with the claim for which Aristotle is most often quoted and most often misread — that man is by nature a political animal, *zoon politikon*. The phrase is not a sociological observation that humans like to hang around in groups. It is a metaphysical claim about what a human being is. The *polis*, for Aristotle, is the natural completion of human nature, in the sense that an acorn's completion is an oak. Outside the polis, he says in a famous passage, a man is either a beast or a god — something below the properly human or something above it, but not the thing itself. The human being comes into what it is by participating with other human beings in the shared project of governing a common life. That is the aperture through which the characteristic human capacities — reason, speech, judgment, the capacity to give an account of the good and argue about it — actually open. Alone, we cannot do what we are for.

This is a large claim, and a modern reader has to resist two opposite temptations. The first is to dismiss it as quaint because it assumes a political community that no longer exists, rested on slavery and on the exclusion of women, and operated at a scale we cannot reproduce. The second is to embrace it uncritically as nostalgia, as though one could reconstitute the Athenian assembly with a Zoom account and a civics pamphlet. Aristotle deserves neither treatment. What we should take from him is the structural insight: human flourishing is not a private matter; it has a public face; and the public face is not an optional decoration on the private good but an ingredient of it. If you cannot act in public with equals, something in you does not get to grow. That claim is separable from fifth-century Greek institutions. It survives them.

Aristotle's second contribution is his account of virtue. A virtue, for him, is not a feeling and not a moral sentiment but a *disposition* — a stable, habituated way of responding to the world, acquired over time through repeated practice in an environment that rewards the right responses and punishes the wrong ones. You become just by doing just things in a community that knows the difference. You become courageous by doing courageous things among people who recognize courage. The virtues are not downloaded; they are formed, and formation is a social process, carried out in institutions — the household, the school, the army, the assembly. Take away the institutions of formation and you do not get an unformed but innocent human being. You get a human being formed by whatever default environment remains, which is unlikely to produce the dispositions a self-governing community requires. This is a claim whose force the twentieth century made painfully concrete and the twenty-first has done nothing to refute. The virtues have to be cultivated, and cultivation has conditions, and the conditions can be destroyed.

There is a third Aristotelian contribution, mentioned less often but as important as the other two. *Politics*, he says, is the master science — not in the sense that it gives orders to the other sciences, but in the sense that it sets the ends within which the others operate. Medicine is for health; rhetoric is for persuasion; economics is for household management; but what is health for, and what is persuasion for, and what is a well-managed household for? Only a political community can answer. The question of the good human life is a political question, because it is the question of what a community is trying to sustain. The idea that politics is just one domain among many, or an unfortunate distraction from the real business of private flourishing, would have seemed to Aristotle a category error. We mention this because the book we are writing is, in a sense, a book about the damage done when this hierarchy is inverted.

***

Cicero is the bridge. If Aristotle is the ancestor of every subsequent argument about virtue and the polis, Cicero is the ancestor of every subsequent Western argument about law, office, and the public thing. His enormous achievement, extended across the *De Re Publica*, the *De Legibus*, and the *De Officiis*, was to translate the Greek inheritance into a Roman vocabulary of law and institutions — and, in so doing, to put the republican tradition into a form that could survive the transition from the city-state to the large, legally organized polity. His formula, in one of the most consequential sentences in the history of political thought, defines the *res publica* as a partnership — *res populi*, the thing of the people — bound together not by common blood or common place but by common agreement about right and by shared interest. A republic, for Cicero, is not a nation in the ethnic sense and not a multitude in the statistical sense. It is a *populus* — a people constituted by its agreement to live under law and to pursue its good in common.

Several things follow from this definition that the tradition would never let go of. First, the republic is an artifact of *justice*. A regime that does not rest on a shared commitment to right is not merely a bad republic but not a republic at all; Cicero is willing to follow the logic to the point of denying, against the tyrant, that the tyrant's state is a state in the proper sense. Second, the republic is a *partnership* — *societas*, a term that carries legal and almost commercial weight. To be a citizen is to be a partner in a joint undertaking whose burdens are shared and whose benefits are enjoyed as of right rather than doled out as charity. Third, the republic is held together by *institutions* — law, office, custom, religion — more than by personal virtue alone. Cicero was a practicing politician, and he knew that no community could be sustained on private virtue unsupported by institutional scaffolding. That is why the Roman contribution to the republican tradition, over and above the Greek, is the contribution of *constitutionalism* in the deep sense: the idea that the public thing is held in place by a structure of offices and laws, checked against one another, rotating in the hands of citizens who serve for a time and then return to private life.

The American founders read Cicero as closely as they read any pagan writer. They absorbed the vocabulary and they absorbed the architecture. If Aristotle gives us the anthropology of the citizen — what a citizen is — Cicero gives us the institutional grammar: what a republic looks like from the inside, what offices it fills, what it demands of those who hold them, how it names the public thing it is trying to preserve.

***

Machiavelli is the figure most distorted by his reputation, and the recovery of the republican Machiavelli — the author of the *Discourses on Livy* rather than the author of *The Prince* — is one of the genuine scholarly achievements of the last half-century. What the *Discourses* give us is not the cynical advisor to tyrants of popular caricature but a passionate partisan of free cities, a close reader of the Roman republic, and the thinker who adds to the Ciceronian inheritance a set of claims that the tradition had not yet fully articulated and that would prove enormously consequential.

Three claims stand out. The first is that a free city requires armed citizens. Machiavelli was obsessed with this, partly because of his experience watching Italian city-states hire mercenaries and pay the price, and partly because of what he took from his reading of Rome. A people that will not defend itself cannot remain free, because it places its freedom in the hands of someone else, and that someone else will sooner or later discover that he has the power to take the freedom away. There is a modern reading of this point — the gun-rights reading — that is neither wrong nor sufficient. Machiavelli's deeper claim is about civic muscle in a more general sense: a republic of citizens who have delegated to specialists every demanding public function, from fighting to deliberating to governing, has already lost the thing it was trying to preserve. Freedom is not a commodity that one can buy from professionals.

The second claim is that republics survive by *institutions that channel conflict*, not by institutions that suppress it. This is Machiavelli at his most modern. The Roman republic, he insists, grew strong not in spite of its social conflicts — patrician against plebeian, Senate against tribunate — but because of them, because those conflicts were contained within legal and institutional forms that turned them into productive contests rather than civil wars. A polity that tries to eliminate faction eliminates the energy that makes self-government possible. A polity that lets faction run without institutional channels destroys itself. The republican art is the art of designing the channels — of building the assemblies and courts and offices in which disagreement becomes deliberation rather than violence. Madison will echo this exactly in *Federalist* 10 and 51, and anyone who has read the *Discourses* will recognize the family resemblance.

The third claim, the one that troubles most modern readers, is that republics require *public virtue* — *virtù*, in Machiavelli's untranslatable term — and that when a citizenry ceases to have it, the game is over. Virtue here is not the moralistic feeling the word now conjures. It is closer to civic muscle: the disposition to prefer the common good to private advantage when the two conflict, to stand up for one's institutions, to do one's share in the joint work of the city. The classical concept of *corruption*, which the tradition carries forward into the eighteenth century and beyond, refers not primarily to graft — though it includes graft — but to the loss of this disposition. A corrupt citizenry is not one whose politicians take bribes. It is one that has stopped being willing to do its part. Once that has happened, no constitutional mechanism can save the city, because constitutional mechanisms are operated by citizens, and corrupt citizens will operate even good mechanisms badly.

One does not have to sign every Machiavellian claim to see that this vocabulary — armed citizens, institutional channels for conflict, the disease of corruption as the loss of public-spiritedness — has been the operating vocabulary of every subsequent republican argument up to our own moment.

***

James Harrington, writing in 1656 in the wake of the English civil wars, matters less for the particular institutions he proposed than for the structural argument he made. His *Commonwealth of Oceana* is the first modern work to argue, with full philosophical seriousness, that the distribution of *property* is the foundation of the distribution of *power*, and that no constitution can be stable that is not fitted to the property structure of the society under it. A republic of free citizens, Harrington argued, requires a broad distribution of property — above all, of land — because citizens who do not control the means of their own subsistence are structurally dependent on those who do, and dependent men cannot be free in the republican sense. Give one man enough land to buy the bread of all the others, and you have already given him the power to command them, regardless of what the written constitution says.

This is a proto-modern argument, and its influence on the American founders was enormous. Thomas Jefferson's obsession with the yeoman farmer and the homestead, John Adams's writings on the necessity of a middling sort of landowner, the whole Jeffersonian suspicion of great concentrations of wealth — these are not agricultural nostalgia. They are Harrington, domesticated for a new continent. The argument also gives the tradition a handle for thinking about economic unfreedom in a way that liberal rights-talk, with its focus on formal non-interference, often struggles to grip. If the structural distribution of property determines whether citizens are dependent or independent, then the question of who owns what is a republican question of the first rank, not a separable economic question that politics addresses only at the margins. We will come back to this in the context of Pettit, and again in the context of Sandel and Ahmari. The point for now is that the republican tradition, from Harrington forward, has never had the luxury of treating economic organization as external to the question of political freedom.

***

Rousseau is the figure we have to handle honestly, because he is the figure the tradition's critics most often hold up as the proof that the whole enterprise slides into tyranny. The charge is that Rousseau's concept of the *general will* — the will of the people as a moral body, distinct from the aggregated wills of the individuals who compose it — opens the door to the claim that people must be "forced to be free," that a political authority speaking in the name of the general will can override any particular will that disagrees with it, and that this is the road to the terror. Eric Hoffer and a long tradition of cold-war liberals read Rousseau this way. The reading is not baseless.

But Rousseau deserves better than the caricature. What he was trying to articulate, in the *Social Contract* and more fully in the later political writings, is a problem that no republican thinker can evade: how can a people be genuinely self-governing without either collapsing into a sum of private interests (in which case the "public" is a fiction) or empowering some part of itself to speak for the whole (in which case the self-government is fraudulent)? The general will is his answer, and whatever one thinks of the answer, the question is real. A republic whose politics is only the arithmetic of private preferences is not a republic at all; a republic in which some faction claims to speak for the public against the public is a tyranny in republican drag. Rousseau is trying to locate, between these two failures, the conceptual space in which a people can genuinely will something in common that is not reducible to what its individual members happened to want when the survey was taken.

We think this problem is real, we think Rousseau's solution contains dangers the tradition must guard against, and we think the best response is to keep the problem alive while rejecting the formulation that "the people" can be represented so completely by any institution that dissent from that institution becomes dissent from the people itself. The liberal revolution, which we turn to next chapter, will insist on protecting the dissenter against exactly that kind of claim, and it will be right to do so. Rousseau at his best — in the concern with civic religion, in the insistence that citizens must be formed to their freedom, in the refusal to settle for a politics that is only the management of private appetites — belongs in the republican line. Rousseau at his worst belongs in the warning signs the line has to read.

***

Tocqueville is the figure through whom the tradition meets America directly, and if there is one text in the republican lineage that every reader of this book should spend a month with, it is the *Democracy in America* — the two volumes, slowly, in a good translation, with the attention one would give to a long poem. What Tocqueville saw in the young United States in 1831 was a republican society operating at a scale that no classical republican had thought possible, and operating by means that no classical republican had theorized. He saw it with astonishment and with a certain measured alarm, and his book is still the fullest account we have of what a working civic culture looks like from the inside.

The claim most often quoted from Tocqueville — that the Americans of his time were constantly forming *associations* for every imaginable public purpose, from building roads to founding schools to promoting temperance — is the claim most central to his republican argument. Voluntary associations, he wrote, are the "schools of self-government." They are the institutions in which the habits of democratic citizenship are actually formed, because they are the institutions in which ordinary people acquire the skills of meeting, proposing, disagreeing, deciding, and acting together. A democracy that consists only of a relationship between isolated individuals and a centralized state has, Tocqueville thought, no mechanism for producing the citizens it requires. The associations are the mechanism. Without them, even good constitutional forms become empty. With them, even imperfect forms can become schools in which a people practices the arts a free people has to have.

Tocqueville also gives the tradition two warnings whose relevance to our moment is almost painful. The first is the *tyranny of the majority*, which he distinguishes carefully from the tyranny of a despot. A majority that has made up its mind, and that controls not only the ballot box but the schools and the newspapers and the pulpits and the manners of the country, can crush dissent more thoroughly than any king, because there is nowhere outside its reach to stand. The second warning is *soft despotism* — his name for the future he thought democracy was drifting toward. A soft despotism does not oppress its subjects; it infantilizes them, taking over one by one the functions of self-government in exchange for comfort and security, until the people it rules is a flock of well-tended animals that has forgotten what it would mean to govern itself. Tocqueville thought the remedies for both dangers were the same: robust associational life, local self-government, a healthy civil religion, and the cultivation in the citizenry of the kind of pride that refuses to be administered.

We quote Tocqueville so often in this book because we believe he was right about what he saw and because we believe the dynamic he feared is the one we now inhabit.

***

Hannah Arendt carries the tradition across the twentieth century's darkest passage, and she does it with a force and originality that makes every subsequent civic republican either explicitly her student or unconsciously her debtor. Two of her books matter most for this chapter. *The Human Condition*, published in 1958, is the philosophical anthropology of politics that the tradition had not previously received at this level of rigor. *On Revolution*, published in 1963, is her reading of the American founding as the closest a modern polity has come to recovering the republican possibility.

Arendt's distinctive contribution is her account of *action* — a term she uses with great precision. Action, for Arendt, is what human beings do when they speak and deed in the open space of a political community of equals, initiating something that was not there before, taking responsibility in public for what they do, and being seen by others who are free to agree or disagree. Action is categorically different from *labor*, the activity that sustains biological life, and from *work*, the activity that makes durable things. Labor and work have their own dignity, but they are not the specifically political activities, and a human life that contains only labor and work — even a very prosperous life so composed — is a life that has not yet entered the distinctively public dimension. To act is to appear, to take up a place in what Arendt calls the *space of appearance*, the common world in which human beings are visible to one another as agents rather than merely as bodies or as functionaries.

This sounds abstract until one notices that Arendt's analysis of totalitarianism, which was the intellectual project of her life, rests on it directly. What totalitarian movements destroy, she argued, is exactly the public space in which action is possible. They destroy it by destroying the plurality — the simultaneous presence of many different persons with their many different perspectives — that the public space requires. They replace plurality with ideology, which gives every citizen the same "correct" perspective, and they replace action with conformity, which performs agreement rather than initiating anything. The lonely, atomized individuals she describes in *The Origins of Totalitarianism* — the ones susceptible to the movement because they have no world of their own — are lonely in a quite specific sense: they have lost access to the space in which action is possible, because the associations and institutions that maintained it have been destroyed. The movement offers them a fraudulent public in exchange for the real one they have lost, and they take the offer because they have no other.

This is the deepest version of the claim that structures our whole book. The atomized individual and the mass movement are one phenomenon. The mass movement is what happens when the public space has been hollowed out and a counterfeit offered in its place. To restore the citizen is therefore to restore the public space — the genuine one, in which plurality is real and action is possible — and this is not a job for individual heroes but for institutions and associations, for the slow work of rebuilding the molds within which public life occurs.

***

Philip Pettit, writing at the end of the twentieth century, gives the tradition its sharpest modern formulation, and his central concept — *liberty as non-domination* — is the one we are going to carry forward most explicitly into the rest of this book. Pettit's 1997 *Republicanism* and his 2012 *On the People's Terms* are the canonical sources, and their argument deserves to be laid out carefully, because it corrects a mistake in the standard liberal account of freedom that has real consequences for how we think about the unfreedoms of our own moment.

Isaiah Berlin, in his famous 1958 essay "Two Concepts of Liberty," divided the family of freedoms into two: *negative* liberty, the absence of interference by others, and *positive* liberty, the presence of the conditions that enable one to govern oneself. The distinction has dominated Anglophone political theory for two generations, and Berlin used it, among other things, to warn against the positive-liberty tradition's tendency to slide into the tyranny that claims to be forcing people to be free. Pettit's move is to insist that there is a third concept Berlin missed, and that it is the one the republican tradition had always been about. Freedom, on the republican account, is neither merely the absence of interference nor the possession of the capacities for self-rule. It is the absence of *domination* — the condition of not living at the mercy of another agent's arbitrary power.

The difference matters, and Pettit illustrates it with an image the tradition had used for centuries. Consider a slave with a benevolent master. The master chooses never to interfere with what the slave does. He is kind, inattentive, or on holiday. By the negative-liberty standard, the slave is free: there is no interference. By the republican standard, the slave is emphatically unfree, and obviously so, because his non-interference is a gift the master can withdraw at any time. He lives in the shadow of another person's will. He must shape himself, cultivate deference, avoid offense, read the master's moods — all the behaviors of the unfree — precisely because the master's forbearance is contingent and could end. A freedom that depends on the good graces of a power one cannot check is not freedom but permission.

This concept changes what one can see when one looks at the modern world. A debtor who owes more than he can pay is not free in the republican sense, even if his creditor chooses to be lenient, because his creditor can at any moment choose otherwise. A worker whose employer can fire him at will for any reason or none, in a labor market where the next employer has the same power, is not free in the republican sense, even if his current boss is decent. A user of a digital platform whose terms can be changed unilaterally, whose account can be suspended without appeal, and whose livelihood depends on the platform's continuing willingness to host him is not free in the republican sense. Each of these figures lives at the discretion of a power he cannot check, and the tradition classifies each as dominated. Non-domination names a real unfreedom that the negative-liberty tradition often struggles to see.

The policy implication is not that these forms of economic life should be abolished but that the republican standard adds a dimension to our assessment of them. A free society is one in which citizens stand, as much as politics can manage, on ground that is not owed them at someone else's pleasure — one in which power, private as well as public, is checked by institutions that make arbitrary interference impossible or costly. This is not a new demand. It is the oldest republican demand, now articulated for a world in which domination comes as often from an algorithm or a balance sheet as from a prince.

***
