Michael Sandel's *Democracy's Discontent*, published in 1996 and expanded in 2022, is the book that put the civic-republican diagnosis in the hands of a wide American audience, and it is the contemporary book closest in spirit to the one we are writing. Sandel's central historical claim is that the American political tradition, through most of its history, was not merely liberal in the modern rights-procedural sense but civic-republican in a substantive sense — concerned with the formation of citizens, with the dispersal of economic power, with the cultivation of self-governing habits in ordinary life — and that American public philosophy, over the course of the twentieth century, quietly abandoned the civic-republican side of its inheritance in favor of what he calls the *procedural republic*. The procedural republic understands its task as the neutral umpire of citizens' freely chosen ends; it brackets questions about the good life, about virtue, about what kind of people a polity needs; it confines itself to rights and procedures, and it hopes that everything else will take care of itself.

Sandel's argument is that everything else did not take care of itself. A polity that brackets every substantive question about the good discovers that its citizens are left without a shared language for the things that matter most to them, and that into the vacuum rush ideologies — market fundamentalism, identity tribalism, technocratic expertise — that are not in any serious sense neutral, but that present themselves as such because the political language of the good has been abandoned. Sandel's later book *The Tyranny of Merit* extends the argument into the meritocratic regime that has come to dominate elite politics, and it makes the case that this regime's inability to speak honestly about dignity, contribution, and the common good is a direct cause of the populism now rising against it. We think Sandel is substantially right, and Volume II of this book will develop several strands of his analysis at length. For now, what we want to register is that his diagnosis is a civic-republican diagnosis — it rests on the tradition we are sketching in this chapter — and that the recovery he recommends is a recovery of exactly the inheritance we are trying to make visible.

Alongside Sandel, two contemporary theorists have done the synthesizing work that makes the tradition usable in present-day terms. Iseult Honohan's *Civic Republicanism*, published in 2002, is the clearest one-volume statement of republicanism as a live political philosophy, distinguished from both liberalism and communitarianism. Richard Dagger's *Civic Virtues*, published in 1997, gave us the phrase *republican liberalism* which we have borrowed, with some modification, to describe our own position. Dagger's project was to show that the best version of the civic-republican tradition and the best version of the liberal tradition can be reconciled — that one does not have to choose between rights against the state and participation in the state, that the autonomous individual and the engaged citizen can be, and must be, the same person. That is our thesis too, and we acknowledge the debt openly.

***

If one reads this lineage carefully, a cluster of commitments emerges that constitutes the tradition's core, distinct from any particular thinker's particular emphasis. Six of them are worth naming.

The first is *liberty as non-domination*, in the Pettit formulation: freedom is the condition of not living at the mercy of an arbitrary power, and a free society is one whose institutions secure this condition for all its citizens. This is the tradition's master concept of freedom, distinct from the negative-liberty concept of the absence of interference and from the positive-liberty concept of self-realization, and it is the concept that gives republicanism its analytical edge on questions of economic and social power that other traditions often struggle to grip.

The second is the *rule of law*, in the full Ciceronian sense: that a republic is constituted by its laws rather than by the will of any person or faction, that the laws bind officials as well as subjects, and that the path to every legitimate political end runs through lawful institutions rather than around them. The rule of law is the institutional form of non-domination; a society in which law itself is the discretionary instrument of whoever holds power is a society in which no one is secure against arbitrary interference.

The third is *anti-corruption*, in the classical sense. Corruption here is not graft, though it includes graft. It is the loss of public-spiritedness — the disposition in a citizenry to prefer private advantage to the common good, to let the public thing deteriorate because tending to it is someone else's problem, to treat political office as a private asset rather than a shared trust. A corrupt citizenry cannot be saved by good institutions, because institutions are operated by citizens. This is the tradition's explanation for why it cares so much about virtue: without it, the rest collapses.

The fourth is *mixed government* — the idea, running from Polybius through the American founders, that stable free government requires the institutional balancing of different principles, interests, and orders against one another, so that no single faction can capture the whole. The principle is older than any particular constitutional form, and it underwrites everything from the Roman Senate's relation to the tribunes to the American separation of powers.

The fifth is *civic virtue*, understood as a disposition rather than a moralistic feeling. The virtues the tradition prizes — courage, justice, prudence, public-spiritedness, the willingness to serve and the willingness to listen — are habits formed in institutions and practiced in action. They are not private moral achievements. They are the operating equipment of the citizen, and they are cultivated in the associations, offices, and daily practices of a working republic.

The sixth is *popular sovereignty*, understood not as the raw rule of a numerical majority but as the commitment that the political community is the people's own, constituted by its members, answerable to them, and legitimate only insofar as it tracks their genuine common interest. The tradition has always struggled with how to institutionalize this commitment without sliding either into the tyranny of the majority or into the fiction that some elite speaks for the people against the people. The struggle is perpetual and the commitment does not lapse with it.

These six commitments, taken together, are what it means to speak the word *citizen* in the republican key.

***

It is time to make, before we criticize the tradition, the strongest case we know how to make for it. Here is that case.

The liberal account of freedom, on its own, cannot explain why the benevolent master's slave is unfree. The liberal, holding the negative-liberty concept, has to say either that the slave *is* free, because there is no interference, which is absurd on its face, or that the slave is free in the negative sense but lacks something else we also care about, which is a retreat rather than an answer. The republican does not have to dodge. The slave is unfree because he lives at another's discretion, and discretion is itself a form of unfreedom. This is not a marginal case. It is the structural form of unfreedom, and it recurs wherever one agent's well-being depends on another agent's arbitrary choice. The republican diagnosis, once seen, is hard to unsee, and it illuminates a vast terrain of modern life — the precarious employee, the indebted household, the tenant in an unregulated market, the small-town resident dependent on a single employer, the citizen of a company town, the platform creator whose audience is borrowed — that the liberal framework tends to represent as a collection of private choices freely made. Sometimes they are that. Sometimes they are dominations dressed as contracts.

The tradition also names something the other traditions often miss: that freedom has a public face. A human being who is formally unmolested but cannot appear in public as an agent, cannot take part in the deliberations that shape the world she lives in, cannot see herself reflected in the institutions that govern her, is not simply a private person enjoying her private liberties. She is a diminished version of a human being, and her diminishment is political. The tradition does not accept the modern bargain in which public life is outsourced to specialists and the citizen is reduced to a consumer of political services, because it does not accept that such a reduction leaves the citizen intact. Something essential is lost in the outsourcing, and the tradition insists on saying so.

Finally, the tradition is right that the individual and the community are not the opposed terms of a zero-sum contest. The citizen, as the republican tradition understood the word, is an individual *and* a member, and the membership is constitutive of the individuality rather than in competition with it. The person who belongs to a functioning public world is a larger person than the person who does not. This is the core republican claim, and we think it is true. It is the claim that makes the word *citizen* mean something that neither the word *individual* nor the word *subject* can mean on its own.

***

Having made the case, we owe the reader the dangers. A full critique of civic republicanism belongs to the chapters on the liberal revolution and on the post-liberal turn, where the tradition meets its most serious modern challenges. But three hazards are worth flagging here, because they are internal to the tradition and a reader ought to see them now.

The first is the slide into *authoritarian communitarianism*. A tradition that insists on the primacy of the public good, on the formation of citizens by institutions, and on the dispositional requirements of a self-governing people is always one short step from the claim that the polity should use its power to form its citizens in the right way, by force if necessary. Some republican voices have taken that step, and the revolutionary career of the Rousseauian tradition is the cautionary tale. The danger is real, and the liberal insistence on rights against the polity — which we take up in the next chapter — is the necessary corrective. A republicanism that forgets this corrective is not a republicanism worth reviving.

The second is the *exclusion of the non-citizen*. Historical republicanism has almost always been the republicanism of *some* among the inhabitants of a territory, and the "some" has usually been defined by criteria we now rightly reject — sex, property, religion, race. The Athenian citizen was a male property-holder in a society sustained by slaves. The Roman citizen was a category defined against a much larger category of non-citizens and foreigners within the empire. Even the American republican tradition, which expanded the category more broadly than its predecessors, did so slowly and against fierce resistance, and the full extension of citizenship to all adult inhabitants is barely a human lifetime old. The tradition's critics are correct that the old citizenship was built on exclusions, and a revival of the tradition has to be a revival *without* those exclusions — which means the revival is not, strictly, a revival but a completion.

The third is *nostalgia*, the deepest hazard of a tradition this old. A writer who loves the polis can easily find himself in the posture of mourning a world that was built on inequalities he does not want back and operated at scales that cannot be reproduced. Nostalgia is a comfortable posture, because it relieves the nostalgic of the harder task of translating the tradition's insights into a form usable in present conditions. We have tried, in this chapter, to resist the nostalgia while preserving the insights. Whether we have succeeded the reader will judge; our conviction is that the insights are portable, and that a republican tradition with its exclusions stripped, its scales rethought, and its vocabulary retranslated is still the tradition most capable of naming what has been lost.

***

The last task of this chapter is to point ahead. The civic-republican inheritance, taken alone, is not sufficient for the modern citizen, and we do not want to be read as saying it is. The tradition we have just walked through has a blind spot, and the blind spot is serious.

The blind spot is that classical republicanism never developed a worked-out theory of *individual rights against the political community*. It could describe, with great subtlety, the conditions under which a citizen participates freely in the common life of the polis. It could specify the virtues a citizen must have and the institutions that form them. It could articulate non-domination as the structure of free life among equals. What it could not quite do — not in Aristotle, not in Cicero, not even, for different reasons, in Rousseau or in the earlier American republicans — was say clearly what the polity *may not do to its members*, no matter how general the will that commanded it, no matter how virtuous the citizenry that endorsed it, no matter how pressing the common good that seemed to require it. The tradition treated the well-formed citizen as someone whose good would largely align with the good of the polity, and it did not build, into its vocabulary, a strong fence around the individual that the polity could not cross.

The liberal revolution, which we take up in the next chapter, is the story of that fence being built. Its great names — Locke, Kant, Mill, Berlin, Rawls — are the names of a philosophical tradition whose central achievement was the articulation of rights that are held *against* the state, of zones of conscience and choice that no political community may legitimately invade, of a conception of the person as an end in himself who cannot be sacrificed to the good of the whole, no matter how good the whole. This is moral progress, and we are going to say so in just those terms. A recovered civic republicanism without the liberal fence is a republicanism that can, in principle, crush its dissidents in the name of the people; history is full of examples. We do not want that recovery.

But the liberal revolution came with its own blind spot, which is the opposite of the republican one. In putting the fence around the individual, it too often forgot that the individual, fenced in from the polity, is not yet a citizen — that a world in which rights are secure but no one has any particular reason to participate in the public life of the community is a world in which the citizen, in the republican sense, has already begun to disappear. The liberal tradition, at its best, knew this; at its worst, it forgot it, and its forgetting is one of the things that has produced our present trouble. The work of the rest of Volume I is to hold the two traditions together — to honor what each saw, to name what each missed, and to argue that a responsible politics in our century requires both.

For now, we have done what this chapter set out to do. We have walked through the republican inheritance, from Aristotle's polis to Pettit's non-domination, and we have tried to show why the word *citizen* carries a weight that no mere modern usage can dissolve. The word comes with a history. The history comes with commitments. A person who wants to claim the word honestly — and the argument of this book is that we must — has to know the weight she is picking up. Now we turn to the liberal revolution, which both challenged the inheritance and completed it, and which a serious contemporary civic republicanism cannot afford to refuse.


---

# Chapter 3
## The Liberal Revolution

The tradition we walked through in the preceding chapter is the oldest continuous strand of political thinking the West has produced. It begins with a polis that precedes the person, an Athens and a Rome in which the citizen is a role completed by participation rather than a right exercised against intrusion, and it carries that assumption, through every mutation of language and circumstance, from Aristotle to Arendt. The tradition we turn to now is, by comparison, startlingly new. It is no older than the seventeenth century. The oldest of its canonical texts was published within living memory of Shakespeare. And yet this tradition — the liberal revolution, the revolution of rights — has done something the republican tradition did not do and perhaps could not have done. It has reversed the ancient presumption. It has insisted that the person precedes the polity, that political authority is answerable to each individual it binds, and that the purpose of a government is not to complete us but to leave us the space in which to complete ourselves.

That reversal is the moral center of modern political life. It is what stands behind the phrases that even people who have never read a page of Locke or Kant or Mill can recite from memory: that governments are instituted among men, that they derive their just powers from the consent of the governed, that every person is endowed with inalienable rights, that no one may be deprived of life, liberty, or property without due process of law. These phrases are so deeply lodged in the American vocabulary that it is easy to miss how strange and specific they are. No ancient Greek wrote them. No Roman would have signed them. No medieval chancery would have drafted them. They are the signature of a particular philosophical revolution whose premises we have so thoroughly internalized that we mistake them for common sense, and it is worth the effort of de-familiarization to see what they actually claim.

The central liberal move is stated in a sentence. The individual is prior to the political community. Rights are not granted by the state; they are held against it. The purpose of political order is to secure those rights so that persons may pursue their own conception of the good. Every other commitment of the tradition — consent, constitutionalism, toleration, the rule of law, due process, the division of powers, the protection of conscience, the protection of property, the protection of speech — follows from that starting point. One begins with the person, not with the regime. One asks what the regime must do, and what it must not do, to be compatible with the dignity of the person. Political authority requires justification to each individual it binds, and the individual who cannot be given such a justification is being wronged, not governed.

This is what we mean, in the pages that follow, by the liberal revolution. It is the tradition that asks, for the first time in the history of political thought, the question that now seems to us so obvious as to hardly need asking: what right does any political power have to bind any particular person? It is also the tradition — and this is the deeper point — that supplies an answer under which most of the readers of this book have lived every day of their lives without having to think about it. The liberal revolution is not an ideology we chose. It is the atmosphere we breathe.

***

Its first serious breath was Locke's.

John Locke, writing the *Second Treatise of Government* in the shadow of the English Civil War and publishing it in 1689 as the Glorious Revolution was being justified after the fact, did what no political theorist before him had quite done. He began his argument not with the polity, and not with God's ordination of kings, but with an imagined condition in which there was no political order at all — the state of nature — and asked what rights a human being would possess in such a condition. His answer was the sentence the American revolutionaries would paraphrase a century later: every person has a natural right to life, liberty, and property. These rights are not conferred by governments, because they exist prior to the existence of any government. They are, in Locke's idiom, the endowment of a creator, but the political implication is one an atheist could sign: the individual is the bearer of rights before any king is the bearer of a crown.

Government, on Locke's view, is therefore an instrument. It exists to secure the rights its subjects already possess, because the inconveniences of the state of nature — the partiality of private judgment, the inadequacy of private force — make a common public power necessary for the protection of rights that existed before the common power did. But this instrumental framing has a radical consequence. If government exists to secure rights, then a government that fails to secure them, or, worse, one that violates them, has forfeited its warrant. It becomes what Locke calls a tyranny, and a tyranny may be resisted. The right of revolution — the right of a people to throw off a government that has turned against the purposes for which it was instituted — is written directly into the Lockean argument. It is the sentence Jefferson lifted nearly word-for-word. It is the sentence that made every subsequent revolt against arbitrary power in the Atlantic world legible as a Lockean claim.

The Locke who matters to us is not the Locke of the seventeenth-century disputes about consent and tacit consent, property and the mixing of labor, the Hobbes-Locke-Rousseau triangle that undergraduates are asked to draw on examinations. The Locke who matters is the one who performed the conceptual inversion: the one for whom political authority stands accused until it has given an account of itself, and for whom the accuser is the ordinary person whose life and liberty and property are at stake. That inversion is what the American framers inherited. It is what the Declaration performs in paragraph after paragraph — an indictment of a specific king measured against a standard older than he is. And it is what every bill of rights, every constitution, every human rights declaration the liberal West has produced since 1689 has quietly repeated. The individual has rights. The state must respect them. The state that does not respect them has failed at the only job for which a state exists.

***

Adam Smith is the figure the tradition has, almost without noticing, mutilated. He is remembered as the prophet of the invisible hand, the apostle of self-interest, the economist who proved that greed is good. He wrote no such thing. The flattening of Smith is what happens when a thinker becomes more useful as a slogan than as an author, and we mean, in this chapter, to resist the flattening, because the real Smith is one of the indispensable figures in the liberal tradition and one whose recovery is essential to any serious account of it.

Smith wrote two books. The one everyone has heard of is *An Inquiry into the Nature and Causes of the Wealth of Nations*, published in 1776, the year of the American Declaration. The one hardly anyone outside the academy has read is *The Theory of Moral Sentiments*, published seventeen years earlier, which Smith revised and expanded throughout his life and which he considered his chief work. The two books are halves of a single argument, and the argument is nothing like the caricature. Commercial society, Smith said, is possible only because human beings are moral animals before they are economic ones. The market functions because the people in it possess certain dispositions — sympathy, prudence, justice — that are formed in the ordinary moral life of families, neighborhoods, and trades and that cannot be generated by the market itself. A society of pure self-interest would not produce prosperity. It would produce the kind of systematic cheating and violence that makes prosperity impossible. The butcher and the baker whose self-interest Smith famously invokes are not amoral optimizers. They are tradesmen embedded in a dense local fabric of trust, reputation, and conscience, without which their self-interest would have nothing to work on.

The Smith who matters to this book is the Smith for whom freedom and decency are linked at their roots. Commercial society is to be defended, in his argument, because it enlarges the sphere within which ordinary people can pursue ordinary lives without being at the mercy of arbitrary lords, but it is to be defended only so long as the moral culture that makes it possible is maintained. When Smith warned, in both books, that the division of labor could degrade the workers who staffed it — reducing them to routines so narrow that the characteristic virtues of a free citizen could no longer develop — he was stating, in the eighteenth century, the problem the twentieth century would rediscover in the language of alienation. The flattening that turned Smith into a cheerleader for laissez-faire was performed against the grain of his own text. The real Smith is a moralist who thought commerce was compatible with human dignity under specific conditions, not a mystic who thought commerce generated dignity as a byproduct.

We will have reason, in later chapters, to return to the consequences of the flattening. The post-liberal critics of liberalism, when they indict the commercial reduction of freedom, are often indicting a Smith that Smith would not have recognized. It is important, at this stage, to name the thinker who is actually there in the tradition. He is one of the most morally serious figures liberalism ever produced, and treating him as a caricature disarms one of the tradition's best defenders.

***

If Locke supplied the political move and Smith supplied the economic one, Kant supplied the philosophical depth the tradition required to survive its own critics. Immanuel Kant, writing in Königsberg in the last third of the eighteenth century, asked what it would mean to treat every person as the bearer of a dignity that could not be traded away, and he gave an answer so austere and so demanding that every subsequent argument about human rights has lived in its shadow.

The formulation that matters for our purposes is what Kant called the second formulation of the categorical imperative: "So act that you treat humanity, whether in your own person or in the person of any other, always at the same time as an end and never merely as a means." The sentence is easy to misread as a prohibition on using other people, which is not what Kant meant. We use other people constantly, in every transaction and every cooperation, and the sentence does not forbid this. What it forbids is treating them *merely* as means — treating a person as though they were a thing, as though their status as an end in themselves, as a being with their own purposes, their own reason, their own capacity to legislate the moral law, could be set aside when it was convenient to set it aside. The sentence is the philosophical form of the ancient prohibition on slavery, torture, and conscription-into-the-purposes-of-another, and it is the ground on which Kant builds his account of human dignity.

That account is what distinguishes the liberal tradition from every utilitarian, perfectionist, or authoritarian rival. A utilitarian could, in principle, justify the destruction of an innocent person if the aggregate good were sufficiently great. A perfectionist could justify coercion on the ground that a person was being made better by it against her will. An authoritarian could justify the subordination of the person to the regime on the ground that the regime represented a higher reality to which she was obligated. Kant closes all three doors. The person is an end in herself. No calculation of aggregate welfare, no judgment about her good, no appeal to a collective above her, can justify treating her as a mere instrument. Her autonomy — her capacity to determine her own life in accordance with reasons she can recognize as her own — is the thing political order is there to protect, and anything that treats her as though she did not possess that capacity is a wrong that cannot be defeated by the benefits that might follow from committing it.

This is the philosophical bedrock of modern rights. It is why we say, and why we believe, that torture is impermissible even when it would produce useful information. It is why we say, and why we believe, that a person's religion, her speech, her conscience, her body, are not available to the state even when the state has reasons it considers sufficient. It is why the standard of political justification for the liberal tradition is not the aggregate welfare of the population but the acceptability of the arrangement to each person it binds. Kant wrote in the prose of the late Enlightenment and in the vocabulary of a particular German philosophical tradition, and his arguments are not easy. But the thing he saw — that the dignity of the person is the non-negotiable starting point of any legitimate political order — is one of the few philosophical claims that has survived every serious attempt to dislodge it, and it is the deepest source the liberal tradition has.

***

The figure on whom this book most directly leans is John Stuart Mill. There are reasons. Mill is, of the canonical liberals, the one who wrote most directly about the problem we are trying to write about — the problem of a free person in a society that is increasingly dominated by mass opinion and mass sentiment — and he saw it coming with a clarity that we have, over the intervening century and a half, mostly forgotten.

*On Liberty*, published in 1859, is a short book whose central claim is stated in its opening chapter and defended in those that follow. The claim is what later readers have called the harm principle. "The only purpose for which power can be rightfully exercised over any member of a civilized community, against his will, is to prevent harm to others. His own good, either physical or moral, is not a sufficient warrant." The sentence is easy to read quickly; it rewards slow reading. Mill is not saying that the individual's good does not matter. He is saying that the individual is the judge of his own good, and that the social and political apparatus has no standing to override his judgment in the name of his own improvement. The paternalism of the state, the paternalism of the majority, the paternalism of respectable opinion — each is an intrusion on the sphere within which the person has the authority to live his own life. The state may act to prevent harm. It may not act, in Mill's careful formulation, merely to produce what someone else takes to be the person's good.

The second argument of *On Liberty*, and for our purposes the more important one, is the argument for liberty of thought. Mill believed that truth is not a possession but a practice, and that the practice requires a specific social condition to function. It requires what he called, in a phrase that should be carved above every newsroom and every university, the "collision of adverse opinions." A belief that has never been tested against the strongest case for the opposite position is not, on Mill's view, a belief at all; it is a prejudice. The person who holds it cannot give a reason for it that is stronger than the authority of those around him who also hold it. The society that has suppressed dissent, or that has so shaped opinion that dissent is no longer imagined, has not achieved truth. It has achieved a uniformity that is indistinguishable, from the inside, from truth, and that will be indefensible the moment it is seriously challenged.

The harm principle protects the person from coercion. The collision of adverse opinions protects the culture from decay. Mill saw that they were two halves of the same argument, because a society that permits coercion in the name of the good will also find it convenient to suppress the opinions that question what the good is, and a society that suppresses those opinions will soon find that it has no principled resistance to coercion. Freedom of conduct and freedom of thought are not separate items on a list of civil liberties. They are the conditions under which a citizen remains capable of the reasoning that makes her a citizen at all. This is the point in Mill we most need now, and it is the point we will return to in Volume III, because a public sphere that has lost the capacity to host a collision of adverse opinions is a public sphere that has lost the capacity to produce citizens.

Mill is also the liberal most alert to the danger that freedom would be consumed by the very mass society it had helped to produce. He saw, in the commercial republics of the mid-nineteenth century, the beginnings of what Tocqueville had described in America — a society in which formal liberty coexisted with a crushing uniformity of opinion, a society in which no one was forbidden to think differently but no one, in fact, did. He called this the despotism of custom. It is what we would now call the algorithmic monoculture, or the partisan feed, or the social-media mob. The ancestry is the same. Mill understood that liberty is not merely a legal status; it is a practice that requires particular conditions to survive, and that those conditions can be eroded by social pressures as effectively as by state coercion. A book about the return of the citizen leans on Mill because Mill is the one who saw that the citizen could be destroyed by the very freedom in whose name the destruction would be carried out.

***

A century after Mill, Isaiah Berlin delivered the lecture that has organized every subsequent argument about freedom. "Two Concepts of Liberty," given as his inaugural address at Oxford in 1958, drew a distinction so lucid that its terms have passed into ordinary political speech. There are two ideas of liberty, Berlin said, and they are not the same. Negative liberty is freedom *from* — freedom from interference, freedom from coercion, freedom from arbitrary authority. It is the sphere within which a person may act without being stopped. Positive liberty is freedom *to* — freedom to realize one's true self, freedom to achieve one's higher purposes, freedom to become the person one was meant to be. Both ideas, Berlin allowed, have respectable histories. Both capture something real in our sense of what it is to be free.

But positive liberty, Berlin warned, contains within it a specific and terrible slide. If freedom is the achievement of one's true self, then someone else may come to believe that they know better than you what your true self is. If freedom is the realization of one's higher purposes, then someone else may claim to see those purposes more clearly than you do. And if they can make that claim stick, they can describe their coercion of you as the liberation of your true self from your false self. The prisoner is really the free man. The dissident is really in the grip of false consciousness. The resistor is being forced to be free. Every twentieth-century tyranny that marched under the banner of liberation performed exactly this move, and Berlin was writing in the shadow of a century in which both the Bolshevik and the fascist had claimed, with equal sincerity, that their coercion was their victim's deepest freedom.

Berlin did not say that positive liberty was always wrong, and he did not say that negative liberty was always sufficient. He said that the slide from positive liberty to the justification of coercion for the subject's own good was a real danger that real people had actually taken, and that a tradition that cared about the freedom of actual human beings needed to keep negative liberty front and center precisely because it was the form of liberty that could not be turned against the person in whose name it was being invoked. A negative liberty has an obvious test. The person who has it is not being interfered with. A positive liberty has no such test, because its meaning depends on a theory of the true self that can be supplied by any authority willing to insist on its theory strongly enough. This is why the liberal tradition, when pressed, has returned and returns to negative liberty as the core. Not because it thinks the positive conception is wrong, but because it knows what the positive conception can be made to justify when the wrong people get hold of it.

***

The most ambitious restatement of the liberal tradition in the twentieth century was an attempt, published in 1971, to put liberal political philosophy on a rigorous footing after the catastrophes that had consumed the first half of the century. John Rawls's *A Theory of Justice* is one of the few academic books of the last hundred years that genuinely changed the shape of its field, and it did so by asking a simple question and pursuing it with a relentlessness that most political philosophers before and since have not matched. What principles of justice would free and rational people, concerned to advance their own interests, agree to if they had to choose from behind a veil of ignorance — not knowing which position in the resulting society they would occupy, not knowing their class, race, talents, conception of the good, or moment in history?

The thought experiment, which Rawls called the original position, is the most famous piece of philosophical machinery the tradition has produced in the last century, and it has been endlessly attacked and endlessly extended. Its purpose, which critics sometimes miss, is not to tell us what a just society looks like. Its purpose is to generate a standard of political justification that does not depend on the particular advantages of the people doing the justifying. The veil of ignorance is a way of asking whether a principle of political order could be defended to everyone it binds, not merely to those whom it benefits, and Rawls believed — on the strength of a long and intricate argument we cannot do justice to here — that the principles free people would choose from behind the veil would be something like the following. First, each person is to have an equal right to the most extensive basic liberties compatible with similar liberties for others. Second, social and economic inequalities are to be arranged so that they are both to the greatest benefit of the least advantaged and attached to positions open to all under conditions of fair equality of opportunity.

It is worth saying, at the risk of seeming partisan in a chapter that is trying to be fair, that the Rawlsian achievement is extraordinary. Before Rawls, serious liberal political theory had been under philosophical siege for decades, cornered by utilitarianism on one side and various forms of skepticism on the other, and it had lost the capacity to articulate its own premises with confidence. Rawls reconstructed the tradition on a basis that drew on Kant without being dependent on Kant's metaphysics, that treated the equal standing of each person as the foundational commitment of political morality, and that generated substantive principles of justice from that commitment without collapsing into either procedural thinness or perfectionist paternalism. The sentence that summarizes the whole project — "the right is prior to the good" — is what Rawls meant by insisting that principles of justice must be acceptable to persons whose conceptions of the good life differ, and therefore must be defensible independently of any particular conception of what that life should contain.

We will say, in the section that follows, where we think the Rawlsian program ran into trouble. We want to be clear here about what it accomplished. The twentieth century had produced, in the name of the good, in the name of the true self, in the name of the historical destiny of a people or a class, some of the most comprehensive evils human beings have ever committed. A political philosophy adequate to the moral lessons of that century had to find a way to insist that political authority must be justified to each person it binds in terms that do not require her to accept, as a precondition, some particular account of what her life is for. That is the work Rawls did. It is the reason his book became the reference point against which every subsequent political philosophy has had to position itself. It is also the reason his book is a moral document as much as a philosophical one — an attempt, in the shadow of the camps and the gulags, to describe a politics that treats each person as what Kant said she was.

***

The sober contemporary defender of this tradition is Francis Fukuyama, whose 2022 book *Liberalism and Its Discontents* is the best statement the liberal tradition currently has of why its critics on both flanks are serious and why its critics on both flanks are nonetheless wrong about the cure. Fukuyama is not a polemicist. He has read Deneen, he has read the identity-politics left, and he acknowledges without flinching what both camps see: that liberalism has been flattened in practice into a thin proceduralism that has starved its adherents of the shared goods on which a political community depends, and that the resulting vacuum has been filled by illiberal movements that offer what liberalism has neglected to offer. His response is not to capitulate to either side but to argue that the pathologies both diagnose are distortions of liberalism rather than liberalism's logical outcome, and that the repair is a recovery of what liberalism was rather than a replacement of liberalism with something else.

We will take up, in the chapters that follow, whether Fukuyama's optimism is warranted. It is enough to say here that his book is the most credible contemporary version of the tradition's self-defense, and that any honest engagement with the critiques of liberalism — the communitarian critique in Chapter 4, the post-liberal critique in Chapter 5 — needs to have read Fukuyama before it concludes that the tradition has nothing left to say.

***

We have walked through this tradition at some length because we believe — and the book's title notwithstanding — that the liberal revolution was moral progress, not merely a philosophical preference. This is the claim we want, at the center of the chapter, to state without hedging.

The recognition that every person is an end in herself, that no one is merely a subject, that political authority requires justification to each person it binds, is one of the durable goods of the modern age. It is not a matter of taste. It is not one conception of politics among the several on offer. It is an achievement, hard-won over centuries, against which the political alternatives are to be measured. The horrors that liberal rights protect against — arbitrary arrest, confiscation of property, torture, religious persecution, caste hierarchy, the reduction of persons to the instruments of a ruler's purposes — are real. They are not abstractions. For most of human history they were the ordinary content of political life, and in most of the world for most of the twentieth century they were the ordinary content of political life, and in much of the world today they remain the ordinary content of political life. The reason most readers of this book have not personally experienced them is not that human nature has improved. It is that the political and legal architecture of the liberal revolution has held, in our particular corner of the world, for a particular historical moment, against pressures that in other times and other places have swept such protections away.

The writ of habeas corpus. The right against self-incrimination. The prohibition of cruel and unusual punishment. The protection of religious conscience. The protection of speech. The protection of property from seizure without compensation. The requirement that a person be charged with an offense known to the law before being deprived of her liberty. The principle that the law applies equally to the person who wrote it and to the person charged under it. These are not procedural niceties. They are the mechanisms by which the claim that every person is an end in herself is made to bite in the institutions that would otherwise treat her as a means. They are written in the Bill of Rights, in the European Convention on Human Rights, in the Universal Declaration of Human Rights adopted by the General Assembly in 1948 — a document whose drafting, it is worth remembering, was the direct moral response of the international community to what had been done to human beings in the preceding decade. They are the inheritance the liberal revolution bequeathed to the modern world, and the inheritance is not negotiable.

The steelman of liberalism is not a desiccated procedural republic. The steelman is a polity in which each person is permitted, and supported in, the pursuit of her own best understanding of a life, and in which the state does not pretend to know better than she does what her life is for. That polity is a generous vision. It is the vision under which a religious minority, a sexual minority, a philosophical eccentric, an artist whose art no one else yet understands, a person whose conscience tells her to refuse what the majority demands — each of these, in a liberal order, has a standing that no prior political tradition ever gave them. The person who does not want to fit is protected. The life that the majority does not recognize is permitted. The authority that would coerce conformity, in the name of the nation, the faith, the race, the revolution, the market, or the cosmic plan, is held to account by a standard higher than its own ambition. The generosity of that vision — the willingness to extend to each person the space within which her own life is hers — is what we mean when we say the liberal revolution was moral progress. And we are on liberalism's side.

***

We are also, and for the same reasons, obligated to name the internal strains the tradition has developed, because the critiques that will occupy the next two chapters are serious and the liberal who pretends otherwise is indulging in complacency. The strains are not destroyers of the tradition. They are the places where it has become clear that the tradition, left to itself, does not have the resources to solve the problems it generates, and that this is one of the reasons the citizen has become harder and harder to find.

The first strain is the bracketing of the good. Rawls said that the right is prior to the good, and the formulation was a principled attempt to justify political order to persons who disagreed about what the good life contained. It has proved, in practice, harder to sustain than the argument supposed. A society organized around the bracket cannot, by its own stated commitments, articulate the shared goods that hold it together, because to articulate them would be to violate the bracket. A politics of this kind finds itself, at moments of crisis, unable to say what it is for, because its official philosophy has forbidden it to say what it is for, and the citizens raised in it find themselves incapable of describing the public life they share, because the language in which it might be described has been ruled out of public order. The critics will say that a political community cannot survive on procedural commitments alone, that it needs a thicker moral vocabulary, and that the liberal insistence on the bracket leaves its adherents without the resources to resist the tribes that offer such a vocabulary by offering a worse one. They are not entirely wrong.

The second strain is what Michael Sandel, in a book we will take up in the next chapter, called the unencumbered self — the picture of the person implicit in the Rawlsian original position, the self that exists prior to any of its ends and chooses them freely from a position of reflection uncontaminated by community, tradition, or constitutive attachment. Sandel's charge, which we will develop in the chapter to come, is that this self is a philosophical fiction and that the fiction has sociological consequences. The actual selves that liberalism purports to protect are not unencumbered. They are formed, all the way down, by the families, neighborhoods, congregations, languages, and traditions into which they were born, and a political philosophy that pretends otherwise — that treats those formative attachments as so many preferences to be adjusted by the chooser at her convenience — does not protect the self. It corrodes the self, by teaching the self to regard its own formative ties as external to who she really is, and ends up producing exactly the kind of deracinated individual who is raw material for the mass movements the tradition is supposed to protect her against. The critique is older than Sandel and deeper than any one book, and its deepest version is that a liberalism that takes the unencumbered self as its anthropology has hollowed out the persons whose freedom it was supposed to secure.

The third strain is what we will call the commercial drift. Locke listed three natural rights — life, liberty, property — and the inclusion of property was part of the tradition from the beginning. Over three centuries the weight placed on that third right has grown, and with it a tendency to reduce freedom to market choice and the citizen to the consumer. This tendency is not intrinsic to the tradition. Smith, as we saw, would not have recognized it. Kant would have been appalled by it. Mill warned against it. Rawls constructed an entire theory designed to insist that the basic liberties were prior to economic distribution and that the worst-off had a claim on the society that mere market outcomes did not capture. And yet the drift has happened. Contemporary liberal societies routinely present themselves to their members as marketplaces of lifestyle options, and the language of freedom has, in much public discourse, become indistinguishable from the language of consumer choice. The post-liberal critics see in this drift the essence of liberalism, and they are wrong to see it as the essence. But they are right that it is a real tendency within the tradition, and that the tradition has not always had the philosophical resources to resist it. The commercial drift is not what the best liberals wanted. It is something the best liberals should have been more alarmed by.

***

These strains do not falsify the liberal revolution. They locate it. They tell us that the revolution succeeded, brilliantly, at the work of the last three centuries — the limitation of arbitrary power, the protection of conscience, the legal recognition of each person's standing — and that it has run into difficulties at the work the coming century requires, which is the cultivation of the shared life in which the individual protected by those rights can live something more than a protected loneliness. The tradition the liberals supplanted, the republican tradition we walked through in Chapter 2, had no worked-out theory of individual rights and suffered for the lack of it. The tradition the liberals built supplied the theory, and bequeathed to us the institutions, and enabled the freedoms under which most of us live. We are not prepared, in the pages that follow, to trade those freedoms for anything. The point is that the liberal revolution did not replace the republican tradition. It supplemented it. The question the rest of this volume pursues is whether the two can be held together — whether the moral achievement of the liberals and the civic achievement of the republicans can be combined into a single adequate answer to the question of what the citizen is and how the citizen is to be.

The communitarians we take up in the next chapter will argue that liberals have not solved this problem and, on their own resources, cannot solve it. The post-liberals we take up in the chapter after that will argue that the problem is unsolvable on liberal premises at all, and that liberalism must therefore be replaced. We think both of those judgments are too quick, and we will say so in Chapter 7, where we describe the synthesis that animates this book. But we say here, at the end of the liberal chapter, that we owe liberalism more than a defensive nod. We owe it the recognition that the citizen as a figure of the modern age is not thinkable without it. The person who bears rights against the state, who is addressed by political power as someone to whom a justification is owed, who cannot be tortured or conscripted or silenced or stripped of her property at the pleasure of a ruler — that person is the liberal achievement. The republican tradition gives us the citizen as a member. The liberal tradition gives us the citizen as a bearer of dignity that no membership can override. Any account of the citizen adequate to modern life has to hold both, and the work of this book, from here to the end of the first volume, is to show how it can be done.


---

# Chapter 4
## The Communitarian Reply

The previous chapter ended with the liberal tradition at the height of its powers. Rawls had given liberalism its most rigorous modern form; Berlin had given it its most humane philosophical voice; and the liberal order, in the decades after the Second World War, had delivered more freedom to more people, across more lines of sex and race and class and religion, than any rival had managed in the whole history of the species. If the tradition has critics — and it does, and they are serious — we owe those critics the same charity we just paid to liberalism itself. We owe them, in particular, an honest statement of what they actually believe, unflattened by caricature, before we can begin to say where we agree and where we do not.

This chapter is about the first wave of those critics, the ones who arose in the 1980s in direct philosophical combat with *A Theory of Justice* and who have collectively come to be called the communitarians. We use the label because it has stuck and because the figures themselves, with varying degrees of comfort, accepted it; but the reader should know from the outset that "communitarian" is a family resemblance rather than a doctrine. Alasdair MacIntyre and Charles Taylor and Michael Sandel and Michael Walzer would not all sign the same manifesto, and at least one of them would decline to sign anything. What unites them is not a shared program but a shared diagnosis. They looked at the liberal self — the unencumbered chooser behind the veil of ignorance — and they said, in their different voices, that this self cannot exist, that the political philosophy built upon it is therefore built on a fiction, and that the fiction is not harmless. The person the fiction conjures into being, if pursued long enough by the institutions that rely on it, becomes a real person, stripped and hollow and available for any ideology that offers him a world. That, the communitarians said, is the society we are building. We are building it not out of malice but out of a philosophical default that cannot articulate the value of the thick communal life it is quietly dissolving.

This is the argument we need to reconstruct carefully, because it is the hinge of the whole book. If Arendt and Hoffer are right that loneliness is the raw material of mass movement — and the second volume will argue that they are — then the communitarians are the tradition that explains *why liberal societies produce so much loneliness*. They do not explain it as a moral failing of liberals. They explain it as an unintended consequence of a philosophical self-understanding that has leaked from the seminar rooms into the culture, and from the culture into the institutions, until the dominant vocabulary for describing what a person is has lost the power to describe most of what a person actually is.

***

To understand why communitarianism had to arise when it did, one has to understand what Rawls had done. Before *A Theory of Justice* was published in 1971, political philosophy in the English-speaking world had been, for a generation, in a state of polite suspended animation. The great question — what makes a political order just — was being treated as, at best, a historical question (what did Locke mean? what did Kant mean?) or, at worst, a matter of linguistic analysis (what do we mean when we say *just*?). Rawls did something that almost no one had dared since Sidgwick. He offered a full constructive theory of justice, with intuitions specified, principles derived, and a method — the original position, the veil of ignorance, reflective equilibrium — that allowed the argument to be tested rather than merely admired. The book was an event. It did what only a handful of books in any generation of philosophy do: it reset the conversation.

And because it reset the conversation, every critic who came after had to engage it. One could not reply to Rawls by waving in the general direction of Aristotle. One had to show, in Rawls's own terms, where the argument went wrong. This is the task the communitarians took on. Their books are not populist jeremiads. They are patient, technical, sometimes dry, always respectful engagements with the master text of their generation. MacIntyre's *After Virtue* appeared in 1981, Sandel's *Liberalism and the Limits of Justice* in 1982, Walzer's *Spheres of Justice* in 1983, Taylor's *Sources of the Self* in 1989, Bellah and his collaborators' *Habits of the Heart* in 1985. Five books in eight years, from five authors working largely in isolation from one another, arriving at a convergent verdict. The period is one of the most intellectually fertile in twentieth-century moral and political thought, and it is worth honoring as such before we take up the individual arguments.

***

The most philosophically radical of the five is probably MacIntyre, and we begin with him because his argument operates at a level of depth beneath which the others largely assume. *After Virtue* opens with what has become one of the most arresting images in modern moral philosophy. MacIntyre asks us to imagine a catastrophe — a natural science catastrophe rather than a political one. Imagine that a backlash against the sciences destroys the laboratories, burns the books, executes the scientists. Generations pass. Then a revival begins. People dig up fragments of the old texts. They find a bit of a periodic table here, a half-equation there, a lecture note from a seminar whose context is gone. They start using the words — *mass*, *neutrino*, *gene*, *valence* — but they no longer stand in the web of practices and tests and shared inquiries that gave those words their meaning. They argue furiously with each other using these terms. The arguments cannot be resolved, because the vocabulary has been severed from the form of life that made it intelligible. A sophisticated outside observer would see that what looked like science was a kind of ritual mimicry of science, the shell without the animal.

That, MacIntyre says, is the state of modern moral discourse. We use the old words — *good*, *right*, *justice*, *virtue*, *rights*, *duty* — with every appearance of confidence, but the words are fragments taken from incompatible traditions whose contexts we have forgotten. When a Kantian and a utilitarian and a rights-talk liberal and a residual Aristotelian argue about abortion or war or punishment, they are not disagreeing in the way scientists disagree, on a single question within a shared framework. They are shouting across a rubble-field of broken vocabularies, each invoking the authority of a tradition most of them could not reconstruct if pressed. This is why, MacIntyre says, modern moral argument is "interminable." It is not that we lack good will. It is that the conceptual resources have been shattered, and we are fighting over the pieces.

This is where MacIntyre makes his most radical move, the one that still scandalizes liberal readers a generation later. There is, he argues, no such thing as ethics "in general." Ethics is always ethics *within a tradition of practice* — a tradition of the kind the Aristotelian Nicomachean Ethics took for granted, in which the virtues are defined in relation to goods internal to practices, the practices are situated within a form of communal life, and the form of life is carried by a narrative that each member of the community is in some sense living through. The Enlightenment project, he says, made the audacious attempt to derive an ethics without any of this — to deduce moral principles from the premises of unencumbered rationality alone — and the attempt failed because it could not have succeeded. The Enlightenment bequeathed to us, not a finished ethics, but the debris of one, and our moral life now consists in arranging that debris in patterns we find temporarily pleasing.

The reader new to MacIntyre often thinks at this point that he is reading a reactionary — that the argument is meant to return us to an imagined Aristotelian polis with its hierarchies and exclusions intact. This is a serious misreading. MacIntyre is not a political theorist offering a model. He is a moral philosopher making a diagnostic claim: that we cannot get from where we are to anywhere good unless we first admit that our moral vocabulary is not functioning as we think it is. The positive program he eventually offers — most fully worked out in his later books on dependent rational animals and on the recovery of practices within subcommunities — is monkish rather than imperial. It asks us to find or build small communities within which moral meaning can again be transmitted across generations, and to wait. It is a philosophy of the dark ages, as he himself has more than once acknowledged.

We will come back to the question of whether this is adequate. For now, notice what MacIntyre has added to the conversation that was not there before. He has argued that the modern liberal citizen — the one who announces his "values" as if choosing items from a catalogue — is not exercising autonomy in any deep sense. He is arranging fragments he does not fully understand. And the reason radical ideologies are able to recruit him so effectively is that they offer him something the fragment-vocabulary cannot: a total, coherent, narratively unified account of what he is for. The mass movement is, among other things, a counterfeit tradition. It mimics the structure MacIntyre says every intelligible moral life requires. And in the absence of real traditions to inhabit, the counterfeit will do.

***

Sandel's contribution is more surgical. *Liberalism and the Limits of Justice*, published when its author was twenty-nine, is one of the great feats of close reading in late twentieth-century political philosophy. Sandel does what nobody had quite done before: he takes the metaphysical assumptions lurking underneath Rawls's political theory and pulls them, one by one, into the light.

Rawls had tried to sidestep metaphysics. His theory of justice, he insisted, was "political, not metaphysical." The veil of ignorance was a device of representation; the original position was a thought experiment, not a description of any actual self. But Sandel shows, patiently and relentlessly, that the device cannot do the work Rawls asks of it unless some picture of the self is already in place. To choose principles of justice from behind the veil — to bracket one's particular commitments, relationships, religious convictions, attachments — the self doing the choosing must be the kind of self for which those commitments are, in principle, separable. The self must be, as Sandel puts it in the book's most quoted phrase, "prior to its ends." It must be a bare unity of will, able to step back from everything that is normally called its "identity" and evaluate those attachments as if from outside.

Sandel's question is whether any such self is coherent. On one hand, the Rawlsian self must be prior to its ends, or else the whole contractualist apparatus collapses: if the self is constituted by its particular attachments, then it cannot meaningfully choose principles of justice while bracketing them. On the other hand, the self must be the *author* of its ends, the agent whose autonomous choice is the moral source of those ends' authority — this is the Kantian inheritance Rawls explicitly honors. But if the self is prior to its ends in the strong sense the argument requires, it cannot also be the author of them, because a self stripped of all particulars is not an author of anything. It is a point, a bare locus of consent, with no resources to generate any particular choice rather than another. And if the self is the author of its ends in the robust sense Kantian autonomy requires, then the ends it authors must belong to it, must be constitutive of its moral personality in a way that makes their bracketing incoherent. The deontological self must be both prior to its ends and the author of its ends, and it cannot be both.

This is, we think, one of the great philosophical moves of the late twentieth century, and it has aged well. Sandel is not saying that liberalism is wrong to prize autonomy. He is saying that the particular conception of autonomy Rawls needs — autonomy as the bare chooser, untouched by attachments — is incoherent. Any real human autonomy is exercised from within a thicket of constitutive commitments. A person deliberating about whether to keep a promise to his mother is not bracketing his relationship with his mother in order to evaluate the promise "from nowhere"; he is deliberating as the person whose mother that is. A citizen deliberating about justice in his country is not bracketing his citizenship; he is deliberating as a citizen of that country, with its particular history, obligations, shames, and loves. To bracket these is not to reach a purer moral standpoint. It is to reach no standpoint at all.

Sandel's later work extends this founding insight into a sustained critique of what he calls the "procedural republic" — the polity that has organized itself around the liberal ideal of bracketing substantive questions about the good, and that finds itself, having so organized itself, unable to generate the civic ties, loyalties, and shared purposes that a democracy needs to sustain itself. *Democracy's Discontent* traces the American version of this story: how a republic founded on a civic-republican tradition of self-government has, over a century and a half, reorganized itself around procedural neutrality and now discovers that the procedural neutrality cannot answer the questions its citizens are actually asking. *The Tyranny of Merit* turns the same argument on the meritocratic ideology that has become the procedural republic's implicit theodicy — the story that tells the winners they deserve their winnings and, more corrosively, tells the losers they deserve their losses. We will come back to Sandel's merit argument in Volume II, where it does important diagnostic work. For now it is enough to note that Sandel has been, for forty years, the most consistent and philosophically disciplined voice arguing that the liberal settlement, taken as a total account of political life, is inadequate to the political life of the liberal societies that have tried to live by it.

***

If MacIntyre is the most radical and Sandel the most surgical, Charles Taylor is the most philosophically capacious. Taylor's achievement is hard to summarize because it is vast. *Sources of the Self* (1989) is nearly six hundred pages of intellectual history tracing the construction of the modern self through Augustine, Montaigne, Descartes, Locke, the Romantics, and the twentieth-century critics — a book which, when one finally closes it, has changed the way one reads almost everything else. *The Ethics of Authenticity* is a short, almost popular book that distills one strand of the longer argument into a meditation on the modern ideal of being true to oneself, its real moral weight, and the ways in which it has been degraded into self-expression severed from any horizon of significance beyond the self. *A Secular Age* (2007) is the long book about the modern religious condition — about what it means that we now inhabit, as Taylor puts it, an "immanent frame" in which transcendence is no longer the default, and what it costs and offers the self who lives within it.

The point of reading Taylor, for our purposes, is to absorb a single liberating observation: the modern self is *a historical achievement*. It is not the universal human condition — not what humans always were, released from ignorance, when the fog of superstition finally cleared. It is a specific configuration of self-understanding, painstakingly assembled over five centuries of Western moral and religious and intellectual life, and it carries within it both real goods we should not want to surrender and real pathologies that its architects did not foresee. Understanding how this self was built — the particular inwardness of Augustine, the disengaged reason of Descartes, the affirmation of ordinary life in the Reformers, the expressive self of Rousseau and the Romantics, the buffered self of the modern secular — is prerequisite to understanding its pathologies. We cannot diagnose what we cannot see, and for most moderns the modern self is invisible precisely because we are inside it.

Taylor's coinage of the "buffered self" is especially useful for our argument. The premodern self, he says, was *porous*. Its boundary was not fixed; it could be penetrated by spirits, by gods, by the force of a relic, by the enchantment of a place, by the ancestral dead. To be a person was to be continuous in certain ways with the world around you, including its unseen dimensions. The modern self, by contrast, is *buffered*. It has a sharp boundary; meaning arises inside the boundary and is a product of the self's own reflective activity; the world outside is mute matter, available for manipulation. The buffered self is, among other things, the precondition of modern freedom. It is what allows you to walk through an enchanted landscape unaffected. It is also, Taylor notes, the condition of a specific kind of loneliness — a sense of being sealed off, of having to generate meaning from within because the outside will not supply it — that is a distinctive modern ache. The "nova effect," his name for the explosion of plural spiritual options within the immanent frame, is one of his names for the disorientation that follows when the sources of meaning multiply past the point where any of them can be firmly inhabited.

This is not a sermon against the modern self. Taylor is a Catholic, and he is not above making spiritual arguments, but *A Secular Age* is resolutely philosophical and descriptive, and it takes pains to honor the moral gains of the modern condition: the affirmation of ordinary life, the end of theologically sanctioned cruelty, the seriousness with which modernity takes the inner life of the individual. The book is not a case for going back. It is a case for knowing where we are. The communitarian pay-off of Taylor's work, for our purposes, is this: the self that shows up in Rawls's original position is not a neutral abstraction. It is a particular historical type, the buffered self at a late stage, taking itself to be the universal form of rational agency because it has forgotten how it came to be. A political philosophy built on this self will, without intending to, push the institutions that surround it in the direction of the self's own assumptions — toward contract rather than covenant, toward choice rather than inheritance, toward procedure rather than substance — and the institutions so pushed will gradually cease to form any other kind of self. This is not a conspiracy. It is a feedback loop, and its end state is the hollowed-out individual our book is trying to understand.

***

Walzer's *Spheres of Justice* comes at the problem from a different angle, and it is important to include him because he makes a move the more metaphysical communitarians do not. Walzer is, by temperament and politics, a social democrat of the mid-twentieth-century variety, and his communitarianism is the least nostalgic in the tradition. His argument is not that the self is constituted by a tradition. His argument is about *goods* — the things we distribute — and about the meanings those goods have within particular communities.

Rawls had tried to derive principles of just distribution from a single standpoint, abstracted from any particular community. Walzer asks whether this is even the right ambition. Goods, he observes, are not fungible units of welfare; they have meanings, and the meanings are social. What counts as a fair distribution of medicine is different in kind from what counts as a fair distribution of political office, and both are different from what counts as a fair distribution of love, or honor, or grace, or a place in a college class. Each of these spheres has its own internal logic, which arises from the way the good in question is understood within the community that distributes it. Justice, Walzer says, is "complex" — a matter of keeping each sphere in its proper domain, preventing the kinds of good that should dominate only within their own sphere from colonizing others. (The dominance of money over medicine, or of pedigree over political office, is a characteristic form of injustice precisely because it violates spheres rather than internal rules.)

The deep point, for us, is the argument against what Rawls and his tradition had tried to do. There is no "view from nowhere" in political philosophy, Walzer says. Justice is not a theorem derivable from principles outside history. It is a practice worked out by particular peoples with particular histories of struggle over particular goods, and the philosopher's job is not to replace that practice with a theorem but to participate in it as a thoughtful citizen — to reason about the meanings of the goods his community is fighting over, to draw out the implications of those meanings for distribution, and to submit his reasoning to the same public deliberation as anyone else's. This is a communitarian claim about the method of political philosophy itself, and it is one of the most widely accepted of the communitarian positions even among readers who have no other communitarian sympathies. The view from nowhere, it turns out, was always a view from somewhere that had forgotten it was somewhere.

***

Etzioni's project is the most practical of the five, which is why it is both the most accessible and, in a certain way, the most constrained. Amitai Etzioni — a sociologist rather than a philosopher, and a veteran of Israeli and American public life — did not try to win the argument with Rawls in metaphysics. He tried to build a movement. *The Spirit of Community* (1993) and the associated platform of what he called "responsive communitarianism" set out a middle way between the libertarian and the authoritarian: rights without responsibilities are adolescent, responsibilities without rights are authoritarian, and a decent public philosophy has to affirm both in the same breath. The communitarian network he organized produced a platform in 1991, signed by an unlikely cross-ideological roster, arguing for specific policy positions — on family, on schools, on public safety, on campaign finance — that would restore the balance between the autonomy of the individual and the order of the community.

Etzioni is not as philosophically powerful as Sandel or Taylor, and he knew it. What he offered instead was a bridge between the theoretical critique and the practical life of a liberal democracy — a set of positions a working politician could plausibly run on, a set of concerns a local school board could plausibly address, a set of sensibilities that could organize a PTA meeting or a zoning hearing as easily as a philosophy seminar. The responsive communitarian movement made real headway in the 1990s, with echoes in the Clinton-era "third way" and in some of the Blair-era British reformism, before it stalled. The reasons it stalled are instructive and we will return to them at the end of this chapter, but it is worth noting that Etzioni made a serious attempt to turn the communitarian diagnosis into a livable civic practice, and the attempt has not been surpassed in the decades since.

***

The sixth figure, or rather the sixth voice — since the book is collectively authored — is *Habits of the Heart*, the 1985 study by Robert Bellah, Richard Madsen, William Sullivan, Ann Swidler, and Steven Tipton. If MacIntyre and Taylor give us the philosophical and historical bones of the communitarian argument, *Habits of the Heart* gives it the empirical flesh. The book is an interview study — two hundred middle-class Americans talking at length about love, work, community, and the good life — and its great achievement is to listen closely enough to hear the gap between what its subjects say and what they do.

The Americans in the study live, on the whole, in dense webs of commitment. They are embedded in marriages, churches, neighborhoods, civic organizations, voluntary associations. Their lives are structured by obligations they did not choose and could not easily shed without becoming unrecognizable to themselves. But when the authors ask them to describe what they are doing, the vocabulary available to them is almost entirely the vocabulary of expressive individualism. They describe their marriages as opportunities for personal growth. They describe their churches as places where they are supported in their individual spiritual journeys. They describe their civic commitments in terms of the satisfaction they derive from them. The *language* is the language of the unencumbered self, choosing values. The *life* is the life of the encumbered self, held together by ties the speaker cannot articulate.

Bellah's most famous example has entered the English language: "Sheilaism." A young nurse named Sheila, asked about her religious beliefs, describes her faith as a private, personal system — "my own Sheilaism," she calls it — with a god who wants her to love herself, be gentle with herself, pursue her own fulfillment. She is not alienated; she is a kind and generous person. She is not unreligious; she is sincere. But she has no vocabulary for the tradition that would connect her inner life to any community of others, no practice that would hold her to any standard outside her own sense of flourishing, no narrative that would link her life to anyone else's. If asked to articulate what she believes, all she can do is point back at herself.

Bellah's point is not to mock Sheila. It is to observe that the language available to her has no other shape. American public vocabulary, in the years the study was conducted, had been so thoroughly colonized by the idioms of expressive individualism that even the most community-minded person had to reach for an individualist phrasing to describe what she was doing. The ties were still there — marriages, churches, kids, neighbors, work — but the ability to *say* what those ties meant, to name the substance of the life they supported, had been largely lost. And once a practice cannot be named, it cannot be transmitted; and once it cannot be transmitted, the next generation may inherit the practice only as a weakened residue, or not inherit it at all.

*Habits of the Heart* is the book that shows, in the voices of ordinary Americans, what MacIntyre and Taylor are talking about in the abstract. It is also, for that reason, the most politically portable of the communitarian texts.
