It is the most accurate forecast of the condition of a mature liberal democracy ever written, and the fact that it was written before the invention of the telephone, before the welfare state, before television, before the internet, before the smartphone, is one of the intellectual miracles of the nineteenth century. What Tocqueville saw was that the combination of democratic equality and commercial prosperity and administrative centralization, without any strong countervailing layer of associational life, would produce a population that was formally free, materially comfortable, and incapable of governing itself. He was right. We are his forecast.

The reason Tocqueville matters so much to this book is that he is the writer who sees both halves of the problem we are trying to describe. He sees the value of the individual — he is no enemy of modern liberty, and his defense of religious pluralism and private property is unambiguous. He sees the necessity of the polity — he understands that free individuals are produced only by the associations and townships and deliberative habits that turn formal freedom into practiced freedom. And he sees, with a clarity no subsequent writer has matched, that the collapse of the second produces the degradation of the first. The soft despotism passage is not a prediction about the state. It is a prediction about what happens to souls when the state becomes the only reliable public actor in their lives.

Every argument this book makes, at some level, is an elaboration of a passage Tocqueville wrote in 1840.

***

If Tocqueville is the visiting diagnostician, Lincoln is the American voice the argument relies on most for its specifically civic-republican register. We do not invoke him as a rhetorical convenience. We invoke him because the problem he faced — the problem of how a free people can preserve the institutions of their freedom against the passions that would dissolve them — is our problem, and because the answers he worked out, in a political vocabulary that was recognizably American and recognizably classical at once, are answers we have not improved upon.

The Lyceum Address, delivered in January of 1838 to the Young Men's Lyceum of Springfield, Illinois, is the text. Lincoln was twenty-eight. He was speaking to a local audience of small-town Illinoisans. The topic was announced as "The Perpetuation of Our Political Institutions." What he produced is one of the strangest and most penetrating speeches in the American canon.

Lincoln begins by observing that the danger to American institutions will not come from abroad. No foreign power could cross the Atlantic and the Appalachians and conquer a free people in arms. "If destruction be our lot, we must ourselves be its author and finisher. As a nation of freemen, we must live through all time, or die by suicide." The danger, he says, is internal, and it is specifically the danger of mob violence and the disregard for law that mob violence breeds. He cites recent lynchings, recent riots, recent summary killings by crowds who had decided that justice was too slow and the law too weak. In each case, Lincoln says, the crime against the particular victim was bad enough. But the deeper crime was against the law itself — against the habit of submitting grievances to courts, of arguing disputes through institutions, of accepting procedural outcomes one did not personally favor because the procedure was the thing being defended.

Lincoln's remedy is a phrase he uses with no apology and that has scandalized some of his subsequent interpreters. He calls for what he names a "political religion" of reverence for the laws. Let every American, he says, let every lover of liberty, let every mother pledge her child, let every schoolmaster teach his pupils, let every pulpit and bar and legislature speak of reverence for the Constitution and the laws. Let the bad laws, he adds, be repealed as soon as possible — but while they continue in force, let them be religiously observed, because the habit of lawful observance is the habit that preserves the institutions themselves.

The word "religion," for Lincoln, is doing precise work. He does not mean piety in the sectarian sense. He means what Cicero meant by *religio* and what Tocqueville meant when he wrote that "despotism may govern without faith, but liberty cannot." A free polity requires a shared reverence — a set of pre-rational commitments — that citizens carry into public life as a kind of moral substrate. Without that substrate, every institution becomes renegotiable in the moment of any particular citizen's dissatisfaction with its outcome, and institutions that are renegotiable in the moment of dissatisfaction are not institutions at all. They are provisional conveniences, and they will be burned when the inconvenience passes a threshold.

The political religion of the Lyceum Address is the civic-republican claim that a free people has to love the institutions that make their freedom possible, not merely use them. It is the claim against which every subsequent American argument about reform and preservation has had to measure itself. We quote Lincoln on this point because the twenty-first-century American, on both the left and the right, has largely abandoned it. Contemporary political discourse treats institutions instrumentally — as means to partisan ends, to be preserved when one's own side controls them and torn apart when the other side does. This is the posture Lincoln identified as suicidal, in 1838, before any of the specific institutional stresses of our own time had appeared. He was right then; he is right now; and a country that cannot recover what he meant by political religion is a country that will not last as a republic, whatever it continues to call itself.

The Gettysburg Address, twenty-five years later, is the other text, and its 272 words are, in our view, the most compact civic-republican statement in the American tradition. Lincoln is dedicating a battlefield cemetery; he is speaking over the bodies of men who have just died in the largest battle ever fought on American soil; and he is telling the surviving citizens of the republic what those men died for. He does not say they died to defeat the South. He does not say they died to free the slaves, though freeing the slaves is the action that will now follow. He says they died so that "government of the people, by the people, for the people, shall not perish from the earth."

That formula — of the people, by the people, for the people — is not a liberal slogan. It is a republican one. Liberal government is government that protects the rights of the people. Republican government is government that is *done by* the people — that has no existence apart from their participation, their judgment, their responsibility, their sacrifice when the sacrifice is required. Lincoln is not abandoning the liberal inheritance; he is adapting it. The Declaration's "all men are created equal" is preserved and radicalized. But the frame in which it is preserved is the civic-republican frame: the nation is the people acting together, and the people acting together is what the dead at Gettysburg were defending. A republic whose citizens have ceased to act together has nothing for its soldiers to die for, whatever the formal rights on its books.

Lincoln is the American bridge between the Founders' generation and the mass-democratic twentieth century. He is also, and this is the deeper point, the figure who shows that the Federalist/Anti-Federalist argument can be held together by someone of sufficient moral and political seriousness. Lincoln is a constitutional nationalist; he is also a small-town Illinoisan who carried the habits of the New England town meeting into the White House. He defends the large republic; he also understands that the large republic lives only on the moral capital generated by the small ones. He is Madison and Brutus at once. He is the American synthesis in a person.

***

The last figure we need for this chapter is the one who tried, in the full daylight of the twentieth century, to rescue the thing Lincoln had defended. John Dewey's *The Public and Its Problems*, published in 1927, is the most serious American attempt of its century to revive democratic citizenship in the conditions of mass society. Dewey is writing against Walter Lippmann, whose *Phantom Public* of 1925 had argued that democratic citizenship in a modern industrial society was essentially impossible — that ordinary people could not possibly know enough about the complex technical questions of modern governance to make informed judgments, and that democracy would therefore have to be replaced, in practice if not in name, by expert administration with periodic plebiscitary consent.

Dewey's response is the key move. Democracy, he says, is not a set of procedures. It is "a way of life." It is a mode of associated living, of communicated experience, in which every member of the community participates in shaping the conditions of his own life and in learning from the participation of others. The procedures — voting, legislatures, courts — are the outward form. The substance is the continuous practice of communication, inquiry, and shared decision among citizens who have learned to take one another seriously. A society in which the procedures operate but the substance has drained away is not a democracy. It is a husk of one.

Dewey's positive program is pedagogical all the way down. Every institution, he argues, is also a school of citizenship, or it is not fit for its place in a democratic polity. A workplace either teaches its workers the habits of cooperation, deliberation, and responsibility, or it trains them in subordination and resentment. A school either forms its students into the kind of people who can participate in self-government, or it produces credentialed consumers of expert opinion. A neighborhood either teaches its residents the arts of public life, or it is a sorting mechanism for privately lived lives. The question one asks of any institution, from a union to a church to a family to a baseball league, is not only "does it do its stated job?" It is also "does it produce citizens?" And if it does not, Dewey insists, it undermines the polity it inhabits, no matter how well it performs its formal function.

This is a profound argument and, in our view, substantially correct. It is also the argument with the clearest lineage to our own. When we argue in Volume III that institutional renewal is not a question of reforming institutions from the outside but of the conduct of persons within them, we are restating a Deweyan claim.

Dewey has weaknesses, and we will not hide them. His optimism about expert-guided publics — the faith that science, adequately communicated, would in time produce a competent democratic citizenry — underestimated something he could not yet see: the twentieth-century discovery that expertise can be a disguise for ideology, that the sciences of man are especially vulnerable to capture by partisan programs, and that a citizenry conditioned to defer to experts is a citizenry that has lost something Dewey himself valued, namely the practice of independent judgment. His book was written in 1927, before Stalinism, before Nazism, before the century had shown what ideological mass movements could do to the kind of "public" Dewey was trying to reconstruct. He did not see, because he could not yet see, how fragile the conditions for democratic inquiry are and how readily they dissolve into the conditions for democratic capture. Arendt, writing twenty-four years later with that experience behind her, understood something Dewey did not.

But the central Deweyan insight stands, and we rest on it. Democracy is pedagogical all the way down. A society that wants to remain self-governing has to treat every institution that forms its citizens as part of the constitutional order, and has to judge those institutions, at least in part, by whether they form the kind of citizens the constitutional order requires. A country that has stopped doing this — that treats schools, workplaces, media, families, and churches as matters indifferent to the political order, to be arranged on any grounds whatever — is a country that will discover, over time, that the political order is unsupported by the people it was supposed to contain.

***

Pull the threads. The Federalists built an institutional architecture capable of housing a free people across a continent. Their bet — that ambition could be harnessed, that representation could substitute for participation, that a large commercial republic could sustain liberty where small republics had failed — was largely vindicated. The Anti-Federalists warned that the architecture they could not prevent would, over time, erode the civic substrate on which free government depends. That warning, too, was largely vindicated. Tocqueville, arriving fifty years later, saw both at once: a republic whose national freedom rested on a layer of local associations whose decline, when it came, would leave the formal republic intact and the substance hollowed out. Lincoln, in the middle of the century, tried to name the political religion by which a free people could love the institutions that sheltered their freedom, and died in the attempt to preserve them. Dewey, in the next century, tried to translate that project into an industrial democracy and produced, for all his blind spots, the theoretical language in which we still argue.

The American tradition does not resolve the Federalist/Anti-Federalist argument. It keeps it alive. The Constitution is Federalist. The Bill of Rights is Anti-Federalist. The national government is Federalist. The federal system is Anti-Federalist. The Supreme Court is Federalist. Trial by jury is Anti-Federalist. The standing army is Federalist. The Second Amendment is, in its original civic-republican reading, Anti-Federalist. Madison and Hamilton gave us the frame. Brutus and the Federal Farmer shadowed it with a critique we still read when we want to know what was given up for the frame to stand. American political life has been, from the start, an unstable compromise between the commercial republic and the civic republic, and the instability has been its greatest strength. When the Federalist side wins decisively — when the national government absorbs everything, when representation fully displaces participation, when commerce eats civic life — the system degrades toward the soft despotism Tocqueville forecast. When the Anti-Federalist side wins decisively — when local particularism becomes absolute, when the national order fractures into the sectional passions the Constitution was designed to manage — the system degrades toward disunion, which in our history has meant civil war.

Health, for an American polity, is the maintenance of the tension. It is not the splitting of the difference; it is not centrism; it is not "both sides have a point." It is the preservation, in the institutional architecture and the cultural habits of the people, of both halves of an argument that cannot be won and should not be. The national commercial republic needs the local civic republic to supply it with citizens. The local civic republic needs the national commercial republic to defend it against aggression and to secure rights its own particularisms cannot reliably protect. Each depends on the other; each is the other's corrective; each degrades without the other. The American genius, when the American order has displayed any, has been the refusal to collapse the tension into a single principle.

We began this chapter by saying that the American experiment addressed, under real pressure, the tension this book is about. That tension is the tension between the individual and the polity, between rights and virtue, between the person who holds claims against the community and the person whose very capacity to hold claims is generated by the community he belongs to. The Federalists, for the most part, spoke for the first. The Anti-Federalists, for the most part, spoke for the second. Neither spoke for both; neither needed to, because their argument with each other did the speaking. What the American tradition has to teach is that the rights-and-virtue problem is not a problem to be solved once, by a philosopher, in a book. It is a problem to be managed, continuously, by a people who have learned to hold both terms in their hands at once. Rights without virtue decay into the soft despotism of equal and solitary consumers. Virtue without rights decays into the hard despotism of the community that permits no dissent. The American order, at its best, has insisted on both, imperfectly, unevenly, with terrible failures along the way — slavery chief among them, and the failures are part of the record — but with a persistence that has kept the tension alive for two and a half centuries, which is longer than most political orders have lasted.

The claim we want to land this chapter on is the claim that matters for what follows. The American tradition already contains what this book calls republican liberalism. It has always held rights and virtue together — unevenly, imperfectly, but in practice. It has always insisted that the citizen is an individual, with rights no majority can vote away, and a member, with obligations no private pursuit can dissolve. It has always known, in its institutions more than in its explicit theory, that the two terms are not rivals but conditions of each other. The Constitution protects rights; the Bill of Rights limits the government; the federal system preserves the local arenas in which virtue is practiced; the political religion Lincoln described is the habit of loving an order in which both hold. The American synthesis is not a formula. It is a living practice, often failing, periodically renewed.

The intellectual task of the next chapter is to make explicit what has been implicit in this inheritance and to extend it. We will argue, with Richard Dagger but for more ambitious purposes, that republican liberalism — rights against the state and non-domination in the polity, individual conscience and civic virtue, the person and the citizen as a single figure — is the usable answer to the question this book has been circling. It is not new. It is old. The Americans have been half-practicing it from the start. What we can do, at this hour, is name what they have been doing, say why it is worth defending, and begin to imagine what a renewed version of it would look like in the twenty-first century. For that we need a chapter of our own. We turn to it now.


---

# Chapter 7
## Republican Liberalism: A Usable Answer

We began this volume with the oldest question in political philosophy, the question that every tradition we have walked through is an attempt to answer. What does a human being owe the political community, and what does the community owe the human being? We noted then that the answer was not obvious, had never been obvious, and that the Western tradition had four serious, incompatible candidates on offer. We have now taken each of those candidates in turn. We have listened to the civic republicans from Aristotle to Pettit; we have listened to the classical liberals from Locke to Fukuyama; we have listened to the communitarians from Sandel to Taylor; we have listened to the post-liberals from Deneen to Vermeule. We have watched the Federalist and the Anti-Federalist make the same argument in American accents, Tocqueville mark what was extraordinary about the country that emerged, and Lincoln and Dewey try, in their different centuries, to save it.

We have put off saying what we think. It is time.

Our thesis is one sentence long. *The citizen is an individual who belongs.*

We want to be understood precisely. This is not a compromise between liberalism and republicanism. It is not a both-sides gesture of the kind that editorial writers perform when they do not want to be accused of a view. It is not a middle muddle, arrived at by splitting the difference between traditions and hoping no one notices the incoherence. It is the thesis that the deepest versions of the two traditions, read honestly, converge. Neither is complete without the other. Classical liberalism, pursued to its conclusion, requires the civic substrate it has been silently consuming; civic republicanism, pursued to its conclusion, requires the rights-bearing individual whose conscience it has sometimes been tempted to override. A citizen who is only an individual is a consumer with a ballot. A citizen who only belongs is a member of a tribe. The person we have in mind, the person the preceding chapters have been circling, is both at once.

We will make the argument by laying out four commitments, each of which the remaining two volumes will defend in practice. We want the reader to have them in view before we go any further.

***

The first commitment is that the person is prior to the state, but not prior to the community.

Locke was right about the first half. The state is a trust, held from free persons, who retain their rights whether or not the state chooses to recognize them. This was a moral discovery of the first rank, and every decent political order since the seventeenth century rests on it. A polity that forgets it ends at the re-education camp. When we say, as we do throughout this book, that we are not writing against the liberal revolution but writing to complete it, this is one of the things we mean. A person does not owe her existence to the polity. The polity does not own her. If it tries to, she is within her rights to resist it, and her resistance is not sedition but fidelity to a deeper law.

But Locke was wrong about the second half. He imagined the free persons as pre-social, as choosers surveying a landscape of possible associations from a state of nature and rationally selecting among them. The philosophical construction is useful as a thought experiment and disastrous as an anthropology. Real persons are never pre-social. They are born into families they did not choose, languages they did not invent, histories they did not author, and moral vocabularies that were given to them before they were old enough to refuse the gift. They become persons through these memberships. They learn to reason by being taught to reason; they learn to love by being loved; they learn what counts as a life worth living by watching people who are already living, and failing to live, such a life. The communitarian critics were right about this, and the classical liberals have still not fully absorbed it. The self that is capable of choosing is not found; it is grown.

What does this mean politically? It means that the state may not treat persons as its property, but neither may it treat them as if their capacities for agency, reason, and choice came from nowhere. The person whose rights the state is bound to respect is a person who has been formed — by family, by neighborhood, by school, by congregation, by workplace, by voluntary association, by shared cultural inheritance — and who will continue to require formation if she is to retain the capacities that make her rights meaningful. A polity that protects a person's right to think but is indifferent to whether she has the conditions to learn how to think is a polity that will eventually be protecting a right that no one can any longer exercise. Rights without the formative substrate of rights are letters of credit drawn on a bank that has closed.

The citizen, then, is someone whose personhood is prior to the state in the moral sense that classical liberals have always insisted on, and who is at the same time a creature of a community in the sociological sense that communitarians have always insisted on. The two are not in contradiction. They are two angles on the same fact. The human being is a being who must be formed in order to become free — and, once free, cannot be reduced to the forming conditions without being degraded. This is not a paradox. It is the ordinary structure of any formed life. A pianist is formed by her teachers and is not, for that reason, the property of her teachers. A scholar is formed by her tradition and is not, for that reason, barred from correcting it. A citizen is formed by her polity and is not, for that reason, available to be conscripted into its designs.

***

The second commitment concerns liberty itself. The classical liberal tradition, at its sharpest in Berlin, made a distinction between negative and positive liberty — between freedom *from* interference and freedom *for* some substantive purpose — and argued, with the twentieth century's cautionary tales in view, that the first was the only version a decent politics could aim at. The republican tradition, at its sharpest in Pettit, has since argued that this dichotomy misses the phenomenon that republicans have always cared about most. There is a third thing: liberty as *non-domination*. To be free, on this account, is not merely to be uninterfered with. It is to be not subject to the arbitrary will of another.

The example Pettit likes to use is the kind master. A slave whose master has decided, for now, not to beat her is not interfered with. If interference is all we care about, she is free. But she is not free, and we all know she is not free, because she lives at her master's pleasure. The master could change his mind tomorrow. The very structure of her life is that her well-being depends on someone else's discretion. The republican claim is that this structure is itself the unfreedom, independently of whether the master happens to be exercising it harshly in any given week. The liberty republicans care about is not the absence of actual interference. It is the absence of the *standing capacity* of another to interfere at will.

Once you have the concept you see it everywhere. The worker whose employer will not fire her this month but could, for any reason or none, fire her next month, is not living at her own pleasure; she is living at his. The tenant whose landlord has raised the rent by ten percent and could raise it by a hundred is not negotiating, she is being permitted. The citizen whose rights exist because the regime currently finds it convenient to let them exist is not a rights-holder but a beneficiary. Each of these persons, in the strict Berlinian sense, may be uninterfered with at the moment. Each is also, in the older sense that republicans have preserved, unfree.

We take both halves of this argument seriously, and we build on both. The classical liberal guarantees — freedom of speech, freedom of conscience, property, due process, equal standing before the law — are protections against interference, and they are indispensable. Any regime that hopes to produce citizens has to secure them. But these guarantees do not, by themselves, protect against domination. A society in which the law on the books respects every right but in which an employer can fire you for your politics, a platform can deplatform you for its mood, a prosecutor can ruin you with the process itself, an administrative agency can extract your compliance through the cost of noncompliance — such a society has not achieved freedom simply because, on any given day, most of these powers are held in reserve. The republican supplement is precisely the set of institutions and habits that prevent the arbitrary hand from falling in the first place: the dispersion of power across branches and levels of government, the independence of associations from both state and market, the practices of deliberative self-government that give ordinary persons a voice in the conditions that bind them, the culture of civic equality that makes it unthinkable to treat a fellow citizen as a subject.

A usable account of freedom, we are saying, needs both. Negative liberty without the republican supplement becomes the freedom of the formally uninterfered-with to be informally dominated. Republican liberty without the classical liberal guarantees becomes the freedom of the self-governing majority to override the conscience of the minority in the name of virtue. Either, alone, is a half-answer. Together they are close to a whole one. The citizen we are describing is a person whose rights protect her from interference and whose polity, through its institutions and its culture, protects her from domination. Both jobs have to be done. A politics that specializes in one and neglects the other will fail at both eventually, because the neglected half will be invaded by the very forces the attended half was trying to hold back.

***

The third commitment is where we have to say the hardest thing. It is the book's answer to the quarrel between Rawls and Sandel, which is to say to the central philosophical argument of the last half-century of Anglophone political theory.

The polity should be neutral about ways of life, but it should *not* be neutral about the conditions that make persons capable of choosing a way of life.

Let us unfold this carefully, because it is easy to hear the first clause and assume the second and easy to hear the second and lose the first. The liberal tradition is right that the state must not impose a particular conception of the good on its citizens. The state that decides for its citizens which religion to hold, which kind of family to form, which aesthetic and moral ideals to pursue — this state is a tyranny, regardless of whether the conception of the good it is imposing happens to be correct. The dignity of a person requires the latitude to work out for herself, in the company of those she loves and trusts, the question of how to live. The state should treat her as a being entitled to pursue her own answer to that question. Classical liberalism is not wrong about this. It is the deepest moral insight the modern political tradition has produced.

But the polity — and we mean here not only the state, but the whole texture of public life, the schools and workplaces and associations and cultural institutions that together compose a people's common world — cannot be indifferent to whether its members are *capable* of such pursuit. The formation of persons capable of free and reasoned choice is not a private hobby. It is a common good, and the communities and institutions that accomplish it are performing a public function regardless of whether they are explicitly public bodies. A child who has not been taught to read cannot choose her own way of life in any meaningful sense, because she cannot reach the materials out of which a way of life is built. An adult who has never watched anyone deliberate with her about something that mattered cannot deliberate well herself, because deliberation is a practice learned by apprenticeship. A person who has never belonged to an association in which she was accountable to others and in which others were accountable to her has not acquired the disposition that membership in any voluntary community requires.

We are saying, in the old idiom, that liberty has conditions. We are saying, in a more contemporary idiom, that *capability* is prior to *choice* in the order of political concern. We are saying, in the idiom of this book, that a polity has to attend to the formation of its members, not because it has decided on their behalf what kind of life they should live, but because it has not, and because only a being so formed is in a position to answer the question for herself.

The metaphor that comes naturally here is the farmer's. A farmer who refuses to cultivate his fields on the ground that he does not want to impose wheat on his soil has not avoided imposition; he has merely imposed neglect, and his fields will grow weeds and nothing else. A polity that refuses to attend to the formation of citizens on the ground that it does not want to impose a conception of the good has made the same mistake. The fields do not stop growing. The question is only what they grow. A polity that lets its schools decay, its associations wither, its public culture be colonized by the shallowest forms of commerce, its deliberative institutions turn into theater — such a polity has not preserved neutrality. It has abdicated. And what grows in the abandoned ground is not the free and reasoned pursuit of a chosen life. It is the loneliness and susceptibility Arendt and Hoffer described, which is the raw material of the tribe.

So we commit to a view that the Rawlsian will find too thick and the Sandelian will find too thin. The state, on most questions of how to live, should be neutral. It should not tell its citizens which god to worship or which art to love or which vocation to choose. But the polity as a whole, and the state within its proper sphere, should be deeply engaged in the question of whether citizens have what they need to choose well. Education, a public culture of reasoned argument, the preservation of associational life, the access to meaningful work, the preservation of spaces in which ordinary persons can practice self-government at the scales at which they live — these are the material of freedom, not optional decorations added after the freedom has been secured. Classical liberalism has always depended on these goods. It has usually been better at consuming them than at replenishing them. We are saying: name the dependency. Fund the dependency. Treat the dependency as what it is, which is a public matter in the strict sense.

***

The fourth commitment is the one we have been hinting at for six chapters and it is the one most often misheard. We mean to say something specific about virtue.

Virtue has come down to us, in American public life, as a deeply contaminated word. It connotes moralism, censoriousness, sexual hypocrisy, and the particular voice of the person who tells other people how to live. This is not what the word meant in the republican tradition, and it is not what we mean by it here. Virtue, in the older sense the tradition preserves, is the *disposition to act well in the role one is occupying*. It is not a set of private feelings about the good. It is not a bedroom inspectorate. It is not a uniform to be enforced on the neighbors. It is a trained capacity for doing a particular kind of thing well, where the kind of thing is given by the role.

A doctor has virtues of a doctor. They are not the same as the virtues of a soldier, or a parent, or a juror, or a legislator. A juror who cannot subordinate her private sympathies to the requirements of the role is not exhibiting authenticity; she is failing to be a juror. A legislator who treats her seat as a platform for personal brand-building rather than as a constraint on how she may act is not expressing her true self; she is cheating on the institution she swore to serve. A doctor who prescribes what the patient asks for rather than what the patient needs is not respecting autonomy; she is abandoning her craft. In each case there is a role with internal standards, a practice that has been developed over generations of people who have done the work well and poorly and learned from both, and the virtue is the acquired disposition to meet those standards.

This is the tradition Alasdair MacIntyre tried to recover when he wrote *After Virtue*, and it is the tradition Yuval Levin is writing in when he asks, of every institution, whether it is still a *mold* that forms the character of those who pass through it or whether it has become a *platform* on which they perform. We will argue at length in Volume III that institutional renewal, properly understood, is virtue ethics extended to modern life. We will argue that the doctor who has let the practice of medicine form her, the teacher who has let the practice of teaching form him, the reporter who has let the ethics of reporting form her, the legislator who has let the constraints of office form her — each of these is doing something more important than it looks. Each is keeping alive a practice whose death would mean the loss of a particular kind of good thing that a society cannot produce by any other means.

None of this is moralism. No one in the tradition we are drawing on is proposing that the state jail bad doctors, shun rude legislators, or publish a list of citizens who have fallen short. The practice of virtue is internal to the role and is maintained by the community of other practitioners and by the self-respect of the person occupying the role. That is the whole point. What the republican tradition adds to the picture is that one of the roles everyone occupies — whether they have noticed or not — is the role of *citizen*, and that this role, like any other, has standards internal to it, which a person either meets or does not. You cannot be a good citizen merely by feeling that you are one. You can be a good citizen only by doing the things the role requires: attending to public matters, showing up, reasoning honestly with people who disagree with you, keeping your promises to institutions that are not under your control, recognizing that others in the polity are your equals whether you like them or not. These are not private virtues. They are the work of the role. A citizenry that has lost them has not lost its moral fiber in some metaphorical sense; it has lost a specific practical capacity on which the polity depends.

***

With the four commitments in view, let us say directly where this puts us in the arguments of the preceding chapters.

To the post-liberals we say the following. Yes, modern liberal societies have hollowed out the intermediate institutions — the families, congregations, neighborhoods, unions, lodges, clubs, local governments, and voluntary associations that used to stand between the individual and the nation-state. Yes, this hollowing has produced a characteristic modern pathology of lonely, susceptible persons and mass movements that feed on them. Yes, the bracketing of the good, pressed far enough, has cost us a shared moral vocabulary without which citizens cannot even tell each other what they want. On all of this, you are correct, and the diagnosis is worth taking seriously.

But the prescription is the catastrophe. The response to hollowed intermediate institutions is not to replace the liberal rights with a substantive regime that will fill the hollow with the preferred content of whoever has won the argument. It is to recover the civic-republican supplement that the American order at its best had, has partly lost, and can still, with work, rebuild. Rights without virtue is the procedural republic Sandel diagnosed: a machine for managing conflicts among strangers with no common life. Virtue without rights, however, is the illiberal republic, and history has been unkind to it in every century in which it has been tried. It ends, when it ends badly, in the persecution of the person whose conscience the majority has decided not to respect. It ends, when it ends ordinarily, in the slow suffocation of the minority, the dissenter, the oddball, and the reformer — the very figures without which a polity goes stupid and then corrupt. We do not want this. No one should want it. A usable answer preserves the rights *and* rebuilds the virtues. Both are required; neither is optional.

To the communitarians we say the following. Yes, the self is encumbered. Yes, the liberal picture of the chooser behind the veil is a philosophical abstraction that has never corresponded to any actually existing person. Yes, persons who are encouraged to think of themselves as uniquely self-authored become persons who find themselves unable to explain their lives to themselves or anyone else. You are right about this, and the post-Rawlsian generation in liberal theory is right to have taken the criticism on board.

But the encumbered self has also, historically, been a woman barred from the career her mind equipped her for, a gay man constrained to simulate a life he did not have, a black citizen in Jim Crow who was told by every institution around him that his place was beneath. The encumbrances from which liberal rights have rescued such persons were not incidental features of their communities; in many cases they were the defining features. To celebrate encumbrance as such, to treat the thickness of inherited community as an unqualified good, is to abandon the persons for whom thickness has been a cell. The answer cannot be to choose between encumbered community and individual rights. It has to be to insist on thick community *and* on the individual's rights to exit, criticize, and reform the community. The greatest moments of American civic-republican life, the ones we most want to recover, were not communities of conformity. They were the abolitionist meetings and the labor halls and the civil rights churches and the suffragist parlors — communities of *dissent* against the wider society, in which the solidarity of the members was precisely what gave each of them the standing to speak back to the country. That is the model. The thick community of equals, bound together by a common project and by mutual respect, in which the individual is not diminished by membership but enlarged, and in which membership itself is continuously open to argument. Anything less is either loneliness or conformity, and we do not want either.

To the classical liberals, including the ones we most admire, we say the following. Yes, the individual is an end in herself. Yes, the political community exists for its members and not the other way around. Yes, to treat any person merely as a means is the cardinal political wrong. We take none of this back. It is the moral floor of our position, and the place where the argument begins.

But the individual is not a brute fact. She is formed. The classical liberal tradition has always depended on civic resources it did not acknowledge it was consuming. Adam Smith knew this; he wrote *The Theory of Moral Sentiments* before he wrote *The Wealth of Nations* because he understood that a commercial society presupposes a moral substrate it does not itself produce. Tocqueville knew this; his description of American associations is the description of a country whose liberty is made possible by non-liberal forms of solidarity. Dewey knew this; his insistence on democracy as a way of life was an insistence that the formal institutions of liberal democracy are the tip of an iceberg whose submerged mass is civic practice. Jonathan Rauch has more recently called the submerged mass the *constitution of knowledge* — the set of norms, institutions, and practices by which a free society establishes what is true and what is false — and has noted that classical liberalism has been spending this constitution without noticing the draw-down. In each case the point is the same. Freedom is not self-sustaining. It has an ecology. The ecology has to be tended, or the freedom dies.

We are asking classical liberals to name this dependency. Once you have named it, you have to fund it. Not necessarily with money; often with attention, with participation, with the forbearance of treating civic life as a hobby. But to fund it somehow. A tradition that goes on celebrating its results while refusing to resupply its conditions is a tradition that is eating its own seed corn and will be surprised by the famine it is producing.

***

At this point we owe the reader a more precise account of the philosophical ground we are standing on. The synthesis we are sketching is not ours alone, and it is not new. The most worked-out academic version of it appears in Richard Dagger's *Civic Virtues*, published in 1997, and we take that book as the most important direct antecedent of the argument we are making.

Dagger's project, in brief, was to show that the standard opposition between liberalism and republicanism is misleading. Liberalism, Dagger argued, is committed to autonomy — to the capacity of persons to govern themselves, choose their ends, live their own lives. Republicanism is committed to civic virtue — to the disposition of citizens to act for the common good, to participate in self-government, to subordinate private interest to public concern where the role requires it. These are sometimes posed as rivals. Dagger's point was that, read carefully, they are not. Autonomy, properly understood, is not merely the absence of external constraint; it is a *capability*, and capabilities are developed. The conditions under which persons become autonomous include education, participation in shared decision-making, the experience of being treated as an equal by equals, the exercise of responsibility in roles that matter. These are civic conditions. They are produced and maintained by the practices republicans care about. The liberal ideal, pursued to its depth, requires the republican practice to realize it.

Conversely, civic virtue, properly understood, is not the dissolution of the self into the polis. It is the disposition of a free person to act, voluntarily and with reflection, for the common things. The civic virtues Dagger catalogues — civility, open-mindedness, honesty, a willingness to deliberate, a sense of fairness, a capacity to abide by procedures you did not choose — are virtues of *autonomous* persons. They presuppose the liberal self, just as the liberal self presupposes the civic conditions. The republican ideal, pursued to its depth, requires the liberal framework to make it compatible with dignity.

Dagger's term for the synthesis is *republican liberalism*, and we have borrowed it because it says what we want to say. A politics organized around the cultivation of the civic virtues and the protection of individual rights; a conception of liberty that includes both non-interference and non-domination; a conception of the citizen as someone who is formed by the polity and whose rights constrain what the polity may do to her.

Where we extend Dagger rather than simply adopting him is in what we do with the view next. Dagger's project is a philosophical reconciliation, aimed primarily at the academic literature in which the liberal-communitarian debate had been conducted for a generation. Our project is programmatic. We take the reconciliation as the premise, not the conclusion, and we devote the next two volumes to asking what it implies for understanding the particular pathologies of American life in the 2020s and for the practical work of repair. The diagnoses of Volume II and the practices of Volume III are what the philosophical position of Volume I is *for*. A reconciliation that stayed in the library would be a reconciliation that had not earned its name. We are trying to take it into the street.

***

We anticipate an objection, and we should answer it before we close. The objection is that our position is not a distinctive one at all. It is, the objector will say, merely liberalism plus a gesture toward civic-ness, the kind of thing a columnist gestures at when she wants to sound balanced without actually committing to anything. To this objection we reply that the position we have sketched has at least four distinct commitments, each of which would be denied by one or more of the traditions it is adjacent to, and each of which commits us to stances most of those traditions are reluctant to take.

We reject the bracketing of the good, but we do not reject it in the post-liberal direction. Certain goods — the goods of civic life itself, the conditions of personhood, the practices of self-government, the maintenance of deliberative institutions — must be publicly pursued, and a polity that pretends to neutrality about them is a polity that has already chosen. But most goods — the goods of religion, of culture, of intimate life, of personal pursuit — remain properly private, and a polity that colonizes them in the name of civic formation has become the thing the liberals rightly feared. The line is not always easy to draw, and we will spend parts of Volume III trying to draw it in specific cases. But the existence of a line is itself a distinctive commitment, and not the one the pure liberal or the pure post-liberal would endorse.

We reject the liberal presumption that rights alone will produce the citizens who maintain the rights. The rights are necessary. They are not sufficient. The production of citizens is a separate and larger task, partly public and partly private, partly political and partly cultural, and it requires its own attention and its own resources. A political order that treats the production of citizens as a byproduct of correctly specified procedures will be a political order that runs down its civic capital until none is left.

We reject the communitarian presumption that any thick community is better than a thin liberal one. Some thick communities are tyrannies. Some are cults. Some are, in the specific sense Arendt and Hoffer identified, mass movements. The question to ask of a thick community is not only whether it provides belonging but whether the belonging it provides is compatible with the dignity of its members. A community that requires the surrender of conscience, the suppression of dissent, the humiliation of the deviant, is not a remedy for atomization; it is its mirror image, the other face of the same pathology. We want community. We are not indifferent to what kind.

We reject the post-liberal presumption that liberalism is the disease rather than a stage in its own partial solution. We have tried throughout this book to take the post-liberal diagnosis seriously and to concede where it is right. But the prescription rests on a misreading of what liberalism has achieved. The moral recognition of the individual is not a mistake to be corrected. It is a permanent advance, one of the few things the modern West has gotten unambiguously right, and any politics that proposes to throw it over in the name of restored solidarity will produce neither solidarity nor a tolerable politics. The task is not to transcend liberalism. It is to complete it.

Together, these four rejections describe a distinct position. We are not a species of post-liberalism, and we are not a species of the managerial liberal consensus that post-liberalism is reacting against. We are not a species of communitarianism, and we are not a species of the libertarianism that communitarianism was right to criticize. What we are, we have said, is republican liberalism — the older American idea, recovered and made useable for a moment that has forgotten it.

***

Some of this will strike readers as hard, and where it is hard we mean to say so. We are asking the classical liberal to accept that the freedom he cherishes has conditions he has not been paying enough attention to. We are asking the republican to accept that the virtues she cherishes cannot be cultivated in a political order that permits the majority to override conscience. We are asking the communitarian to accept that not every thick community is better than a thin one, and that the rights to exit and to dissent are not concessions to liberalism but constitutive of any community worth belonging to. We are asking the post-liberal to accept that his diagnosis, which we have honored, points to a different prescription than the one he has reached for. Each of these asks is real. Each will feel, to the reader whose camp we are pressing on, like a demand to give up something dear.

We can only say, in response, that we do not think anything is actually being given up. We think the versions of each tradition we are urging are the traditions' own best versions, the ones their deepest voices arrive at when they have thought the matter through. We think classical liberalism is better off acknowledging its civic preconditions than pretending it does not have them. We think civic republicanism is better off acknowledging the rights-bearing individual than pretending she is a regrettable modern invention. We think communitarianism is better off arguing for the specific thick communities that are worth defending than for thickness as such. We think post-liberalism is better off as a supplement to liberalism than as a replacement for it.

And we think, more broadly, that the argument of Volume I has now done what it needed to do. The citizen is an individual who belongs. The person is prior to the state but not prior to the community. Liberty is both non-interference and non-domination. The polity is neutral about ways of life but not about the conditions of choice. Virtue is the disposition to act well in the role one is occupying, and citizenship is one such role, and a demanding one. These are the commitments we will carry forward.

Volume II will ask what has actually happened to the citizen in our time. It will work through the six partial diagnoses — the return of mass-movement psychology, the collapse of social capital, the decay of institutions, the polarization findings, the platform public sphere, the tyranny of merit — and argue that they are not rival explanations but aspects of a single phenomenon, the phenomenon Arendt and Hoffer named in 1951. The argument of Volume II will make sense only to readers who have absorbed the argument of Volume I, because a diagnosis is intelligible only against a picture of health.

Volume III will ask what can be done, at the scales at which any of us actually live. It will draw on the empirical literature on depolarization, on the history of successful civic revivals, and on the practices — personal, institutional, and political — that have been shown to work. It will be a practical book, organized around things that can be begun this week at no cost. But its practicality will be intelligible only against the philosophical picture this volume has tried to lay out, because the question of what to do depends on the question of what we are trying to make.

What we are trying to make is the citizen. Not the voter, not the consumer, not the partisan, not the follower, not the content producer or the content consumer. The citizen: the person who belongs to a polity of equals, who bears its burdens and partakes of its goods, who can address her fellows in the second person plural and be addressed back, who owes the polity and is owed by it, whose individuality is not compromised by her membership and whose membership is not cheapened by her individuality. This is an old figure, and we are aware that much of the reading public has been taught to regard her as a ghost. We do not think she is a ghost. We think she has been displaced, distracted, and discouraged, and that her recovery is not a matter of nostalgia but of the practical conditions of any tolerable common life. We have tried in this chapter to say who she is. In the volumes that follow, we try to say where she went, and what it will take to bring her back.


---

# VOLUME II
## Broken Systems in an Age of Abundance

*The diagnosis: what has actually happened to citizens, institutions, and the public sphere in the 21st century.*

---

# Chapter 8
## The Paradox of Plenty

Consider where we are.

A person reading this sentence in the spring of 2026 is living, by almost every measure human beings have ever used to evaluate the goodness of a time and place, inside the most fortunate society in the history of our species. We do not say this to be provocative or to pick a fight with the mood of the age. We say it because it is true, and because no honest diagnosis of what has happened to the American citizen can begin anywhere else. The paradox that organizes this volume depends on taking the goodness of our moment seriously. Without that first fact the second fact, which is much darker, becomes incomprehensible.

Start with the most basic indicator a political economy can produce, which is the number of human beings alive in conditions of severe material deprivation. A generation ago roughly two billion people lived in what the World Bank classifies as extreme poverty. That number today is closer to seven hundred million, in a world population substantially larger than the one that produced the earlier figure. More than a billion human beings, by the cleanest measure we have, have crossed a line that separates a life of subsistence terror from a life in which food and shelter are not in daily question. This is, in any plausible accounting of moral history, the largest single improvement in the material conditions of humankind that has ever been recorded. It happened in our lifetimes. Most of us were not paying attention.

The next indicator is the one that most closely tracks the deep sentimental meaning of the word *progress*, which is the rate at which children die. For most of recorded history, between a quarter and a third of all human beings born did not reach their fifth birthday. This was the condition of life under the pharaohs, under the Caesars, under the medieval kings, and in the villages our great-grandparents came from. Today, globally, that figure is under four percent and falling. In the wealthy countries it is under one percent. The small coffins that filled the graveyards of every premodern community we have records of have largely disappeared. The parents in our own neighborhoods mostly do not bury their children. A century and a half ago, they often did.

Violence, the other great shortener of human lives, has been in retreat over the long arc and in the recent arc together. Violent crime in most rich countries is a fraction of what it was in the late twentieth century; homicide rates in the United States, even after the pandemic-era surge and the subsequent correction, remain well below their early-1990s peak. The great killers of the twentieth century — famine, smallpox, the wars between great powers that burned entire generations — are either extinct or, for now, in remission. A person born in 1926 was four times as likely to die in a war as a person born in 2006. The most destructive disease ever encountered by the human species was eradicated by a coordinated public health effort in our parents' lifetimes and exists today only in two secure laboratories.

Information, the raw material of every human endeavor that is not purely physical, is free. A medieval monarch with the largest private library in Christendom had access to perhaps a thousand volumes, hand-copied, chained to desks, inaccessible to the unlettered. A twelve-year-old with a phone in a trailer park in Kentucky has access, at no marginal cost, to essentially the entire documentary inheritance of the species: the Vatican archives, the Library of Congress, the arXiv preprint server, the complete works of Shakespeare and Dostoevsky, every major religious canon in every major translation, every peer-reviewed paper ever published on a topic, the recorded performances of every major musician of the last century, and the living lectures of the best professors in the world. That this fact has been so thoroughly absorbed that it no longer strikes us as astonishing is itself a datum about how we live.

We could extend the list. Life expectancy at birth has nearly doubled over a century. The proportion of women who die in childbirth, once a standard feature of married life, is in the low single digits per hundred thousand in the developed world. Hunger in the wealthy countries is no longer a biological condition but a policy failure; the food stores are full. The burden of physical labor that crushed our ancestors' bodies has been mostly offloaded onto machines. The range of vocations open to an American woman today is incomparably greater than the range open to her grandmother. The range open to an American of color is incomparably greater than the range open to a generation earlier. Most of us, most days, can reach most of the people we love by tapping a glass rectangle. We all know this. We are all, in some sense, ungrateful for it.

***

And yet.

The citizen reading this paragraph is also living inside a society that, by every measurable indicator of civic and psychological health, is unraveling in ways that cannot easily be reconciled with the picture just drawn. The two pictures are both true. The paradox is not that one of them is false. The paradox is that they describe the same country.

The suicide rate among American adolescents has risen relentlessly for two decades, after falling for most of the twentieth century. The reversal is large enough and consistent enough across demographic groups that it cannot be explained away as measurement artifact. Rates of depression and anxiety among young people, on every instrument that has tracked them, have climbed over the same period, with a particularly sharp inflection in the early 2010s. In 2023 the United States Surgeon General, Vivek Murthy, issued an advisory declaring loneliness and social disconnection a public health crisis in their own right, with mortality effects comparable to smoking fifteen cigarettes a day. The fact that it was felt necessary to use the vocabulary of public health to describe the problem of Americans not having enough other Americans in their lives is itself a kind of indicator.

Trust in institutions has cratered over the past fifty years along a slope so steep that to see it laid out in a single table is to understand why it is hard to govern this country. The Gallup organization has been asking the same questions about institutional confidence in roughly the same language for half a century, which gives us a rare longitudinal view. The proportion of Americans expressing high confidence in doctors and the medical system has fallen from around eighty percent in the early 1970s to something closer to thirty-six percent in recent readings. Confidence in organized religion has fallen from around sixty-five percent to under forty. Confidence in the public schools has fallen from around sixty percent to under thirty. Confidence in Congress has fallen from about forty-two percent to around eleven — a number that, in any normal accounting of consent to be governed, should be treated as a constitutional emergency. Newspapers, banks, the criminal justice system, big business, the presidency: all have tracked the same descent, in most cases losing half or more of the public confidence they once enjoyed. The military is the lone major exception, which is an interesting datum we will return to in Chapter Eleven.

Civic behavior has tracked civic sentiment. The voluntary associations that Alexis de Tocqueville identified in the 1830s as the distinguishing feature of the American regime — the lodges, the unions, the parent-teacher organizations, the mainline congregations, the bowling leagues, the Lions and Elks and Rotarians — have hemorrhaged members across two generations. Americans sign fewer petitions than they used to. They attend fewer meetings. They know fewer of their neighbors' names. They have dinner in other people's houses less often. Robert Putnam's *Bowling Alone*, whose empirical architecture we will work through in Chapter Ten, is the chief document of this long subsidence, and the figures in its 2020 revision are, if anything, worse than the figures in the 2000 original.

Most alarming, because it points most directly at the political future, is the erosion of democratic commitment itself. The World Values Survey has for decades asked respondents in mature democracies how essential they regard living in a democracy to be. Roberto Stefan Foa and Yascha Mounk, working with this data, found a generational collapse that ought to stop anyone who reads it. Among Americans born in the 1930s, roughly seventy percent call it essential to live in a democracy. Among Americans born in the 1980s, the figure falls to roughly thirty percent. A regime cannot indefinitely run on the consent of a population that is losing the conviction that the regime is worth having. We are in the early decades of that loss, and the question of whether it can be reversed is the question under every other question in the chapters that follow.

The two largest political coalitions in the country now describe each other, in survey after survey, not as opponents whose policies are wrong but as existential threats whose victory would end the country. Large supermajorities of Americans in each party report that they would be upset if their child married someone from the other party, a figure that was in the single digits as recently as the 1960s. A non-trivial and rising share — the exact number varies with how the question is worded, and the methodology is contested — tells pollsters that violence against the other side would, in certain circumstances, be justified. The people telling us this are, mostly, our neighbors. They live in the same country we do. They drive to work on the same roads and shop at the same stores. They were not asked, in the Gallup studies of the 1960s, whether they would countenance political violence, because the question would have seemed absurd. It no longer seems absurd.

And in the space opened up by this loss of civic confidence, movements have flourished that define themselves precisely by their rejection of the regime that produced our abundance. On the illiberal right, a politics of grievance and restoration that treats the institutions of the liberal order as enemies to be captured or destroyed. On the illiberal left, a politics of revolutionary transformation that treats the same institutions as expressions of an irredeemable oppression. In the technological subcultures, a politics of secession — whether toward the seasteads or the neoreactionary enclaves or the accelerationist imaginary — that treats democratic life itself as a failed experiment to be superseded. In the identity movements, a politics of group sorting that treats deliberation across difference as a category error, since the groups do not share a world to deliberate about. The common feature across these movements, which otherwise share almost nothing, is the belief that the existing order is beyond ordinary repair and that its transformation requires measures ordinary politics would once have ruled out.

So: the wealthiest, safest, healthiest society in human history, producing a citizenry that is anxious, isolated, furious, and drawn in large numbers to political movements whose foundational claim is that the society is irredeemable. This is the paradox. It is not a rhetorical setup. It is a real puzzle that has to be explained if the word *citizen* in this book's title is to mean anything serious. Yuval Levin opens *A Time to Build* with it, and we open Volume II with it for the same reason. A diagnosis that does not sit honestly with both halves of this picture — the genuine abundance and the genuine disintegration — is not yet a diagnosis.

***

Before we offer our own account, we want to clear away the easy answers. The reason to spend a moment on this is not that the easy answers are stupid. It is that they are tempting. Any sufficiently serious diagnosis will, at first, feel harder than one of the available slogans, and the available slogans will continue to be offered to us, on cable and in campaign speeches and at family dinners, for as long as we have to think about any of this. It is worth saying, once and plainly, why each of them is insufficient.

The first easy answer is that we just need more growth. The country is anxious because the middle class is under economic pressure, and the middle class is under pressure because the growth rate has slowed from its postwar peak; accelerate the growth rate and the anxiety will ease. We have some sympathy with the economic component of this claim — the hollowing of work and the stagnation of wages for non-credentialed Americans is a real datum and a real grievance, and Michael Sandel's diagnosis in Chapter Fourteen takes it seriously. But as an explanation of the paradox of plenty, the growth answer is disqualified by its own terms. The paradox *is* that the growth has happened. Real per-capita GDP in the United States today is roughly double what it was in 1970, a period across which institutional trust collapsed, civic participation collapsed, and the suicide rate among the young reversed course and began to climb. A variable that has been moving in the right direction for half a century cannot be the primary cause of outcomes that have been moving in the wrong direction for the same half-century. The growth answer is addressing an earlier problem, not this one.

The second easy answer is that we just need more democracy. If people feel that the regime does not represent them, the cure is more voice, more participation, more direct expression of the popular will against the machinery that has sidelined it. This is the answer of an honorable progressive tradition and of a less honorable populist one, and it has been offered in one form or another for most of the last century. The difficulty is that the regimes experiencing the crisis most acutely are, by every ordinary measure, the most democratic regimes on earth. Affective polarization is sharpest in the United States, the United Kingdom, France, and the other long-standing Western democracies. The countries turning most decisively toward illiberal strongmen are doing so through their democratic machinery, not around it. If the problem were simply a deficit of democratic voice, the cure would be visible in the places where voice is loudest, and the pattern of evidence runs the other way. The democracy answer also, notoriously, begs the question of which democracy we mean. Deliberative democracy, plebiscitary democracy, identity-based democracy, and constitutional democracy have different relationships to our current disorder, and saying "more democracy" without specifying which is not yet an answer.

The third easy answer is that we just need to go back to the 1950s, or to some similarly gauzy earlier decade in which civic life was thicker and trust was higher and the young were less anxious and families were whole. There is a selective truth in the observation that some of the indicators were better seventy years ago. There is also, in the observation's usual form, a set of occlusions so severe that the comfort it offers is unearned. The America of 1955 was an America in which Black citizens lived under a legal apartheid that most white readers of this book have no living memory of, in which women's professional lives were circumscribed in ways that would be recognized today as actionable, in which gay Americans were routinely imprisoned for existing, in which the psychiatric diagnosis of homosexuality was still seven years from being written down in the DSM and twenty-five years from being quietly deleted. The civic thickness of the period was real; it was also purchased, in part, by the exclusion of whole categories of persons from civic life. Most of the readers of this book, ourselves included, would not actually want to live there. A diagnosis that depends on a return to a world we would not endorse if we saw it clearly is not a usable diagnosis. We have to build forward.

The fourth easy answer is that we just need more science. The scientific method, applied sufficiently to the human problems before us, will yield solutions in the way it has yielded vaccines and semiconductors. We find this the least persuasive of the easy answers, though it is the one most likely to be held by our most educated readers. Science has delivered, in our lifetimes, most of what it promised. It has not failed us. The crisis in front of us is not, in the relevant sense, scientific. Whether a person regards his neighbor as a fellow citizen or as an enemy is not a question that will be resolved by additional data about his neighbor. Whether a young person experiences her life as meaningful or as absurd is not a question the randomized controlled trial can answer, though there is useful social-scientific work to be done at the edges. The crisis is a crisis of meaning, membership, and the public life that frames both, and the tools that will matter most are older than the scientific revolution — moral, philosophical, institutional, practical. We will draw on the empirical literature throughout this volume, and heavily in Volume III, because where evidence can inform practice we intend to use it. But the thought that better measurement will resolve the paradox of plenty mistakes the kind of problem we are facing.

None of these easy answers is wholly without merit. Each of them has gotten some honest thinker somewhere, and some honest reader, most of the way to a real insight. Our claim is only that, taken alone, each of them fails the test of explaining the phenomenon we have to explain — the simultaneous, coincident, apparently contradictory increase in objective flourishing and subjective disintegration that is the American condition in the 2020s. We need a bigger map.

***

The rest of this volume is that map. Over the six chapters that follow, we will work through what we take to be the six most serious diagnoses of the contemporary crisis, each associated with a major contemporary thinker or tradition, each with real empirical weight, each capable of generating its own book and in many cases having done so. We want to introduce them now, one by one, so that when we return to them at length in their own chapters the shape of the whole argument is already in view.

The first diagnosis is the one this book takes most seriously, because it is the oldest and the one that connects all the others. Call it the mass-movement diagnosis. It was first articulated, with full clarity, by Eric Hoffer and Hannah Arendt, writing independently and publishing in the same year, 1951, in the shadow of the totalitarian catastrophes of the middle twentieth century. Their insight, roughly speaking, was that totalitarian movements do not recruit primarily from the politically engaged and the associationally thick. They recruit from the atomized — from the population of modern persons who have lost, or never had, a secure place in a world of other persons. Arendt's phrase was that loneliness is "the common ground for terror." Hoffer's was that the true believer is fleeing himself as much as he is pursuing a cause, and that he is interchangeable in his fundamental psychology across whatever ideological flag he happens to be marching under. The contemporary cult literature — Robert Lifton, Margaret Singer, Steven Hassan — has extended and operationalized this insight into a body of work on how individuals get captured by totalizing ideological formations and how, with difficulty, they get out. Chapter Nine will work this material through and apply it symmetrically to the movements of the contemporary left and right, to religious fundamentalisms and secular ones, to identity crusades and to technological eschatologies. The analysis is not polemical. It is structural. The conditions of modern life produce the kind of person who is susceptible to mass formations, and that susceptibility is the common property of everyone reading this book.

The second diagnosis is the social-capital diagnosis, associated above all with Robert Putnam. Chapter Ten will walk through the empirical architecture of *Bowling Alone* and its 2020 revision. The headline finding is that Americans participate in the institutions of common life — the unions, the clubs, the churches, the leagues, the political parties, the informal sociability of neighborhoods — at a fraction of the rates their grandparents did. Putnam's causal analysis attributes roughly half of the decline to generational turnover: a long civic cohort, formed by the Great Depression and the Second World War, aging out of the population and being replaced by cohorts with much weaker associational habits. Other drivers include suburbanization, the pressures of dual-earner households, and, centrally, the displacement of communal time by electronic entertainment, first television and now the smartphone. The distinction Putnam draws between bonding capital (inward, among people like ourselves) and bridging capital (outward, across lines of difference) is critical: both are eroding, but bridging capital is eroding faster, and the political consequences are severe, because it is the ties across difference that produce moderation. The social-capital diagnosis is necessary; we will argue in Chapter Ten that it is not, by itself, sufficient.

The third diagnosis is the institutional-decay diagnosis, whose sharpest recent statement is Yuval Levin's *A Time to Build*. Levin's move, which we find genuinely illuminating, is to distinguish two different ways a person can inhabit an institution. One can inhabit it as a *mold* — as a structure that forms character by imposing the constraints and disciplines of a role. One can inhabit it as a *platform* — as a stage on which to perform a personal brand before an external audience. A doctor, a journalist, a legislator, a pastor, and a professor all used to occupy their institutional roles primarily in the first mode and increasingly occupy them in the second. When the role-bearer is using the institution to project himself rather than allowing it to form him, the institution loses the capacity to form anyone, including its members. The collapse in public trust that we sketched earlier in this chapter is, on Levin's account, the predictable downstream consequence of an elite that has, across domains, converted its institutions from molds into platforms. Chapter Eleven takes this argument seriously, examines the military as an instructive exception, and asks what the re-moralization of institutional roles would concretely look like.

The fourth diagnosis is the polarization diagnosis, and it is in some ways the most empirically robust of the six. The finding at its center, developed by Shanto Iyengar, Lilliana Mason, Ezra Klein, and the researchers at More in Common, is that Americans have become affectively polarized — they increasingly dislike, distrust, and dehumanize members of the other political party — at a rate that significantly outruns any actual divergence in policy preference. Partisan identity has in many cases swallowed other identities, such that to belong to a party is to belong to a bundle of cultural, religious, geographic, and psychological commitments that may or may not follow the party's official platform. The "perception gap" finding — that each side believes the other side holds more extreme views than it actually does, and that the most politically engaged citizens have the *least* accurate perceptions — is among the most counterintuitive and important results in the literature. It implies that the remedy cannot be more political engagement of the usual kind, because the usual kind of political engagement appears to be part of the disease. Chapter Twelve examines this body of evidence, engages the contested literature on asymmetric and "diploma-divide" polarization honestly, and draws out what it implies about the moral shape of public disagreement.

The fifth diagnosis is the public-sphere diagnosis, which in one form or another has been made by Jürgen Habermas across six decades and is now being made, in different registers, by Chris Bail, Jonathan Haidt, and a growing empirical literature on the political effects of digital media. The claim is that the communicative space democracy requires — the space in which citizens reason together toward considered judgments, in something other than a shouting match — has been fragmented past the point of adequately performing that function. The popular form of this claim emphasizes echo chambers. We will argue, following Bail, that echo chambers are actually the less damaging part of the story.
