Two generations later, the same town had been split by the decline of its manufacturing base into two more or less separate worlds, one of which still had the old social infrastructure more or less intact and one of which had effectively lost it. The children of the first world still had coaches and mentors and youth-group leaders and college-educated parents and intact marriages; the children of the second world often had none of those things. Both groups of children lived within the same town limits and attended, at least nominally, the same public school. Their civic inheritance was not the same. The political implication is stark. When we speak, in the chapters ahead, about the rise of populist movements and the appeal of tribal politics, we should not be surprised that the movements and the tribalism have found their most fervent constituencies in communities where the associational infrastructure has most completely collapsed. Hoffer and Arendt would not have been surprised. The lonely communities are the ones available for capture.

***

We can now say, in the larger frame of this book, what Putnam's evidence is doing. The previous chapter made the philosophical case that mass movements recruit from the atomized — that the lonely, world-stripped individual, in Arendt's phrase, is the common ground for terror, and that Hoffer's true believer is fleeing a self that has nothing to hold onto. That case was made in the language of political philosophy and the psychology of mass formation, and it was made, chiefly, by two thinkers working in the aftermath of European catastrophe and writing in the shadow of Hitler and Stalin.

Putnam's evidence is what that case looks like when it is made in the language of survey instruments and longitudinal data about the United States at the end of the twentieth century. Americans have been losing, measurably, for decades, the associational life that was once the school of citizenship. They have been losing the specific kind of associational life — bridging, cross-cutting, durable, local, face-to-face — that thickens a pluralist democracy and makes factional identity bearable. They have been losing the confidants, the neighbors, the weeknight meetings, the weekend potlucks, the committees, the leagues, the halls. And the rise of mass formations in our politics, the rise of the tribal loyalties in which a great many Americans now experience their political identity, is not a coincidence with this loss. It is, at least in large part, its consequence. You cannot strip out the associational life that forms the citizen and expect the citizen to keep showing up. What shows up instead is the audience for the movement.

This is where we want to be careful about the scale of our claim. We are not saying that Putnam's findings explain everything, and neither is Putnam. Affective polarization has drivers of its own. The collapse of institutional trust has drivers of its own. The transformation of the public sphere by platform capitalism has drivers of its own. The hollowing-out of work and merit has drivers of its own. Each of the chapters that follow will take up one of those partial explanations in turn. What the social-capital literature provides is not the complete theory of how we got here. It is the empirical connective tissue that runs under the other explanations. It is the evidence that underneath the polarization, underneath the platform pathologies, underneath the distrust, underneath the populist surges, the associational ground has been thinning for sixty years, and that thinning is part of what makes the rest of it possible. A society with robust bridging ties can absorb a great deal of polarizing pressure without breaking. A society in which the bridging ties have frayed is a society in which the same pressure cracks things.

***

There is a way of telling this story that leaves the reader with nothing but despair, and we want to close the chapter by refusing that way. Putnam's 2020 follow-up volume, *The Upswing*, written with Shaylyn Romney Garrett, makes an argument we consider essential to the forward-looking half of this book and to which Volume III will return in detail. The argument is that the United States has been through something like this before.

In the decades before the turn of the twentieth century — the period we call, after Mark Twain, the Gilded Age — the country displayed a roster of pathologies that will be uncannily familiar to a contemporary reader. Inequality was high and rising. Political corruption was open and brazen. Trust in institutions was at a low ebb. Immigration and urbanization were pulling the country into new and unsettling configurations. The party system was rigid, polarized, and unresponsive to actual problems. Mass movements of various flavors — populist, nativist, utopian — surged across the landscape, each promising a total break with a present that was felt to be unendurable. Civic life was thin in the places that had been thickened by migration and commerce; the associational infrastructure that would later undergird the mid-twentieth-century boom had not yet been built.

What followed, Putnam and Garrett document, was a long, slow, bottom-up civic revival. It began not in Washington but in settlement houses and women's clubs and Chautauqua tents, in the founding of the Rotary and the Boy Scouts and the Kiwanis, in the professionalization of medicine and law and journalism, in the building of the modern university and the modern public library and the modern city park. It was followed, and reinforced, by the political reforms we associate with the Progressive Era: civil service, direct election of senators, the secret ballot, the regulatory state. It took decades. It was uneven and incomplete. It carried forward, because it had to, many of the injustices of the society that produced it. But it happened. The country pulled itself, over forty years, out of a trough that had looked from the inside like a terminal decline, and it did so through a million small civic acts that were, most of them, not undertaken as grand political projects but as particular responses to particular local conditions.

*The Upswing* is the single most hopeful book on our desk as we write this, and we will return to it at length in Volume III. It is hopeful not because it promises that everything will be fine — Putnam and Garrett do not make that promise, and we will not either — but because it demonstrates that the shape of the curve we are currently on is not a new shape in American history, and that the last time the country was on a similar curve, it climbed out. The climb was not accomplished by any one generation, any one movement, any one piece of legislation, or any one charismatic leader. It was accomplished by an accumulation of people, over a very long time, deciding that the civic world they had been handed was not good enough and choosing to build a better one, one association at a time.

***

We want to close with an honest accounting of where the Putnam tradition does and does not get us, because readers who carry the argument of this chapter into the rest of the book should know its limits as well as its strengths.

The first limit is that Putnam's own prescriptions, in his own writing, tend to be modest, and they have been criticized, not unfairly, as insufficient to the scale of the problem he documents. The closing chapters of *Bowling Alone* offer a list of suggestions — civic education, youth engagement, workplace reform, religious revival, arts and culture, better urban design — that are unobjectionable and, in our view, largely correct, but that do not amount to a program capable of reversing a sixty-year generational trend. Putnam is a careful social scientist, and the carefulness that makes his diagnosis trustworthy makes his prescriptions cautious. We will need more than caution in Volume III.

The second limit is that social-capital language, in less careful hands than Putnam's, can become anodyne. It can be mobilized to dress up nostalgia as policy — to advocate for Things As They Were as though the mid-century associational world were the only possible civic world, and as though its injustices were a price worth quietly paying for its benefits. That is not Putnam's position, but it is a position that is sometimes taken in his name. Any serious program of civic renewal has to reckon with the fact that we cannot and should not reconstruct the particular associational world of 1955. The world we build has to be a world compatible with the freedoms and equalities we have gained in the decades since. The tradition represented by Putnam does not supply, on its own, the philosophical account of what should and should not be carried forward.

The third and deepest limit is that the social-capital tradition is empirical and pragmatic, and it has no built-in philosophical account of why civic life matters beyond its instrumental benefits. *Bowling Alone* tells us, in great and convincing detail, that civic participation correlates with longer lives, better-educated children, higher institutional trust, and lower political extremism. These are real goods and they are worth naming. But they do not, by themselves, tell us why a person should care about public life if she happens to be lucky enough to be healthy, well-educated, trusting, and moderate without it. They do not tell us why participation in a common world is constitutive of a full human life rather than merely conducive to a long one. For that argument, we have to go back to the thinkers we engaged in the previous chapter and in the volume before it — to Arendt's claim that the space of appearance is where a person becomes real to others and thus to herself, to Hoffer's sobering insight that the lonely self, left to itself, will not remain a self for long, and to the older republican tradition in which the citizen is not a person who has been persuaded to do civic things but a person whose personhood is finished by doing them.

The Putnam evidence is, in other words, the empirical floor of the diagnosis this book offers. It is not the whole of the diagnosis, and it is not the philosophical account that makes the diagnosis matter. It is the floor. Without the floor, nothing else stands. With the floor, we can begin to build what comes next.

Americans are, on the evidence of the best longitudinal research we have, measurably more isolated, measurably less associationally engaged, and measurably more politically tribal than they were sixty years ago. The trend has continued through the most recent data we have. The bridging ties that once moderated factional identity have frayed more than the bonding ties that intensify it, with predictable consequences for our politics. The decline has been much more severe at the bottom of our class structure than at the top, with predictable consequences for whose grievances dominate our public life. And nothing about this state of affairs is a metaphor. It is a set of measured facts about the actual lives of actual people, and any response to the present crisis that does not begin with those facts is a response to something other than what is happening.

We are, as the title of this chapter suggests, still bowling alone. The bowling is worse than it was when Putnam first named it. And the question that Volume III will have to answer — the question that any honest reader of this chapter has a right to press on us — is what, concretely, is to be done. We will take up that question in its own time. For now, we stay with the picture the data draw. It is a picture of a country that has been losing, quietly, for a very long time, the ordinary social infrastructure that made citizenship possible. That infrastructure is not gone. It is thinned. It can be thickened again. The Americans who thickened it the last time did not know, at the time they began, that they were building what would become the civic world of the twentieth century. They were just doing the next thing in front of them. That is probably how it will have to begin again.


---

# Chapter 11
## Platforms Instead of Molds

For about half a century now, Americans have been losing faith in their institutions. The Gallup numbers are the most familiar version of the story and they are, by any standard, dramatic. Confidence in doctors and hospitals has slid from around eighty percent in the early 1970s to thirty-six percent by the late 2010s. Confidence in organized religion has fallen from sixty-five percent to thirty-eight. The public schools have gone from sixty percent to twenty-nine. The press has collapsed in much the same way; Congress has collapsed further, from forty-two percent to the low double digits and sometimes lower. Nothing about the curve is ambiguous. Whatever else is true about American life in this period, the people who live inside it no longer believe in the arrangements that are supposed to hold it together.

The standard explanations for this are external. They locate the cause of the collapse outside the institution in question: bad leaders, bad press coverage, bad actors. The doctors are caught in a documentary; the church is caught in a scandal; the newspaper is caught in a lie; the senator is caught on tape. The collapse is presented, in effect, as a series of discoveries — as a public slowly learning what had been hidden from it. On this account the remedy is to fix whichever part of the institution produced the discovery and to reassure the public that the rest of the machine is still working.

There is some truth in this. Scandals are real. Bad leaders are real. Media that magnify both are real. But the explanation is too small for the phenomenon it is trying to explain. Fifty years of nearly continuous decline across almost every major institution in American life is not a story about the occasional rotten apple. Something about the barrel is different. And the most useful recent account of how the barrel is different is the one given by Yuval Levin in *A Time to Build*, published in 2020 — an argument that has become, for us and for a growing number of writers who share our worry about the citizen, the most productive way into the institutional problem.

Levin's central move is to reframe the question. The problem of institutional decay, he argues, is not primarily a problem of how institutions appear from outside. It is a problem of how the people inside the institutions behave. The loss of trust is not a perceptual accident or a narrative failure or a public-relations breakdown. It is the accurate report of an accurate change. The institutions are being used differently than they used to be, and they have lost, in the using, the thing that made them trustworthy in the first place.

To describe that change Levin offers a pair of terms that has become, in the few years since, something close to a piece of common vocabulary among serious readers of institutions. An institution can be used as a *mold* or as a *platform*. The distinction is simple and, once noticed, nearly everywhere.

***

An institution is a mold when it forms the people who pass through it. The medical school forms a doctor. The law firm forms a lawyer. The parish church forms a Christian and forms, over time, a pastor. The newspaper forms a reporter. The committee, the campus, the bar, the academy, the regiment, the seminary — each is a set of norms and habits and constraints and duties, applied over time, to a person who consents in some serious way to being shaped by them. The medical student learns to think diagnostically because her professors think diagnostically in front of her and require her to do the same. The young associate learns to write a brief by writing one badly and having it corrected by a partner who has written three thousand of them. The seminarian learns to preach not primarily from a book on preaching but from standing, week after week, in a congregation whose collective life he is being asked to speak into. The institution pours a set of expectations around a person; the expectations harden; the person comes out recognizable as an example of the kind the institution was trying to produce. Not identical to every other example — molds have some give — but recognizable. You meet a doctor and you know, within a minute or two, that a doctor is what you are meeting. That is the mold at work.

An institution is a platform, by contrast, when the person uses it to project herself. On the platform model the doctor acquires the medical degree and then uses the credential to build a wellness brand, or a podcast, or a supplement line, or a public profile on which her actual clinical practice is a small and shrinking component. The legislator arrives in Washington and uses the committee seat as a studio, the hearing as a stage, the vote as a prop in a content strategy. The professor accumulates a tenure case and then uses the university as a platform from which to issue commentary, appear on cable, collect speaking fees, and accrete followers. The pastor treats the pulpit as a venue for the cultivation of an authenticity brand, and the congregation as an audience. In each case the direction of formation has reversed. The institution is no longer shaping the person; the person is using the institution to shape her audience.

The distinction is not a caricature. Almost everyone who lives inside one of these institutions feels both pulls and in some proportion gives in to both. Few of us are the pure mold-submissive and few of us are the pure platform-performer. The question is which of the two logics is organizing the institution's work — which one the younger members are catching from their elders, which one the incentives point toward, which one the reward system rewards. And the honest answer, across an extraordinary range of American institutions, is that over the last several decades the platform logic has been winning.

***

Why does it matter? One can imagine a perfectly reasonable reply: so what if the doctor has a podcast on the side. So what if the senator makes good clips. This is the age of personal branding. People have always had reputations; now they have reputations at scale. Let them.

It matters because institutions used as platforms lose the capacity to form anyone. That is the whole of the argument, and it is worth sitting with. A medical school whose faculty members primarily use their positions to build personal followings does not produce the same kind of doctor that a medical school whose faculty members primarily use their positions to teach medicine produces. The difference does not lie in the curriculum, or not only there. It lies in what the student is watching. She is watching the grown-ups. She is learning, from what they do rather than from what they say, what it means to be a doctor. If what they do is perform, then she learns that medicine is a performance. If what they do is submit themselves to the constraints of the role and do the boring, demanding, often unrewarded work of diagnosis and care, she learns that medicine is a craft and that she is apprenticed to it. The same is true everywhere. Institutions are not principally teaching mechanisms; they are principally imitative communities. People inside them become, over time, the kind of people the senior members already are.

This is why the public's loss of trust is not a misperception. The doctor who treats her practice as a personal brand cannot be trusted as a doctor in the way her grandmother's doctor could, because the part of her attention that used to be occupied by the patient in front of her is now occupied by the audience watching her treat the patient in front of her. The legislator who treats his seat as a performance vehicle cannot be trusted as a legislator, because the performance logic is not compatible with the quiet, grinding, compromise-heavy work of actually writing a law that functions. The journalist who tweets his feelings in real time and whose personal following is larger than his paper's circulation cannot be trusted to report, because the economy of his attention has shifted away from the things the mold of journalism exists to produce — verification, fairness, thoroughness, the patient refusal to say more than you know — and toward the things the platform rewards, which is the opposite of those things. The public is not being paranoid. The institutions have, in real ways, changed their output, and the public can feel it.

Trust collapses, then, not mostly because people are lying, though some are. Trust collapses because people are using the institutions in a way that makes the institutions unworthy of trust. This is an entirely different diagnosis than the scandal-and-media-coverage explanation. It does not require a villain. It does not require a conspiracy. It simply requires that, on the inside of the institution, the center of gravity has shifted from the mold to the platform, and that the public — which deals with the outputs — has correctly picked up on the shift.

***

Against this general pattern, one American institution continues to hold something like its historic level of public confidence. The military has sagged a little from its peak but remains, alone among major institutions, broadly trusted across the parties and the generations. It is worth asking why, because the answer is instructive — and because the answer is not, we should be clear at the outset, that the military is simply good and the other institutions simply bad.

The military remains, to an unusual degree in contemporary American life, an institution-as-mold. A young person who joins enters a formative structure that is essentially uninterested in her as a brand. Her uniform is identical to the uniforms of a million others. Her title comes from her role. Her habits are corrected by her superiors. She is socialized, over years, into a set of norms — chain of command, duty, mission, the subordination of personal ambition to a collective task — that are explicitly not her own invention and are explicitly not there to serve her self-expression. The institution sanctions, often heavily, attempts to use the role for personal brand-building; the officer who goes on television without authorization to advance her own profile is not promoted, she is disciplined. Whatever else one thinks about the contemporary American military, its internal culture still operates on the logic of formation. It forms people. It is recognizable as an institution that does that.

This is not a blanket endorsement. We are not writing a paean to the armed forces. The military has its own pathologies — covered up, recurring, sometimes grave. Its strategic record in the last three decades is genuinely contested and the book takes no side in that contest here. Its proper role in the polity is a real question for a democratic republic and one that our forebears, from Washington to Eisenhower, were right to worry about. The unusual trust Americans place in the military cuts in more than one direction; it can become the kind of trust that bypasses democratic scrutiny rather than grounds it, and we are wary of that. None of those complications goes away. What we are asking of the reader is something narrower. The military is a useful datum because it is the large American institution in which the mold logic is most visibly still operating, and the public's response to it is the response the public tends to give to institutions that still form the people inside them. The lesson is not "be the military." The lesson is that when an institution forms its members — when it imposes roles and sanctions deviation from them and subordinates the personal to the mission — the public notices, and extends to the institution something the public no longer extends to institutions that have abandoned that work. The trust does not come from charisma or from marketing. It comes from formation.

***

The platform pathology shows up differently in different institutions but the underlying shape is the same. Consider the congressional committee. The committee system is, among other things, the core formative apparatus of the modern American Congress. It is where junior members learn to legislate, where they apprentice under senior members, where substantive disagreement gets hammered into compromise language, where policy gets built by people who have done nothing else for a long time. This is slow work and boring work and mostly invisible work. Until the last couple of decades the mold of Congress — living in Washington part of the week, raising one's children alongside the children of the other party's members, being on committees for years rather than months, having to face the same colleagues tomorrow whose vote you need on something else — bore down on the member in the specific way an institution bears down, and the member was in some degree shaped by it.

The modern pathology of Congress is that its members increasingly do not experience the committee this way. The hearing is not primarily an occasion for the collection of testimony and the clarification of a legislative problem; it is an occasion for the production of clips. A question is not primarily designed to elicit information from the witness; it is designed, often quite precisely, to produce thirty seconds of footage that will travel. The floor speech is not for the record; the record is for the speech. One sees this even in the cadence, the camera placement, the moment at which a member holds up a prop. None of this is because the members are worse people than their predecessors, and we want to be careful to insist on that. Most of them, in our experience, entered public life for recognizable reasons and retain recognizable convictions. The point is structural. The institution no longer forms them to do the work it was designed to do, because the institution no longer bears down on them in the old way. They are, increasingly, performing on a platform that happens to be called Congress.

Consider the university. The tenure-track professor is, in principle, being formed by a guild. She enters as a graduate student. She is trained in methods and in the habits of a discipline. She is reviewed by peers. She is gradually granted authority over other students, then over her own junior colleagues, then over the direction of the department, then over the governance of the institution. Each of these steps is, in its classical form, a mold; each makes certain kinds of demands on her and imposes certain kinds of constraints. The modern pathology is that a growing share of her professional reward has migrated away from that guild and toward a public audience. Her attention is pulled toward the op-ed, the substack, the keynote, the trade book, the cable commentary, the platform following. These are not illegitimate activities; many fine scholars have done some of them well. The question is which is primary. If the audience of strangers is primary, then the department, the committee, the graduate seminar, the faculty meeting, the undergraduate lecture, and the long slog of reading one's students' drafts become costs rather than the core of the vocation. They become the inconvenient overhead of the platform. And students catch this. A university in which the teachers experience teaching as overhead produces students who experience their degree as overhead — not a formation but a credential, not a community of inquiry but a checkpoint on the way to something they can do elsewhere. The university is being used, by both sides of the desk, as a platform; and its capacity to form anyone, teacher or student, erodes accordingly.

Consider the church and the religious community. Here the platform pathology acquires a specifically modern cast, because the thing being performed is often, precisely, authenticity — which is to say, the quality that cannot be performed. A pastor who cultivates his own brand builds a congregation around a persona, which then becomes the product the congregation is there to consume. The congregation is no longer a body knit by worship and sacrament and mutual obligation; it is an audience, and when the persona fails — as personas, in the end, all do — the community that gathered around it has no structure independent of the failure. The older understanding of the pastor, which survives in pockets of American religious life and is visible still in many traditions, is almost exactly the opposite. The pastor is a servant of an institution older than he is; he submits to its liturgy, its canon, its discipline, its elders; his personality is something he subordinates, gladly, to the role he has accepted. He is formed by the pulpit more than he uses the pulpit to form an image of himself. The congregations led by such figures are visibly different from the congregations led by personal brands, and the difference is not a matter of style; it is a matter of whether there is, in the room, anyone who is being formed.

Consider journalism. The trade has always had its stars and always will, and this is not an argument against reputation. It is an argument about what the reputation is for. The institutional mold of journalism consists in a set of boring, demanding habits that journalists used to absorb from older journalists and from the constraints of working at a paper. You verify. You give the other side a chance to respond. You try not to say more than you know. You are willing to be wrong in the first draft in order to be right in the final one. You accept that on any given day your story will be turned down or held by an editor whose judgment you respect and whose authority you acknowledge. These habits are the craft. The modern pathology is that they have become inconvenient to the platform. The reporter whose personal following is larger than her paper's circulation, whose career advances with her audience rather than with her newsroom, whose livelihood increasingly depends on the speed and the heat with which she can metabolize public events into posts — this reporter has, whether she wishes to or not, absorbed the platform's logic. The mold has been replaced by a stage. And the public, which once trusted journalism in numbers now difficult to imagine, correctly observes that the output is different.

And consider, finally, ordinary professional life. The platform logic has migrated from the unusual professions to all of them, along the wires of LinkedIn and its analogues. The employee is no longer primarily formed by her firm; she performs on it. Her profile is a product. Her tenure at a company is a chapter in a personal narrative rather than an apprenticeship inside a community of practice. She is encouraged, by the ambient norms of professional life, to treat every role as content and every colleague as a potential node in a network. There are real virtues in this — mobility, in particular, is a liberation from the older, constraining loyalties of firm and town — and we do not wish to romanticize the version that came before. But there is a cost, and the cost is mostly hidden, and the cost is that the mold of even the ordinary vocation has weakened. People bring to their work, increasingly, the attitude of the performer who is passing through, and they bring to their communities, increasingly, the same attitude. A society of performers has a hard time staffing the roles that require formation.

***

The causal story Levin tells has two layers. The first is cultural: the master value of modern life has shifted, over the course of the last century, from something like duty to something like self-expression. This is a long arc with many authors, from Charles Taylor's account of the ethics of authenticity to Christopher Lasch's bleaker rendition in *The Culture of Narcissism* and the late Robert Bellah's *Habits of the Heart*. However one reads the arc, the upshot for our purposes is that a civilization that regards self-expression as the highest good is going to be a civilization in which institutions-as-molds feel, on the inside, like violations of the self. The young associate's formation at the hands of the partner will be experienced as an insult rather than a gift. The junior member's apprenticeship to the senior member will be experienced as a denial of her voice. The seminarian's submission to the liturgy will be experienced as inauthentic. In each case the very thing the mold is designed to do — the quiet, durable shaping of a self to the demands of a role — runs against the grain of a culture that treats the unshaped self as the valuable one.

The second layer is institutional. The incentive structures of modern media, politics, and higher education have changed in ways that make platform behavior rational and mold behavior costly. The committee chair who refuses to play for clips loses to the colleague who does not. The tenured professor who devotes himself entirely to teaching and the department loses, in salary and in status, to the colleague who trades on a public reputation. The pastor who attends to the hundred elderly members of his aging congregation is outcompeted, in the attention of his denomination and the size of his flock, by the pastor building a personal brand. The reporter who spends three months on a story and kills it because she cannot verify it loses, in career terms, to the colleague who publishes the half-story and the subsequent apology. None of this is inevitable. Other incentive structures are possible, and institutions that deliberately design against the platform dynamic — as some still do — get different outputs. But the drift is real, and it is, at the margin, decisive.

We accept both of Levin's layers. We want to add a third, because we think the argument of this book asks for it.

The platform pathology is also, and we think inextricably, a symptom of the atomization we described in the last two chapters. A person who belongs to fewer thick associations — fewer families, fewer neighborhoods, fewer congregations, fewer guilds, fewer clubs — experiences every institution she encounters as a potential stage, because she has no audience for her personhood anywhere else. In a life thick with non-performative relationships, the office is one place among many, and the impulse to perform on it can be absorbed by the fact that one is already a person elsewhere — a son, a neighbor, a friend, a congregant, a coach. In a life thin with such relationships, the office is the only lit space. Of course one performs on it. Where else is one going to be seen? The lonely person has nowhere to be a person except in performance, and the institutions she enters pay the price for her loneliness. Social media, which is often singled out as the cause of the platform pathology, is better understood as the amplifier of a dynamic that was already underway. It gives the atomized modern person a cheap, endlessly available stage on which to stand. If she had somewhere else to be, she would not need the stage so much. This is partly why, as we will argue in the final volume, the repair of institutional life and the repair of associational life are the same project. You cannot ask the young professional to submit to the mold of her firm if her firm is the only community she has. You have to give her a life in which the firm can go back to being one thing among many.

***

Congress is the paradigm case, and it deserves its own paragraph, because the story of what has happened to it tracks the argument so precisely that it can function as the argument's portable demonstration.

Madison designed Congress, as the *Federalist* tells us clearly enough, to be a deliberative body in which factional conflict would be channeled into compromise. The mechanisms by which the channeling was supposed to occur were institutional: rules of debate, committee structures, norms of seniority, long terms in the Senate, the internal socialization of new members by old ones. These mechanisms are what we are calling, collectively, the mold. They did not work by persuading a member to give up his faction; they worked by bearing down on his factional energy with the weight of a shared workplace, a shared calendar, a shared set of colleagues who knew him and whom he knew, a shared language of deliberation in which he had to operate. The historic Congress — the one you can read about in, say, the careers of its twentieth-century chairmen — was not an assembly of saints. It was an assembly of partisans who had been molded, by the institution, into something a partisan is not naturally: a legislator.

A lot of things accelerated the shift from mold to platform. Some of them are famous: the Gingrich-era reforms in the 1990s that moved members back home on weekends and dissolved the local social fabric that bound them to one another; the growth of cable news and then of social media, which restructured the incentives of political communication around national-audience performance; the rise of the primary as the main electoral threat to a member, which made it more important to brand oneself nationally than to legislate well locally; the centralization of party power in the leadership offices, which hollowed out the committees. None of these caused the platform pathology on its own; together they added up to a change in what the institution was for. A member who wants to legislate, still, in the older sense can still find ways. But the institution no longer forms her to. The reward system forms her for something else.

The result is the Congress we have, which is the Congress that cannot legislate. Not because its members are worse people than their predecessors — again, we insist on this, and it matters — but because the institution that was supposed to turn partisans into legislators has lost the capacity to do so. The mold is gone. The platform remains. The clips get made. The laws do not.

The presidency presents a related but distinct pathology: the long, slow personalization of executive power through the ever-thickening apparatus of mass media. The modern president is a performer at a scale no constitutional designer imagined, and every new communications technology — radio, television, direct cable access, the social feed — has deepened the degree to which the office is experienced, by officeholder and by public alike, as the country's principal stage. The presidency, more than any other American institution, has been a platform almost since the country acquired a national media. But the platform dimension has continued to grow relative to every other dimension of the office, and the constitutional mold — the cabinet, the departments, the structured rhythms of policy-making, the restraining counsel of institutional advisors — has had an increasingly hard time competing with the glare.

The courts, finally, are undergoing their own version of the same story. The confirmation hearing was once, not so long ago, an occasion for at least the performance of deliberation; now it is, visibly, a content event. The opinion — the brief, the concurrence, the dissent — increasingly has the rhythm and the voice of a product designed to travel. One can read certain recent opinions as one reads a viral post: the quotable line, the barbed aside, the appeal to an audience that is not the bar. We do not say every such opinion is written that way; most are not. But the drift is visible, and to a profession whose mold depends on the patient, impersonal voice of judicial craft, the drift is corrosive.

***

What repairs this? Levin's answer, which we endorse and will extend in Volume III, is that the repair of institutions cannot be done from outside. You cannot force a platform back into a mold by legislation; you cannot shame it back with op-eds; you cannot audit it back. The repair, if it happens, has to happen from inside, by the people who inhabit the institution reasserting, in their own practice, the molding function. The core move — and this is Levin's most useful sentence on the subject, in our view — is a single question that every person inside an institution has to learn to ask: *given my position here, how should I behave?*

The question is plain. It is also, in the platform logic, almost impermissible. It presupposes that the position has a content — that it makes demands, imposes constraints, comes with duties, rules out certain conduct and requires other conduct, and does so independent of what the person currently occupying the role happens to want. It presupposes that the right answer to "how should I behave" is not "whatever is most expressive of my authentic self" but "whatever this role requires of me." It presupposes, in other words, that the self is something to be partly surrendered, not something to be projected; and it presupposes that the surrender is not a loss but the condition of a particular kind of personhood — the personhood of someone capable of doing the work the institution exists to do.

This is not a small ask in a culture organized around self-expression. It is, in fact, the hard ask of this book. The citizen we are trying to recover is the person who, standing inside the roles her life has handed her, is willing to ask the question and abide by the answer. She is willing to be a good juror, even when the trial is dull and the case is not the one she would have designed. She is willing to be a good legislator, which means declining some of the performance opportunities her position makes available. She is willing to be a good doctor, which means putting down the podcast. She is willing to be a good reporter, which means holding the story. She is willing to be a good pastor, which means attending the bedside. She is willing, in short, to let her institution do to her what institutions are for: shape her into someone capable of doing its work. And she is willing to extend that acceptance across the many, many institutions a full life includes — the family, the neighborhood association, the volunteer board, the condo meeting, the parish council, the school committee, the state bar — not as grudging concessions to bureaucracy but as the arenas in which the kind of person she wants to be will, if it happens at all, be made.

***

Put all of this in the terms of the book's larger argument. The platform pathology is another face of the master disease: the simultaneous hollowing of the person and the institutions that were supposed to form her. These are not two different phenomena. They are the same phenomenon seen from two sides. The atomized individual — the one who belongs to nothing, who is the raw material of the tribe — has no mold to submit to. The institution used as a platform — the one that forms no one — has no one it is forming. The first produces the second; the second produces more of the first. The young person enters the university expecting formation and finds performance; she absorbs the performance; she goes on to her firm, her committee, her paper, her pulpit, and performs there; the people who come after her absorb, in turn, the performance from her; and another institution finishes its quiet passage from mold to platform, and another generation of its inhabitants is unformed by it.

The Arendt and Hoffer thesis we took up in the chapter on mass movements extends directly into this institutional case. Mass movements, they said, recruit from atomized individuals — from the people who have been stripped of every thick association and are therefore available to be taken in by a fictional world. If institutions no longer form their own members, then institutions no longer produce thickly associated persons; they produce unformed performers who are, just like the atomized individual of the mass-movement literature, available. Available for capture. Available as audience. Available as the congregation of whatever larger tribe happens to be offering a stage. The hollowed institution does not merely fail to produce citizens; it produces raw material for the movements that feed on the absence of citizens. That is how the platform pathology of our institutional life and the cult pathology of our political life come to feed each other. The institutions that cannot form their members become stages on which mass formations perform, and the mass formations recruit, with no resistance, from the unformed members the institutions have failed to shape.

***

If institutions are no longer doing the formative work, then what is forming modern Americans? Something has to be. Character does not go un-shaped; it is shaped by whatever is around it, and in the absence of the old molds the shaping passes to whatever can fill the vacuum. One of the leading candidates, and the one the empirical literature of the last two decades has most thoroughly documented, is partisan identity itself. The next chapter turns to the polarization research, which tells us, with uncomfortable precision, what begins to form a person when the institutions that used to form her have given up the job.



---

# Chapter 12
## The Polarization Machine

There is a sentence one hears now, at dinner tables, in classrooms, in the kind of strained conversation that used to be an ordinary exchange between neighbors, that would have struck an American of the mid-twentieth century as the symptom of a disease: *they are not just wrong; they are evil*. Sometimes it is said out loud. More often it is implied — carried in the tone with which the other party is named, in the grimace that accompanies the mention of the other candidate, in the pre-emptive dismissal of whatever argument the other side was about to make. It is the background music of our public life. It is also, by every measure the empirical literature can now supply, new. Americans disagreed with one another plenty in 1960, in 1976, in 1992. They did not, by and large, believe their fellow citizens in the opposing party were their enemies. They do now. And the question this chapter tries to answer is what exactly has happened, and how much of it is what it appears to be.

The first thing to say is that *polarization* is not one phenomenon but several, and the confusion among them is part of what makes the public conversation about polarization so unhelpful. It is worth slowing down long enough to pull them apart. There is what political scientists call *policy polarization* — the question of whether Americans, as individuals, now hold policy views further from the center than they used to. There is *ideological polarization* — the question of whether the two parties, as coalitions, have become more ideologically sorted, more internally consistent, more unlike each other in their platforms and their voting patterns. And there is *affective polarization* — the question of how Americans feel about members of the other party, and about the other party as such. These are three different questions. The answers are not the same.

On the first, the news is mild. Despite the noise, Americans do not, on average, hold policy views that are radically more extreme than the policy views of their grandparents. The distribution of opinion on most concrete policy questions — taxes, the minimum wage, abortion access, immigration levels, the size of the military — has shifted and widened, but not dramatically, and not uniformly in a single direction. There are real movements at the edges, and the edges matter; but the median American voter, asked what he actually wants on most issues, gives answers that would have been recognizable as mainstream a generation ago. Policy polarization among ordinary citizens is real but modest.

On the second, the news is significant. The parties themselves have become much more ideologically sorted than they were in the decades after the Second World War. This is the familiar story of the end of the Southern Democrats and the extinction of the Rockefeller Republicans — the story, told by political scientists over the past twenty years, of how American politics moved from a system in which each party contained multitudes to a system in which each party became a more coherent ideological vehicle. Liberals are now in one party; conservatives are now in the other. There is almost no overlap in congressional voting. The coalitions have become cleaner, the choices sharper, the parties more programmatic. By most definitions, this is a good thing for the voter, who can at last know what he is choosing. It is also, as we will see, a disaster for civic life, because it removes the cross-cutting pressures that used to force partisans of different stripes to treat one another as human.

But it is the third — affective polarization — that is the master finding of the last fifteen years of political science, and it is the one this chapter is centrally about. Affective polarization is the name Shanto Iyengar and his colleagues at Stanford gave, beginning in the early 2010s, to a phenomenon that had been slowly accumulating in the data and that they were the first to state clearly. In a 2012 paper titled "Affect, Not Ideology: A Social Identity Perspective on Polarization," Iyengar and his co-authors showed that the feelings ordinary Americans expressed about members of the other party had become, over roughly four decades of survey data, remarkably more hostile — more hostile than their actual policy disagreements could begin to explain. Americans had not, on the whole, moved to the poles. What they had done was come to despise each other. The distance between the feelings the average Democrat had about Republicans, and vice versa, had widened into something resembling an ethnic or religious animus.

This sounds like the kind of finding one is tempted to discount because it seems too on-the-nose, a social-science confirmation of what one already believes from reading the news. But the data are stubborn and the methodology is sound. The American National Election Studies — the long-running survey that is as close as we have to a continuous longitudinal record of American political feeling — have asked respondents, since the 1970s, to rate the two parties on a "feeling thermometer" that runs from zero (cold, hostile) to one hundred (warm, favorable). For most of the twentieth century, partisans rated their own party warmly and rated the other party coolly but not icily. Members of the opposing party were, roughly, the temperature of a business acquaintance one had reasons not to trust. Over the past forty years, the warmth toward one's own party has stayed roughly constant while the coldness toward the other party has deepened into something unprecedented. The gap is now the defining fact of the measurement. Americans do not feel more strongly about their own side than they used to. They feel much worse about the other side.

Iyengar's contribution was to reach for the right analytic framework to describe what was happening. He borrowed from the social-identity tradition in psychology — the tradition of Henri Tajfel and his successors, whose minimal-group experiments in the 1970s had shown how quickly and thoroughly human beings could be induced to form in-group loyalties and out-group hostilities on the flimsiest of pretexts. Partisan identity, Iyengar proposed, was no longer operating in the American mind as a mere political preference. It was operating as a *social identity* in the technical sense — a group membership around which in-group favoritism and out-group derogation organized themselves, with all the attendant cognitive and emotional consequences. The Democrat was no longer merely the person who voted for Democrats. He was a kind of person, carrying a package of presumed traits, warranting a package of presumed feelings. The Republican was not merely the person who voted for Republicans. He was, for the Democrat across the street, something like a member of a rival tribe.

If this were the end of the story, it would already be grim enough. But Lilliana Mason, in her 2018 book *Uncivil Agreement*, added a layer that makes it grimmer. Her central observation is that American partisan identity has become *stacked* with every other identity that used to cross-cut it. Once upon a time — not so long ago — partisan identity in the United States ran across lines of race, religion, region, education, and cultural style. There were white Protestant Democrats in Louisiana and Jewish Republicans in New York and union-loyal Democrats in Detroit whose sons voted Republican when they moved to the suburbs. A person's political side was one of many sides he inhabited, and it was not reliably predictable from his other memberships. That is no longer the case. The two parties have become, in Mason's term, *mega-identities*. Race, religion, region, cultural sensibility, even consumption patterns and musical taste now pattern with party at levels unseen in the postwar period. When a person's political opponent is also, with statistical reliability, a person of the other race, the other religion, the other region, the other cultural tribe, then political conflict ceases to be a disagreement about policy. It becomes a conflict in which the opposing party is the embodiment of everything-the-other-people-are against everything-you-are. Mason's work tracks, with unusual care, how this identity-stacking intensifies emotional response. The more identities a person layers onto his partisanship, the more hostile his feelings toward the other side tend to be — not because of anything the other side has done, but because there are more pieces of the self at stake in every political contest.

This is the modern condition of which the feeling thermometer is registering the temperature. The Democrat or Republican of 1960 inhabited a polity in which his opponents were also, with some regularity, his co-religionists, his neighbors, his fellow union members, his fellow fraternal-order members, his fellow bowlers. The intersections moderated the partisanship. The partisan could not entirely loathe the opposing party without loathing substantial pieces of his own life. The Democrat or Republican of the present inhabits a polity in which most of those intersections have thinned or vanished. His partisanship no longer contends, inside his own social life, with other loyalties that would pull against it. It is free to swell into the whole of his political soul. When it does, it acquires the tonal qualities of religious enmity — because, in the absence of countervailing identities, it has moved into the spaces religious enmity used to occupy.

***

The feeling thermometer is one measure. It is not the most alarming one. Over the last decade, Nathan Kalmoe and Lilliana Mason, working together, have documented something considerably more disturbing: the willingness of ordinary American partisans, when asked in surveys, to endorse hypothetical acts of violence against the other party. The numbers are not uniformly terrifying — most Americans do not, when asked, embrace political violence, and the survey methodology has real limits that honest readers of this research should register — but the numbers have risen, the rise correlates with affective polarization, and the floor of partisan hostility has moved in a direction that no democracy wants its floor to move. Kalmoe and Mason also document the rise of *dehumanizing* language in partisan discourse: the ascription of subhuman qualities to members of the other party, the framing of the opposing party as not merely wrong but morally or cognitively defective, the quiet spread of the vocabulary of vermin, disease, and infestation. This is not the language of ordinary political disagreement. It is the language that scholars of genocide use as a warning signal. That it is now audible in the mainstream of American partisan life is a fact to which serious observers must pay attention.

Rachel Kleinfeld, at the Carnegie Endowment, has produced the most useful policy-oriented synthesis of this material, drawing together the affective polarization research, the dehumanization findings, and a comparative literature on polarized democracies around the world. Her central observation is one that American readers, accustomed to the exceptionalist assumption that the United States is immune to the failure modes of democracies elsewhere, should sit with for a while. The United States now resembles, on a range of measurable indicators, other countries that have experienced severe democratic stress in the past generation — Venezuela before Chávez, Turkey before Erdoğan's consolidation, Hungary before Orbán's restructuring, the Philippines before and during Duterte. Not in its wealth, not in its institutions, not in the depth of its civic traditions; all of those remain stronger in the United States than in the comparison cases. But in the feeling-temperature of its partisan politics, in the degree to which partisans of each side now regard the other as an existential threat, in the willingness to excuse norm-breaking by one's own side as a necessary response to the norm-breaking of the other, the United States now sits in a cluster of countries whose subsequent trajectories are not reassuring. The path from our condition to democratic backsliding is well-documented in the comparative literature; it is not a speculative projection. Countries in this condition do not always continue to decline. But the conditions under which they recover are specific, and they require work. They do not recover by accident.

We do not introduce Kleinfeld's analysis to frighten the reader. The book is not written to frighten. We introduce it because one of the routine moves in American discussion of polarization is to shrug it off as so much elite hyperventilation — as an urban, educated, cable-news anxiety that does not touch most Americans' actual lives. There is something to this shrug, and we will come back to it. But the data on partisan hostility, on dehumanization, and on the tolerance of political violence are not confined to the elite. They extend through the population, and the rising floor they document is a fact about the country itself. Polarization in the United States is not a media invention. It is a real feature of the political culture, and it is operating, at this moment, in territory that comparative political science has learned to recognize.

***

The most important empirical finding of the last decade on American polarization — the one that will do the most structural work in this book — is the *perception gap*. The phrase is most associated with the organization More in Common, which published a series of reports in 2019, 2020, and 2021 drawing together survey data on what Americans think members of the other party believe. The findings, which have been replicated enough times now to count as established, are as follows.

Americans systematically overestimate how extreme the views of the other party are. Republicans think Democrats are, on average, considerably more radical than Democrats actually are. Democrats think Republicans are, on average, considerably more radical than Republicans actually are. The gap is not small. It is typically in the range of twenty to thirty percentage points, across a wide array of policy questions. Ask a Republican what share of Democrats holds a given extreme position, and he will name a figure far larger than the share that actually does. Ask a Democrat what share of Republicans holds a given extreme position, and he will do the same. Both sides are systematically wrong in the same direction, and wrong by roughly the same magnitudes. Both sides picture their opponents as a caricature of themselves, and both sides treat their opponents as if the caricature were accurate.

This, by itself, is a finding that ought to reshape how the country thinks about its own politics. The feeling thermometer registers the heat. The perception gap identifies one of the reasons the heat keeps climbing: each side is reacting, not to the actual other side, but to a fictional version of the other side that is much more extreme, much more hostile, much more threatening than the real one. A politics of images cannot be moderated by policy accommodations that were never the source of the heat. One cannot negotiate one's way out of a dispute whose shape both parties have misapprehended.

But the deeper finding in the More in Common work is the one that this book will lean on most heavily, because it cuts against the intuition that seems, at first, obvious. It would stand to reason that the more one pays attention to politics, the more news one consumes, the more political social-media content one ingests, the better-informed one would be about the other side. That is not what the data show. The data show the opposite. The most politically engaged Americans have, on average, the *most inaccurate* perceptions of the other party. The more news one consumes, the worse one's perception gap tends to be. Heavy consumers of cable news, heavy consumers of partisan social media, heavy consumers of political podcasts — the people who would, if asked, describe themselves as well-informed — are the population with the largest distortions of the other side. Their fellow citizens who pay less attention have, paradoxically, a more accurate picture of the country.

This is counterintuitive only if we misunderstand what most consumption of political media is for. It is not, for the most part, an act of learning about one's country. It is, as Eitan Hersh has argued with unusual directness in his 2020 book *Politics Is for Power*, something closer to what Hersh calls *political hobbyism* — the consumption of political content as an identity-confirmation and identity-maintenance activity. The hobbyist does not read the news in order to act in the world. He reads the news in order to feel a certain way about his side and about the other side, in order to have the day's grievance to chew on, in order to participate emotionally in a running national drama whose shape is stylized and familiar. Hersh's intervention is to note, bracingly, that many Americans who believe themselves to be deeply engaged in politics are not *doing* politics in any meaningful sense. They are not volunteering for campaigns. They are not organizing their neighbors. They are not showing up at school board meetings. They are not running for local office. They are not writing letters, joining parties in any real local-participation sense, or doing the dull work that actually moves democratic outcomes. They are consuming. And the media they consume, designed to hold their attention by engaging the emotional centers most responsive to tribal alarm, delivers a picture of the opposing side optimized for outrage rather than accuracy. The more they consume, the more distorted their picture of the other side becomes. The more distorted their picture, the more alarmed they are. The more alarmed they are, the more they consume. The feedback loop is the product.

And social media makes the loop tighter. Frequent users of political social media have, in the More in Common data, larger perception gaps than non-users. This is not because social-media users are getting more information; it is because the information they are getting has been selected, by the attention economy operating on the platform, from the tail of the distribution where the most extreme voices and the most inflammatory framings are to be found. We will return to the platform pathology in the next chapter. What matters here is the finding as finding. The people whose mental image of the other party is most distorted are not the disengaged; they are the engaged, and the means by which they stay engaged is also the means by which their image of the other party becomes less and less accurate.

One further detail from the perception-gap research is particularly worth attending to. Higher education, on its own, does not shrink the perception gap. Often it enlarges it, especially on the left. The well-educated Democrat, who has read more, thought more, and considered himself better positioned to understand the state of the country, turns out, on average, to have a *larger* misperception of what Republicans actually believe than the less-educated Democrat does. The same pattern, to a lesser extent, appears on the right: higher education does not consistently produce more accurate perceptions of the other side. This is a humbling finding for the educated reader — which is to say, for most readers of a book like this one. The credential does not confer understanding of one's fellow citizens. It often confers, instead, membership in a more homogeneous social environment in which the cartoons of the other side circulate with less resistance. The person who has been to graduate school has probably spent more of his adult life surrounded by people who share his political priors than the person who has not, and the cartoons in his head about the people who do not share those priors are, on average, worse.

We dwell on this because it is the empirical hinge of the whole chapter and of much of the book. A politics of affective polarization is being conducted, on both sides, largely against opponents who do not exist. The heat and the image are feeding each other. The consumers of the heat — the engaged, the educated, the well-informed as they would describe themselves — are the least accurate observers of the country whose politics they believe themselves to be following most closely. The citizenry is hostile to an image of itself it has itself constructed. This is not a figure of speech. It is a research finding, and it has survived enough replication that one can build on it without apology.

***

There is another structural fact about American polarization that has come into focus in the last few years, and that requires its own treatment. In 2024 Matt Grossmann and David Hopkins published *Polarized by Degrees*, the most systematic treatment yet of what has come to be called the *diploma divide*. Their central claim, backed by a substantial empirical architecture, is that the most important demographic realignment in American politics over the past generation is the re-sorting of the two parties along educational lines. College-educated Americans have, over the past two decades, moved decisively toward the Democratic coalition. Non-college Americans have moved, at a somewhat slower but still decisive pace, toward the Republican coalition. The sort has been going on across every major Western democracy; in the United States, it is now the single strongest demographic predictor of partisan affiliation, outpacing income, urbanicity, and most of the demographic variables that used to dominate the literature.

The consequence is not merely that the two parties now draw from different educational populations. It is that the two parties now live in substantially different *information ecosystems*, hold different *cognitive styles*, respond to different forms of argument, and trust different kinds of authority. Grossmann and Hopkins call this an *asymmetric polarization* because it is not a matter of the parties moving equally far from a shared center. It is a matter of the parties diverging along a dimension along which they differ in kind. The educated party is more reliant on institutional authority, more responsive to expert consensus, more prone to reason from official statistics, more trusting of the professional press. The less-educated party is more reliant on personal networks and trusted voices, more skeptical of institutional authority, more responsive to populist cues that frame institutions themselves as captured, more trusting of the emergent media that has grown up outside the professional press.

It is essential, in approaching this finding, not to pretend it describes a virtuous party and a vicious one. Both susceptibilities are susceptibilities. The educated party's trust in institutional authority becomes a vulnerability exactly when the institutions it trusts are themselves captured by the cultural assumptions of the educated party; the feedback loop looks, from outside, like a reasoning process but is in fact a homogeneous echo whose participants mistake their consensus for truth. The less-educated party's skepticism of institutional authority becomes a vulnerability exactly when that skepticism is harvested by figures whose relationship to truth is instrumental; the dismissal of institutional checks allows lies to propagate that an earlier political culture would have laughed off the stage. Each party is susceptible, along the grain of its cognitive style, to a characteristic failure mode. Each party is now living out that failure mode in full view of the other. Each party is horrified by the other's failure mode and blind to its own, and each mistakes the other's failure mode for confirmation that the other party is not merely wrong but unfit for self-government. That the two parties are now *epistemically* as well as *politically* different is one of the quieter and more dangerous features of the American condition.

It is not hard to see why the diploma divide amplifies everything else the chapter has so far described. A polity sorted along an educational line that also tracks the informational, cultural, and cognitive lines of modern life has lost, with that sort, most of the remaining cross-cutting ties that used to moderate partisanship in the postwar period. The Republican factory worker and the Democratic banker who used to share a civic club, a church, or a union local are not, with statistical frequency, sharing those spaces now. They have been sorted into different places, where different voices reach them, and where the voices tell them stories about one another that they have no counter-evidence to resist.

***

Why is this happening? Like every question of this kind, it does not admit of one answer, and the answers it does admit of are braided together in ways that resist tidy separation. But the candidate explanations, each with real support in the literature, can at least be named.

The first is the end of cross-pressured identities — the decades-long decoupling of partisanship from the other memberships that used to pull against it. The Southern Democrat, the northern ethnic Catholic Democrat, the Rockefeller Republican, the libertarian-leaning Western Republican: each of these was a figure whose partisan loyalty was in tension with some other loyalty, and the tension acted as a kind of internal brake on partisan fervor. As the parties have sorted, the brakes have come off. This is the Mason point at a different level of analysis: it is not only that individual voters have stacked their identities; it is that the coalitions themselves have lost the internal heterogeneity that used to ensure that no partisan could indulge total hostility to the other side without alienating some of his own.

The second is the *nationalization* of American political life. A generation ago, a great deal of American politics — in school boards, in state legislatures, in the daily operations of city government — was local in content and local in interpretation. The politics of one's town was reported by a local paper, decided on local terms, and understood through local frames of reference. That world is gone. Most local papers are gone, and the ones that survive are thinner than they were. The interpretive frame for local events is now almost entirely imported from the national conversation. A dispute about a school curriculum in a town of twelve thousand is now read, by its participants and by the commentariat, as a local engagement in a nationwide culture war. When everything local is interpreted nationally, local politics loses its moderating effect — the neighbor who voted the other way is no longer a neighbor one knows about the school board; he is a foot soldier in the other national army.

The third is the media transformation. A public sphere organized around a small number of broadcast institutions, each of which was obliged by the economics of mass audiences to avoid alienating large fractions of the country, produced a political media that was, by today's standards, tepid, institutionally cautious, and editorially bounded. The replacement of that media environment with a niche-driven, personality-driven, engagement-optimized environment has had direct consequences for the emotional temperature of politics. The old media's incentives pushed toward the center; the new media's incentives push toward whatever keeps the viewer, reader, or scroller emotionally engaged, which turns out, by the psychology of engagement, to be outrage. The next chapter takes this up in detail. What matters here is that the shift is not neutral with respect to polarization; it has been an active driver.

The fourth is the platform public sphere itself — the social-media and algorithmic environment in which a rising share of political perception is formed. Again, we defer the full treatment to the next chapter. The finding that matters here is that exposure to algorithm-selected political content increases affective polarization, and that the structure of the platforms tends to amplify out-group hostility because out-group hostility is a reliable producer of the engagement metrics the platforms are tuned to maximize. The platforms did not create polarization. They did, over the past fifteen years, give it a bigger megaphone, a faster feedback loop, and a more efficient mechanism for the distribution of distorted images of the other side.

The fifth is the status story, which we take up at length in Chapter 14. The decline of the communities and vocations that sustained a certain kind of American life over the past half-century has produced, on the one side, a population whose felt status loss is acute and whose politics is increasingly organized around the recovery of respect; and, on the other side, a credentialed class whose felt status ascendancy coincides, uncomfortably, with the declining fortunes of the communities from which many of its members came. The collision of these two status stories — the declining community's anger at being told its loss is deserved, and the credentialed class's incomprehension at the hostility its ascendancy has provoked — is a fuel source for affective polarization that policy arguments do not reach, because the underlying dispute is about who is owed respect in the order as a whole.

The sixth, and in some ways the most important to name explicitly, is the behavior of elites. Affective polarization among ordinary citizens does not generally run ahead of elite polarization. It tracks elite polarization, with a lag. When party leaders, major officeholders, prominent commentators, and the central figures of each coalition behave as if the other side is an existential threat, ordinary partisans follow. When elite rhetoric treats the opposing party as illegitimate, ordinary partisans absorb the framing, and the feeling thermometer obligingly registers it. There is no depoliticization of affect possible in an environment where the visible leaders of one's coalition are, week in and week out, modeling contempt for the other side. A country whose elites have decided to conduct politics as war will produce a citizenry whose political feelings are, eventually, warlike. Much of the recovery we will eventually argue for begins, or has to begin, with a change in elite behavior — not because elites are the problem, but because elites are the weather, and the weather is teaching the country what kind of feelings are appropriate to politics. The feelings we are being taught are not compatible with self-government for long.

***

There is a further connection to draw, and it is the one around which this book's contribution to the polarization literature mainly organizes. The polarization research, like most research, tends to treat affective polarization as a more-or-less freestanding phenomenon with its own causes — elite cues, media environments, platform dynamics, status shifts. Those are real. But the argument of this book is that affective polarization is also, and deeply, a *symptom* of the atomization and social-capital collapse traced in Chapter 10. The two diseases are not separate. They interlock.

Consider the profile of the person who is, empirically, most moderate in his affective stance toward the other party. He is not necessarily politically moderate in his views. He is someone who belongs to a strong local community, who has real friendships across lines of difference — the coworker, the fellow board member, the neighbor, the old schoolmate — whose weekly life includes some form of civic or associational work that matters to him regardless of which party controls Washington, whose sources of meaning are not exhausted by his politics. Such a person is, on the whole, less hostile to the other party than his more isolated counterpart. His cross-cutting ties do what cross-cutting ties have always done: they make the other party, in his imagination, harder to caricature, because he already knows specific people who belong to it and whose humanity he cannot deny without denying something of his own life.

Consider, by contrast, the profile of the person who is, empirically, most hostile. He is not necessarily more extreme in his actual policy views. He is someone whose social life has contracted, in the way documented at length by Putnam, around a narrower and more homogeneous circle. His closest encounters with the other party are mediated — by cable, by podcast, by the algorithmic feed — and those mediated encounters are, as we have seen, selected for the most inflammatory exemplars of the other side. He belongs to fewer associations. He has fewer cross-cutting friendships. His political identity has moved, by default, into spaces that other identities used to share. He is the ideal consumer of political hobbyism, because his life has been cleared of the other things politics used to compete with.

This is why the two halves of this book's diagnosis cannot be separated. The lonely person is the affectively polarized person. The polarized polity and the atomized polity are not two different polities. They are the same polity seen from two different angles. Any serious account of American polarization that treats it as a problem of ideas, or a problem of platforms, or a problem of elites, without also treating it as a problem of the social infrastructure of ordinary life, is going to produce interventions that cannot work. Any serious account of American social-capital collapse that does not notice that the vacated social spaces have been filled by a particular kind of partisan attachment is going to produce an inadequate picture of what it is that has been lost. The phenomena are one phenomenon.

That is the theoretical claim this book has been building toward since the Introduction. Hoffer and Arendt saw it first, in a different vocabulary: the atomized person is the person available for the mass movement. We are seeing, in contemporary political science, the same dynamic in more measurable form. The person whose local community has thinned to nothing is the person whose partisan identity has swollen to everything. The person whose partisan identity has swollen to everything is, by predictable psychological mechanics, the person for whom the other party has become the enemy. The perception gap is not an independent variable. It is what happens to the mental image of the other side when there is no ballast of real, specific relationships to check it against.

***
