The good news in the empirical literature — and it really is good news — is that the perception gap is correctable, and that correcting it reliably reduces affective polarization. This is the finding on which much of Volume III's practical program will rest, and we flag it here because it is the hopeful counterpart to the grim material of this chapter. A sustained line of experimental research, much of it now gathered under the umbrella of Robb Willer's Strengthening Democracy Challenge at Stanford and in the related megastudies, has shown that interventions which show partisans what the other side actually believes — as opposed to what they think the other side believes — produce statistically significant, durable reductions in affective polarization. The mechanism is straightforward. Correct the caricature, and the emotions scaled to the caricature decline. Show a Republican what his Democratic neighbor actually thinks about a given issue, not the cable-news stereotype of what a Democrat is supposed to think, and his feelings about Democrats soften, measurably and for some period after the intervention. Show a Democrat the same about Republicans, and the same effect follows.

An adjacent and crucial finding needs to be stated here with care. Unstructured exposure to the other side does *not* reliably reduce affective polarization, and in some experimental conditions actively worsens it. This is the finding Chris Bail has made central in his work on the platform environment and will receive its full treatment in the next chapter. Simply putting partisans in the path of opposing voices — on Twitter, on television, on a social-media feed — can, by exposing each side to the most obnoxious examples of the other, deepen hostility rather than reduce it. The interventions that work are not naive exposure. They are *structured*, *evidence-based*, *correction-oriented* engagements in which the participant is presented with the real distribution of views on the other side, or with real individuals speaking in good faith about their actual beliefs, under conditions that allow the correction to register. The difference between what works and what does not is the difference between contact and caricature. Real contact — structured, careful, honest — reduces polarization. Caricature, even caricature delivered by people of the other side, amplifies it.

This is a serious operational finding, and it is one of the reasons Volume III is not a book of platitudes. We know, from research that has been accumulating for a decade and that is now at the level of reproducible megastudies, that there are specific things that work. The work of Volume III will be to describe them, to set out the institutional architectures in which they can be enacted, and to address the political-economy question of why, given that we know these things, so little of the country's political life is organized around doing them.

***

The temptation, at the end of a chapter like this, is to speak of polarization as a weather system — as something that happens to us, against which we can at best prepare. It is not a weather system. It is a machine, and machines have designers, operators, and power sources. Each of the layers we have traced — the mega-identity, the perception gap, the diploma divide, the platform incentive structure, the elite rhetoric — is an input. None of them is a permanent feature of the American order. Each of them is the current state of a variable that could, under other conditions, take a different value. The machine is being run. Some of those who run it are conscious of running it; most are not; a great many Americans feed the machine daily without realizing they are its fuel. That does not make it a weather system. It makes it a machine whose operations we participate in.

Polarization does not float free of the media and platform environment in which it is reproduced. It is grown in that environment, fed by that environment, and amplified by it. The next chapter turns directly to that environment — to what has happened to the public sphere itself, to the communicative space in which Americans are supposed to form opinions together. If this chapter has shown the heat at which the country's partisan feelings now burn, the next chapter will look at the fuel and the bellows. The puzzle is not merely that Americans dislike the other party. The puzzle is the set of mechanisms by which Americans are now, at every hour of every day, invited to dislike an image of the other party that is not the party they actually share a country with. What those mechanisms are, how they work, and what a citizen who wishes to be more than their fuel is supposed to do about them, is the subject to which we now turn.


---

# Chapter 13
## The Public Sphere Shattered

In 1962 a young German sociologist, lately returned from the rubble of his country's catastrophe and looking for the intellectual conditions that had made its democracy so brittle, published a book that would quietly rearrange the way everyone thereafter thought about democratic life. Jürgen Habermas's *The Structural Transformation of the Public Sphere* is not an easy book, and it is not, in the strict sense, a book about politics at all. It is a book about a communicative space — a *Öffentlichkeit*, literally a "publicness" — that had emerged in the coffeehouses of eighteenth-century London, the salons of Paris, the reading societies and moral weeklies and early newspapers of Hamburg and Philadelphia, in which private persons assembled as a public and reasoned together about the matters the state was doing in their name. This space, Habermas argued, was the hidden precondition of liberal democracy. Formal voting rights, constitutional limits on power, free elections — none of these would have produced what we now call democratic self-government without the prior existence of a place where citizens could form, together, an opinion that deserved to be called public rather than merely aggregated.

We want to be careful about what Habermas did and did not claim, because his argument has been caricatured almost as often as it has been quoted. He did not claim that the eighteenth-century public sphere was ideal. He knew perfectly well that it was narrow. It was overwhelmingly male; it was overwhelmingly bourgeois; it excluded the laboring poor, the enslaved, and the colonized populations whose labor underwrote the commerce that paid for the coffee. It reproduced class hierarchies even as it made arguments against absolute monarchy. What Habermas claimed, more modestly and more powerfully, was that within its narrow compass this public sphere was genuinely deliberative — that when the men gathered in Lloyd's or at Madame Geoffrin's salon or in the pages of the *Tatler* argued about policy or philosophy or religion, they argued under a set of implicit rules that made the arguing worth something. Status was temporarily suspended in favor of the better argument. Reasons counted. Positions could be changed by evidence. The subject of discussion was, in principle, anything the state was doing, and nothing was in principle off the table because of who was asking.

The subsequent history of democracy, on Habermas's account, was the extension of this form beyond the class that first occupied it. The cause of democratic inclusion — the enfranchisement of the unpropertied, of women, of racial minorities, of the colonized — was the insistence that the deliberative form which had been confined to a particular class of men was the birthright of every adult. The corrective to the narrowness was not to abandon the form but to demand admission to it. The abolitionist press, the women's suffrage movement, the civil rights movement — all of them worked, in one of their registers, by appealing to the norms of the public sphere and demanding that those norms be applied without exception. They argued, in effect: *your own rules commit you to hearing us.* And because the rules were real, the rules could be invoked.

Democratic expansion, in other words, depended on the public sphere's form — not its original membership. The rules of mutual address, of reason-giving, of treating the other as someone who might convince you, were the tracks on which inclusion ran. Without them, inclusion is merely the arrival at a table where no one is talking to anyone.

This is the concept we now find in trouble.

***

Sixty years after the book, in 2022, Habermas returned to the subject. The essay — "A New Structural Transformation of the Public Sphere," published in *Theory, Culture & Society* — is the work of an old man who has lived through the rise and partial collapse of the postwar democratic settlement he helped theorize, and it is written with a sobriety that borders at moments on grief. His argument, stripped of its philosophical apparatus, is that the digitalization of public communication is not merely shifting the public sphere to new venues. It is fragmenting that sphere past the point where it can perform its democratic function.

The older public sphere, even in its imperfect mid-twentieth-century mass-media form — a few networks, a handful of newspapers of record, a shared evening news — at least produced an approximation of a common object of attention. When Walter Cronkite or Edward R. Murrow spoke about the war in Vietnam, disagreement took place on the basis of a rough shared stipulation of what had happened that day. The disagreement could be fierce; what the disagreement could not easily be was about whether the events themselves were real. Habermas's 2022 argument is that the digital public sphere has dissolved even that thin shared basis. We no longer disagree within a common frame. We disagree about the frame. And because deliberation requires, at a minimum, that the process of forming opinions be *shared* — that we be reasoning about the same object, with some minimal agreement about what counts as evidence — the disappearance of the frame is not a small thing. It means that democratic procedure can continue to be followed — elections held, laws passed, courts convened — while the substance that once made those procedures meaningful has quietly drained away. Democracy becomes procedurally intact but substantively hollow.

This is the large and careful diagnosis. It is also, we should note, one that some of the digital optimists have legitimately pushed back on. The new media environment is not merely corrosive; it has given voices to people who were excluded from the mid-century gatekept media almost as categorically as the eighteenth century excluded the poor. It is where dissident Iranian feminists and Hong Kong protesters and North African revolutionaries found each other. It is where the isolated gay kid in a hostile small town discovers he is not alone in the world. These are real democratic goods and we are not going to pretend otherwise. The question Habermas's essay forces is whether the architecture that makes those goods possible also, by the same mechanisms, makes something else — the shared frame within which democratic deliberation can take place at scale — structurally more difficult to sustain. That is the question a serious account has to engage.

***

The popular version of the argument has been made most insistently in recent years by the social psychologist Jonathan Haidt, and because his version is the one most readers will have encountered, we want to walk through it carefully and then set it alongside the more cautious empirical literature that has grown up around it.

Haidt's thesis, developed across a series of *Atlantic* essays in 2022 and 2023 and consolidated in his 2024 book *The Anxious Generation*, has two linked components. The first is about adolescent mental health: somewhere around 2012 and 2013, on measure after measure — depression, anxiety, self-harm, suicide, loneliness — something went badly wrong for American and more broadly Western adolescents, and the curves do not bend back. Girls are hit harder than boys. The cohort effect is striking enough that it is visible to anyone who has spent time on a college campus or in a high school over the past decade. The second component is about politics: the period after 2012 is also the period in which American political polarization — specifically affective polarization, the measure of how much partisans dislike and distrust each other — accelerates to levels the country has not seen since before the Second World War. Haidt's explanation for both phenomena is, in his telling, the same phenomenon: the transition, around 2010 to 2012, to a universally smartphone-based social-media environment whose algorithms are optimized for engagement, and whose engagement maxima happen to be located in emotionally activating, out-group-hostile content.

The evidence Haidt adduces is not trivial. The time-series data on adolescent depression and self-harm is, to put it bluntly, striking. The line goes flat through the 2000s and bends sharply upward around 2012 in most of the Anglosphere and much of northern Europe. The curves for screen time, social-media adoption, and smartphone ownership track almost exactly. Haidt's argument is that the simultaneity is too clean to be coincidence and that the causal story he proposes — a fundamental rewiring of adolescent social life around platforms engineered for compulsive use and optimized for outrage — has more predictive force than the competing explanations on offer. The 2008 financial crisis is too early. The opioid epidemic affected a different demographic. School-shooting fear and climate anxiety are present but fail to explain why the timing is so specific and so global.

We find this argument compelling in its broad outlines, and we are not among the critics who dismiss it as a moral panic. But we are also obligated to note that rigorous scholars disagree, and a book that pretends the disagreement is not there has already failed its own epistemic standards.

The most serious empirical skepticism comes from Amy Orben and Andrew Przybylski at Oxford, whose work on adolescent well-being and screen time has consistently found that social-media use accounts for a very small fraction of the variance in adolescent mental-health outcomes — smaller than the variance explained by regular eating of breakfast, on one of their more cited calculations. Their point is not that screens are harmless. It is that the causal story Haidt tells requires effect sizes that are not consistently visible in the individual-level data, and that the ecological correlation between national smartphone adoption curves and national depression curves, while striking, does not settle the matter. Their critique has been influential enough that the most careful reviewers of the literature now describe the evidence as *suggestive but contested* rather than *established*. The most honest reading of this debate, so far as we can tell, is something like this: something real happened to adolescent well-being in the early 2010s; the phone-and-social-media hypothesis has more predictive support than the available alternatives; the effect is probably smaller than the strongest Haidt claims suggest and larger than the strongest skeptics concede. We should say what we know, admit what we don't, and stop pretending the disagreement has been resolved in favor of whichever camp we are already in.

***

On the political side, the most important countervailing voice — and the one we will spend some time with, because it has shaped our thinking more than any other single source — is the Duke sociologist Chris Bail, whose 2021 book *Breaking the Social Media Prism* is, to our minds, the most empirically careful book on the platform-and-polarization question anyone has written.

Bail's central finding is that the popular explanation of social-media polarization — *echo chambers* — is largely wrong, and wrong in an interesting way. The echo-chamber story holds that the platforms sort users into ideologically homogeneous pockets, exposure to disagreeing views is therefore reduced, and polarization increases because people never see the other side. If this were true, the obvious fix would be to expose people to more of the other side. Bail and his collaborators ran the experiment. They paid Republicans and Democrats to follow a Twitter bot that showed them content from the other side's political opinion leaders, and measured attitudes before and after. The result was not that polarization decreased. Polarization *increased*. Republicans exposed to Democratic content became more conservative; Democrats exposed to Republican content became more liberal, though the effect was smaller on that side.

The finding rhymes uncomfortably with decades of social-psychology research on what happens when identity-salient groups are exposed to opposing positions without structure, without shared task, without equal-status contact. The raw exposure is not the repair. In some conditions the raw exposure is itself the injury. A great deal of well-intentioned prescription about our current predicament — "pop your filter bubble," "read the other side's paper," "follow people you disagree with" — turns out to rest on a mechanism that does not behave the way our folk model says it does. Bail's work does not mean the prescription is always wrong. It means the prescription, administered without the structural conditions that make cross-partisan exposure generative rather than corrosive, often produces the opposite of what it promises.

What is actually happening on the platforms, on Bail's account, is something he calls the *prism effect*. Social media is a medium in which the most extreme voices on each side are disproportionately visible, because extremity is rewarded by the attention economy; the moderate voices on each side are disproportionately silent, because moderation does not move the engagement needle; and the users on each side, scrolling through feeds populated by these amplified extremes, form a picture of the other side that corresponds to almost nobody on the other side. The out-partisan one hates on the feed is a caricature. The in-partisan one sees modeled on the feed is also a caricature — an extremist who makes one's own moderate co-partisans invisible, and who thereby generates a silent secondary pressure toward extremity, or toward silence, for everyone watching.

This is the word *prism* is doing. A prism does not block the light on the other side; it distorts it. You see the other side, but you see it refracted into a shape that does not correspond to the object. And because the refraction happens on your own side as well, the image of oneself one forms on the platform is also distorted — more loyal to the tribe, more contemptuous of the opposition, more certain, more performative than one is in one's actual life off the platform. Bail's interviews with heavy platform users include a striking recurring theme: many of them describe themselves as having two political selves, an online one and an offline one, and the online one is more extreme, more angry, more scornful, than they themselves believe themselves to really be.

The mechanism that drives all of this, on Bail's account, is not information-seeking. It is status-seeking and identity performance. People post to signal group membership, not to share knowledge. The algorithms reward what gets reactions. The reactions are driven, reliably, by moral-emotional content, and especially by out-group anger. The engine is not a knowledge engine. It is a status engine — and status, in a mediated environment at global scale, turns out to select against the habits of mind a public sphere requires.

***

A more recent strand of empirical work has taken Bail's argument into the guts of the platforms themselves, where the algorithms actually live. The computer scientist Smitha Milli and her collaborators have run experiments in which users on X are randomized between the algorithmically curated timeline and a chronological baseline, and the algorithmic timeline's content is then analyzed for emotional and out-group-hostile valence. The findings, replicated now across several platforms: the algorithmic timeline contains systematically more emotionally activating content, and systematically more out-group-hostile content, than the chronological feed. Exposure to the algorithmically curated content produces measurable increases in affective polarization. And — this is the finding that should stop the reader — users report that they would *prefer* the less algorithmic timeline. The optimization is not doing what the users themselves, on reflection, want it to do.

This matters, because it moves the argument off the individual level and onto the structural level. For a long time, the debate about social media and democracy had the following shape: the pessimists blamed the platforms, the optimists blamed the users, and the argument stalled at the question of whose fault it was. The Milli-style findings suggest the question is not best posed that way. Platforms are not neutral pipes through which user preferences flow. They are optimization engines aimed at engagement. Engagement is not the same as a functioning public sphere. In most cases engagement selects *against* the conditions a functioning public sphere requires — deliberation, context, slowness, patience, the willingness to let an argument unfold over more than the seven seconds required for a thumbstroke. The problem is not that users have bad taste. The problem is that the architecture systematically amplifies the content that produces the worst democratic externalities, *even when the users themselves do not want that content to be amplified*.

This is the frame in which the platform question becomes a structural question rather than a moral one. It is not that users should have more discipline, though they should. It is not that platforms should have better ethics, though they should. It is that a communicative environment optimized for engagement will, absent countervailing structure, produce a public sphere that selects against deliberation. And a public sphere that selects against deliberation will not deliberate, no matter how many individual users maintain the private habits that a deliberative sphere would require.

***

We want to pause here to name something that strikes us, in both the empirical and the popular literature, as rare and valuable. Haidt and Bail disagree substantively. Haidt believes the platform effect is larger than Bail does; Bail believes Haidt's picture overstates the echo-chamber contribution and underweights the prism mechanism; they come out in different places on several of the key empirical questions. And yet, for the past several years, they have maintained a public, open, jointly curated Google Doc — "Social Media and Political Dysfunction: A Collaborative Review" — in which they catalogue the studies on the question they are arguing about, annotate them, disagree about what they show, and keep the document open to anyone who wants to see the current state of the argument. This, we think, is close to what a functioning public sphere ought to look like when two serious people disagree about a contested empirical question. They are not trying to destroy each other. They are not producing competing monographs that pretend the other's evidence does not exist. They are maintaining a shared reading list. They are making their disagreement visible and productive rather than invisible and corrosive. It is not a perfect model — it is two professors with secure academic posts and the resources to keep a document alive — but it is a model, and the rarity of the model is itself a piece of evidence about the ambient state of the public sphere.

***

Where, having walked through this literature, do we come out?

We think the public sphere has in fact been transformed in a way that degrades democratic deliberation, and we think the transformation is not primarily a matter of individual bad habits, though individual bad habits matter. It is a matter of the architecture of the mediated environment in which most political speech now happens. Four features of that architecture, in our view, are doing most of the damage, and we want to name them as clearly as we can.

The first is the algorithmic amplification of emotionally activating, out-group-hostile content. This is the Milli finding, replicated enough now that we should treat it as established: the ranking algorithms on the major platforms systematically elevate content that triggers moral-emotional reactions, and especially content that triggers anger at the other side. This is not a judgment about any particular platform's intentions. It is a structural consequence of optimizing for engagement in a two-camp political environment. The engagement gradient runs through out-group anger, and so the algorithm, following the gradient, ends up selecting for out-group anger.

The second is the reduction of moderate, slow, context-rich communication. The form of the platforms — short, decontextualized, punchy, reaction-inviting — is not well suited to the kind of speech a deliberative public sphere needs. This is not to romanticize the eighteenth-century salon or the mid-century newspaper of record, both of which had severe pathologies of their own. It is to note that the formal properties of the medium shape the content that flourishes in it. Habermas's argument, all the way back in 1962, was that the coffeehouse afforded a kind of exchange — in-person, extended, topic-bounded, with a stipulated suspension of status — that the print medium of the day could supplement and extend. The platform medium affords something different: rapid, context-stripped, status-saturated, reaction-driven. What flourishes in it is not what flourished in the earlier form.

The third is the collapse of the gatekeepers who, for roughly a century, enforced minimum standards of accuracy and relevance in the main channels of public speech. This one we want to be careful with. The mid-century gatekeepers were not heroes. They were largely white, largely male, largely drawn from a narrow professional class, and they used their gatekeeping power to marginalize voices that deserved to be heard. The platform revolution's democratizing effect — the fact that the previously silenced can now speak — is a real gain, and we are not nostalgic for the world that excluded them. But the disappearance of gatekeeping was not a pure liberation. The gatekeepers were also, at their best, enforcing norms of verification, of context, of relevance, of a distinction between what could be said responsibly in public and what could not. Those norms were, at a minimum, one of the mechanisms by which the mid-century public sphere produced something resembling a shared picture of the world. Their collapse has not been replaced by anything playing the same role. What we have now is less exclusion and more noise, and it is not obvious that the tradeoff, in its current form, favors democratic deliberation over the alternative.

The fourth is the emergence of what we might call a *status economy of public opinion*: a system in which performing opinion in public generates reward — attention, followers, influence, money — independent of any real-world consequences for the speaker. In older communicative environments, publicly staking a position had costs that tracked, at least loosely, the truth of the position. A reporter who filed a false story, a scholar who published a fabricated study, a pundit who made a confident prediction that failed — all faced some version of reputational consequence within a professional community that knew the field and cared about the record. The platform environment has, to a significant degree, decoupled the reward for stating an opinion from the truth of the opinion. What is rewarded is the *performance* of the opinion — its vividness, its loyalty-signal to the right audience, its out-group-hostility — and these properties are not well correlated, and are often anti-correlated, with the opinion's accuracy. In such a system the cost of being wrong about a fact is low; the cost of being off-tribe is high. The incentives run directly against the epistemic virtues a public sphere is supposed to cultivate.

The hardest honest claim we can make here — the one we keep coming back to, because it refuses to be explained away — is this: the prism Bail describes is not neutral. A medium that distorts the political landscape by amplifying extremes and silencing moderates, that decouples reward from truth, that optimizes for engagement at the expense of deliberation, is not merely a prism. It is a prism that *selects against deliberation*. A public sphere that selects against deliberation will not deliberate. You can tell the people in such a sphere to deliberate all you like. The architecture is stronger than the advice.

***

There is a complementary argument to Habermas's and Bail's that we think deserves its place in any serious account of what has happened to the public sphere, and we want to say something about it before we turn back to the book's central thesis. Jonathan Rauch's 2021 book *The Constitution of Knowledge* is, on its surface, a book about the epistemic side of the current crisis: the collapse of the institutions and norms by which a society produces warranted public belief. Peer review in the sciences. Journalistic verification and editorial correction. Professional standards in the law, in medicine, in the civil service. The accumulated practices by which a society distinguishes, imperfectly but usefully, between what is roughly known and what is merely asserted.

Rauch's claim is that these institutions, taken together, constitute something — he calls it the *constitution of knowledge* — that works in a way analogous to how the political constitution works. It distributes authority; it requires checks; it forces claims to pass through multiple points of possible disconfirmation before they can be treated as knowledge; it produces, at the far end of the process, something that is not Truth with a capital T but is far better than any single person's assertion. And his argument is that this constitution of knowledge has been under sustained assault from two directions in our moment — a trollish populist right that simply denies the authority of expertise, and an identity-saturated progressive left that denies the possibility of expertise unmarked by the identity of the expert — and that the damage from the two assaults compounds. The institutions that were supposed to produce warranted public belief are no longer doing so with anything like their former reliability. What replaces them is not a better system. It is a worse one, in which public belief is produced by the same status-performance mechanisms we have just been describing.

We read Rauch as complementary to Habermas and to Bail rather than as arguing something different. Habermas says the public sphere is where opinions are formed through shared reasoning. Bail says the current public sphere distorts the formation. Rauch says the public sphere is not only a place for forming opinions — it is one of the institutions whose properly functioning form actually produces knowledge, and when it breaks, the knowledge-producing function breaks with it. Taken together, the three arguments say something like: the space in which we are supposed to form opinions and the space in which the society is supposed to produce warranted belief are the same space, and when that space is restructured by an architecture that selects against deliberation and against verification, the society loses both the deliberative capacity and the epistemic capacity at once. It is not a crisis of minds. It is a crisis of institutional conditions for the minds.

***

We want to tie this chapter back to the book's central thesis, because the public-sphere argument is not a separate argument. It is continuous with, and makes worse, the phenomena we have been tracking since Chapter 9.

Recall where Arendt and Hoffer left us. The raw material of mass formation, they argued, is the lonely individual — the person who belongs to nothing and is thus available for capture by anything. Lonely people, they showed, do not become the cadres of mass movements because they have thought their way there. They become the cadres because the movement offers them a world to belong to, a cause to lose themselves in, a devil to hate. The movement's appeal is not cognitive. It is existential. And the pre-condition of the appeal is the prior stripping-away of the texture of ordinary life in which a person could, without a movement, be someone.

A public sphere of the kind we have just described *accelerates the formation*. Consider what the prism does, specifically, to a lonely person scrolling a feed. The feed makes the enemy vivid. The out-group, rendered in caricature by the algorithmic selection for its most extreme voices, is present at all hours, available for contempt, saturating the inner life of the viewer with the feeling of being under siege by a hostile force that wants to destroy her and everything she cares about. The feed also makes the ordinary fellow citizen abstract. The moderate neighbor, the co-worker who votes the other way but is willing to talk, the unglamorous town-meeting participant who does not care much about politics but shows up to zoning hearings — these people do not appear on the feed at all, because their speech does not trigger engagement. The public the lonely scroller inhabits, mediated through the prism, is one in which the only visible others are enemies and allies, both extreme, and the vast middle that actually constitutes the country is edited out.

This is the perfect preparation for mass formation. Arendt's lonely individual had at least the advantage of being lonely *without* a vivid picture of the enemy. The mass movements of her century had to construct that picture themselves, through propaganda organs and party newspapers and rallies. Ours is a public sphere in which the equivalent picture is constructed continuously, by no one in particular, as a byproduct of the optimization of attention. The enemy is supplied by the architecture. The movement that organizes the enemy-hatred has only to harvest it.

The argument cuts the other way on Putnam as well. If the decline of bridging social capital — the loss of the ties across lines of difference that Chapter 10 identified as the most corrosive feature of the civic collapse — was always going to be hard to reverse, the shattering of the public sphere has made it harder still. Bridging ties, historically, were formed partly in the local institutions that thinned in the second half of the twentieth century, and were partly sustained by the shared media ecology that at least gave cross-partisan Americans a rough common frame to disagree within. Both of those conditions have eroded. The local institutions have thinned. The media-ecological ground has moved. The people who might form a bridging tie are no longer inhabiting the same perceptual world to begin with, and the attempt to build a bridge across a perceptual chasm is much harder than the attempt to build one across a partisan one.

And the institutional-platform pathology we took up in Chapter 11 is, we now think, partly downstream of the public-sphere pathology. The incentives of the platform public sphere — the reward for public performance of opinion, the punishment for private craft, the substitution of brand for formation — radiate outward into the institutions themselves. A politician who inhabits a platform public sphere will become a platform politician, because the platform public sphere is the environment her reputation lives in. A journalist, a scholar, a clergyman, a doctor facing the same incentive gradient will face the same temptation. Levin's argument that institutions have become platforms is correct, but it is incomplete without the observation that the ambient medium in which all of them now operate is itself a platform. The institutions are downstream of the public sphere as much as the public sphere is downstream of the institutions. The two pathologies reinforce each other.

***

If the diagnosis is structural, what is the repair? We will spend the whole of Volume III on this question and will not try to anticipate its answers here. But we want to leave the reader with a sense of where we think the repair can plausibly happen, because a diagnosis that does not gesture toward repair is a diagnosis that breeds the fatalism it claims only to describe.

Almost none of what can be done about the public sphere happens at the scale of "the public sphere." The scale is too large; the architecture is too entrenched; the centralized reform that would be required to change the architecture is not politically available on any timeline that matters. What can be done happens at smaller scales, and the smaller the scale, the more available the action.

One scale below the national public sphere is the deliberative institution — the citizens' assembly, the deliberative poll, the structured small-group conversation across difference. The empirical literature here is encouraging, and we will spend a chapter on it. The finding, in outline, is that ordinary citizens, given time, information, and good facilitation, deliberate seriously and converge on considered positions even across severe partisan divides. The precondition is not better citizens. It is better conditions — small, bounded, face-to-face, topic-specific, with ground rules. These are the communicative conditions Habermas theorized in 1962. They are possible to reconstitute at small scales even when they are not possible to sustain at scale. They will not replace the national public sphere; they will, in some limited way, serve as its rehearsal spaces, and as existence proofs that the thing the national public sphere no longer easily produces is still producible by human beings when the conditions are right.

A scale below that is the individual practice, and here we want to say the thing that we keep wanting to say and keep suspecting we are not yet saying clearly enough. The most available action anyone in our moment has, in response to the shattered public sphere, is the refusal to participate in its worst incentives. This is not a counsel of retreat. The monastic retreat from the public is exactly the failure mode we warned against in the introduction, and we are not now reversing that. It is a counsel of discipline — the discipline of not posting the outraged take, of not taking the bait, of not performing the in-group signal, of reading the article before quote-tweeting the headline, of noticing when one is being operated on by the engagement engine and declining to be operated on. In Chapter 17 we will call this critical thinking as civic discipline and will develop it more systematically. In Chapter 20 we will return to it as a personal practice. For now we want only to name that it exists, that it is available, and that in an architecture that selects against deliberation the refusal to perform the selected-for behaviors is itself a small contribution to the sum of deliberative capacity in the society. Most of the meaningful moral action in a rigged environment is at the level of the individual choice to be rigged or not.

***

We want to close this chapter where we began it, with Habermas in 1962, and with a small observation that we have been circling for the entire chapter.

The eighteenth-century public sphere was not a natural phenomenon. It did not arise because human beings are naturally deliberative, or because the men of the Enlightenment had better habits than we do. It arose because a specific set of institutional conditions — print technology at a certain density, a commercial society at a certain maturity, a set of legal protections on speech and assembly at a certain depth, and a set of shared social norms about how men assembled in public were supposed to address one another — conspired to produce a medium in which a certain kind of communication was possible. The medium did not make the men deliberative. It made deliberation available to them, given a certain amount of effort on their part. When the conditions changed, the medium changed, and the deliberation that had been available through it either found new conditions or disappeared.

We are in one of the moments when the conditions have changed. The new medium is optimized for something that is not deliberation, and it is optimized for that something by mechanisms that operate below the level of the individual user's conscious preference. Whether the democratic function the old public sphere performed can be reconstituted under the new conditions is the open question of our moment, and we do not know the answer. What we do know is that the reconstitution will not happen by accident, and it will not happen by exhortation. It will happen, if it happens, by the deliberate construction of smaller-scale spaces in which the deliberative conditions can be reassembled; by the personal disciplines that make a person usable as a citizen in such spaces; and by whatever institutional and technological reforms can, over time, make the larger-scale medium less hostile to the thing we are trying to preserve.

It is fair to ask whether this is enough. A prism at global scale is not going to be abolished by a few citizens' assemblies in Ireland and a few thousand individual refusals to post the outraged take. We know this. We are not telling the reader that the answer to a structural problem is a sufficient dose of individual virtue, because we believe that answer is false and we have said so in every chapter of this book. What we are telling the reader is that structural problems, when they cannot be addressed at their own scale, have to be addressed at the scales where action is actually possible, and then those actions have to be aggregated, slowly, into the pressure that eventually moves the structure. This is how almost every durable democratic reform in the history of the countries we inhabit has actually happened. It is the only pattern with any track record. We are not arguing that it is easy. We are arguing that it is the thing to do.

The public sphere has been shattered. It has not been abolished. What a public sphere is — a space in which private persons assemble as a public and reason together about the matters the state is doing in their name — is a thing human beings are capable of making, when they set their minds to making it. They have made it before, under worse conditions than ours in many respects. They can make it again. The question is whether we will.

We think we can. The rest of this volume, and the volume that follows, is our attempt to say how.


---

# Chapter 14
## The Tyranny of Merit and the Hollowing of Work

A diagnosis that stops at the social and the psychological — at the lonely neighbor, the vanished bowling league, the platformed congregation, the partisan stacking of identities, the algorithmic prism — is a diagnosis that has not yet reached the ground. The accounts we have worked through in the last six chapters are true and indispensable. Affective polarization is real. Social capital has collapsed. Institutions have been hollowed into stages. The public sphere has been shattered into feeds. Each of these has its own causal story, and each has furnished a literature of its own. But underneath them, and older than most of them, is a transformation of the American economy and the American status order that neither the classical liberal right nor the therapeutic center-left has fully faced. The populist revolt now coursing through American politics — in its right-wing nationalist form and in its left-wing revolutionary form, so often mistaken for opposites — is at bottom a revolt against that transformation. A book that does not engage it is a book for the credentialed about the credentialed. We have tried, so far, not to write that book. This chapter is the place where we must try hardest.

The transformation has three faces, and three thinkers have given us the clearest view of each. Michael Sandel has written the definitive account of what has happened to merit: how the language of earned success, once an instrument of democratic self-respect, has curdled into a license to humiliate those who did not make it. Christopher Lasch, writing thirty years ago and seeing further than almost anyone, described the secession of the American professional class from the common life it once shared with its fellow citizens, and the resentments that secession would eventually unleash. Sohrab Ahmari, writing from the post-liberal right but drawing on a tradition the right has mostly lost, has made the case that the coercion most Americans experience in their daily lives comes not from the government but from concentrated economic power — from the employer who can fire at will, the landlord who can evict, the platform that can deplatform, the insurer that can deny. Read together, these three books map a terrain on which the master diagnoses of the previous chapters rest. The atomized person of Arendt is often also an economically precarious person. The frustrated person of Hoffer is often also a person whose work has been stripped of dignity and whose community has been stripped of jobs. The mass-movement susceptibility we have been tracking is not evenly distributed across the American map. It is highest where economic dispossession and cultural humiliation overlap. We will not understand the present crisis, or the shape the response must take, until we have looked carefully at that overlap.

\*\*\*

Begin with merit. Sandel's central claim in *The Tyranny of Merit*, published in 2020, is that the most cherished civic ideal in American life — that positions should go to those with the talent and the effort to earn them — has a dark side that has only now become fully visible. The ideal itself sounds unimpeachable. It is, for two hundred years, what Americans have told themselves about the kind of country they live in: a place where you are not shackled by the rank of your parents, where hard work lifts the capable regardless of birth, where every child can aspire to any station. The rhetoric has been bipartisan and near-universal. From Horatio Alger through the mid-century consensus to the "you can be anything you want to be" catechism of late-twentieth-century schooling, the promise of merit has been the American faith.

Sandel does not set out to demolish the faith. He sets out to show what happens when it becomes the dominant, perhaps the sole, public language for describing why some people live well and others do not. Under the pure meritocratic picture, a successful person has earned her place by the legitimate exercise of her talents and effort, and by nothing else. A person at the bottom, by the same logic, must be there by the failure or absence of talent and effort, and by nothing else. This is the crucial move. Meritocracy, in its purest form, is not only an account of why the winners win. It is an account of why the losers lose. And the account is moralizing. The loser is not unfortunate, not unlucky, not blameless; he is insufficient. His place in the order reflects, with a completeness that aristocracy at least had the decency to deny, the measure of his worth as a person.

This is not, Sandel is careful to note, merely a philosophical danger. It is not only a potential misreading of a doctrine that, properly understood, would not entail it. The empirical evidence that Americans have absorbed exactly this moralizing account is abundant and grim. Surveys going back decades show that Americans are unusually willing, among citizens of rich democracies, to attribute poverty to personal failing and wealth to personal virtue. The attribution has grown stronger, not weaker, during the period in which actual mobility has stagnated. The divergence between the inherited rhetoric of opportunity and the measured reality of an increasingly hereditary elite has produced not disillusionment with the rhetoric but an intensification of its moralizing. If the system says that winners deserve to win, and if winners and losers increasingly reproduce themselves across generations, then the moralized conclusion is that some families have figured out how to deserve, and others have not.

The humiliation this inflicts on the bottom two-thirds of the American educational distribution is Sandel's central theme. Americans without four-year college degrees — which is to say, most Americans — have become progressively more alienated from the institutions, the language, and the politics of the credentialed class over the past forty years. They experience themselves as treated with contempt, and the evidence is that they are not imagining it. Listen to how the credentialed speak, in public and in private, about the non-credentialed. Listen to the affect of the evening news anchor describing a rural county; the easy scorn of the late-night comedian; the therapeutic condescension of the professional class explaining, to each other, what the other America is really upset about. The rhetoric of "just go to college" as universal advice — the implicit, and sometimes explicit, suggestion that anyone not holding a credential has failed to accept the basic responsibility of modern life — is a language of moral ranking. It lands harder on its targets than its speakers realize.

Sandel's argument here is not that elite condescension is ill-mannered. It is that it is the visible surface of a transformation in how Americans think about each other. In the pre-meritocratic democratic ideal, a citizen was a citizen — a person of standing in the public life of a republic, whose dignity was not a function of his occupation or his credential but of his membership in the political community. The industrial worker and the accountant shared the same standing in the polity and, often, the same pew, the same bleachers, the same union hall or lodge or PTA. Under the meritocratic regime, standing has been decoupled from citizenship and rebound to achievement. The accountant and the industrial worker no longer share a public. They share, at best, a consumer market. And the accountant has been instructed, by every cultural signal available to him, that his credential confers on him a kind of moral distance from the worker. The language he has been taught to use about the worker's politics, the worker's religion, the worker's diet, the worker's entertainments, is a language of clinical concern laced with derision. This is the diversity-but-not-class professional class, whose inclusiveness along the axes it recognizes is matched by its blindness to the axis that most shapes the lives of those it claims to champion.

Sandel's proposal at the end of the book is modest in tone and radical in implication. It is not a scheme to end meritocracy by fiat. It is a call to rehabilitate the dignity of non-elite work; to reform higher education so that it is no longer the sorting mechanism by which an American becomes eligible or ineligible for public respect; to recover a politics of the common good that does not treat upward mobility as the single, universal metric of a successful life. Behind these proposals lies a deeper claim: that the loss of the pre-meritocratic civic dignity — the standing a person held as a citizen irrespective of his achievements — is the wound around which much of our current politics is organized, and that recovering any version of that dignity will require dismantling the parts of the meritocratic rhetoric that have been used, for forty years, to justify treating fellow citizens as lesser.

We would add one thing to Sandel's account, which is that the humiliation is not confined to the bottom. It travels up. The children of the credentialed are not, by any measure the data can capture, happier than the children of the non-credentialed; their rates of anxiety, depression, and despair are if anything higher. A system that makes the winners prove, over and over, that they deserve their winning is a system that makes the winners miserable. The striving becomes permanent; the resume, infinite; the self, an ongoing audit. This is the joyless, vigilant, self-branding soul that Yuval Levin and others have described so well from another angle. Sandel's point is that it is the same soul, in its upper register, that produces — in its lower register — the person who experiences his life as an unjust verdict. The regime that humiliates the loser also exhausts the winner. Neither half is free.

\*\*\*

Lasch saw the structural precondition of all of this before most observers were ready to believe it was happening. *The Revolt of the Elites and the Betrayal of Democracy*, published in 1995 from the thin edge of Lasch's remaining years, is one of the prescient books of the American twentieth century, and its prescience is now almost embarrassing to recover. Lasch was a man of the left. He had spent his intellectual life criticizing consumer capitalism, the therapeutic state, and the narcissistic self that the two produced. He was not, in any sense, a partisan of the people against the experts. But he came to believe, by the early 1990s, that the most important political fact of his generation was the secession of the American professional class from the common life of the republic, and that the consequences of that secession — whenever they arrived — would be severe.

The argument is worth restating in its plainest form. Democracy as a form of life, Lasch held, rests on something more than the periodic exercise of the franchise. It rests on elites — managerial, professional, cultural — participating in a shared common life with non-elites. It rests on the doctor and the patient, the journalist and the reader, the professor and the neighbor, the general and the recruit, having lived in the same neighborhoods, attended the same schools, fought the same wars, worshipped in the same churches, belonged to the same civic organizations. Not always, not perfectly, not without grave exclusions that a more just country would correct. But enough that the elites of a republic knew their fellow citizens by face and by first name, and that the non-elites had continuous, unmediated experience of the people making the decisions that shaped their lives.

By the last quarter of the twentieth century, Lasch argued, that condition was dissolving. The late-twentieth-century professional class — in finance, in law, in technology, in media, in higher education, in the federal and state bureaucracies — was withdrawing from the shared life into a set of enclaves of its own making. These enclaves were geographical (the affluent suburbs, and later the self-consciously urban neighborhoods, of the credentialed coasts); educational (the increasingly stratified public school system and the parallel private school system that the credentialed opted into for their children); cultural (the coherent bundle of entertainments, diets, parenting practices, and moral vocabularies by which the professional class recognized itself and distinguished itself from those outside); and political (the steady capture of party apparatuses by the college-educated wings of both coalitions). The net result was a class that lived among its own, educated its children among its own, met its spouses among its own, worked among its own, and governed, from a distance, a country whose daily life it no longer shared.

Lasch's critique of this class is what is hardest, and most necessary, to reproduce accurately. He did not argue that the professional class was too generous with the poor, or that it was insufficiently patriotic, or that it despised traditional values. He argued that it was performing a form of concern for Americans it no longer actually knew. The therapeutic vocabulary by which the credentialed spoke about the working class — as traumatized, as needing education, as suffering from false consciousness, as requiring expert intervention — was, Lasch held, a mode of distancing. It replaced the older democratic habit of regarding fellow citizens as interlocutors, capable of speaking for themselves and of being argued with, with a newer professional habit of regarding them as patients, cases, or constituencies. The effect on those so described was corrosive. They could feel themselves being studied rather than addressed. They could feel that the concern shown them was real in some sense, but was not the concern of a neighbor.

Lasch predicted — and this is the part that now reads as prophecy — that the populist reaction to this condition would be fierce, would arrive within a generation or two, and would take forms the secessionist class would find both incomprehensible and morally outrageous. He predicted that the professional class's response to the populist reaction would be to double down on the therapeutic frame: to describe the populism as a pathology, to look for its causes in the deficient education or moral formation of those caught up in it, to propose corrective interventions designed and administered by the same class against whose secession the populism was in fact a response. He did not live to see this cycle play out. But anyone reading him now can see it in the page's margin. The mutual incomprehension that is the leading feature of American politics in the third decade of the twenty-first century is, in significant part, the late return on the social check that the professional class wrote against its own country in the final decades of the twentieth.

It is important to say what Lasch was not saying, because his argument has been hijacked by writers who do not share his premises. He was not saying that populist prescriptions were correct. He was not saying that the instinct to cashier the experts was a sound one, or that the institutions of the modern state could be dispensed with, or that democracy was better served by people who read fewer books. His point was civic and relational. Democracy requires elites who share a life with non-elites; a republic whose elites have seceded from the common life of their fellow citizens is a republic in which the legitimacy of the elites has already begun to erode. Recovering the legitimacy requires not better messaging or more skilled therapeutic outreach, but the partial undoing of the secession itself. That is not a problem policy can solve, and it is not a problem technique can solve. It is a problem about where one lives, where one sends one's children, what civic organizations one joins, and whom one considers to be one's fellow in the work of common life.

\*\*\*

If Sandel has described the moral humiliation produced by meritocracy and Lasch has described the civic abandonment produced by the secession of the elites, Sohrab Ahmari has done something the American right had, for a generation, been reluctant to do: he has taken concentrated economic power seriously as a source of unfreedom. *Tyranny, Inc.*, published in 2023, is in some respects a difficult book for us to make use of. Ahmari is a post-liberal and a cultural conservative, and he stands against several of the positions we defend elsewhere in this book. We will not pretend otherwise. But we cannot steelman the diagnosis we need to steelman without admitting that on the question of where coercion most often comes from in ordinary American life, Ahmari has seen more clearly than much of the tradition to which he belongs — and more clearly, on this question, than much of the tradition to which we belong.

The central claim of *Tyranny, Inc.* is that for most Americans most of the time, the actor whose decisions most directly circumscribe their freedom is not the state. It is the employer. It is the landlord. It is the creditor. It is the health insurance company. It is the platform monopoly that owns the interface between the worker and his customers, or between the citizen and his neighbors. These actors wield the kind of arbitrary power over the persons subject to them that, in the classical republican vocabulary we worked through in Volume I, is the paradigm of unfreedom. Their decisions can end a paycheck, end a tenancy, end a treatment, end an account, end a career. The person on the receiving end of those decisions has, in most cases, no effective recourse. He has the theoretical right to walk away — to find another employer, another landlord, another insurer, another platform — but the asymmetry of information, the concentration of the market, the dependency of his livelihood on the relationship that controls him, ensures that the walking-away is available to him only at severe cost. He lives, in short, at the mercy of another's will. That is what the classical republicans called domination, and what contemporary classical liberals have had the hardest time seeing.

Ahmari's analytical move, which distinguishes him from standard conservative commentary and from standard libertarian commentary alike, is to insist that contract law — the mechanism by which most of these relationships are formally governed — presupposes a symmetry of bargaining power that no longer obtains in most of the cases it is asked to regulate. The "freedom of contract" story, foundational to the classical-liberal account of market relations, imagines two parties bargaining more or less as equals over the terms under which they will exchange value. In contemporary America, Ahmari argues, this story is a fiction when applied to most workers, most tenants, most consumers of essential services, most borrowers, and most users of platform monopolies. The worker does not negotiate his terms of employment with his employer; he accepts the terms the employer offers, because the alternative to acceptance is the loss of his income, and because the employer can replace him much more easily than he can replace the employer. The tenant does not negotiate his rent; he accepts the rent the landlord demands, because the cost and disruption of moving make the theoretical option of refusal practically unavailable. The user of the platform monopoly does not negotiate its terms of service; he clicks through them unread, because he has no alternative that would reach his customers, his friends, his audience, or his employment.

This is not the language in which the American right, outside a few dissident corners, has trained itself to speak about economic life. For several generations, the right's master story has been that the state is the threat to freedom and the market is its guarantor. There is a truth in that story — the state has an instrument of last resort, the monopoly on legal violence, that private actors do not. But as a complete account of where the threats to a given American's freedom actually come from in a given week of his life, the story is radically incomplete. For most Americans most of the time, the decisions that most directly constrain their lives are made not in Washington but in the HR office, the leasing office, the claims department, the compliance committee of a distant corporation. Ahmari's achievement is to have said this in a voice the right can hear. Whether he is heard is another matter.

The prescriptions he draws from this diagnosis would, forty years ago, have been described as left-wing. He endorses sectoral bargaining on the European model, an expansion of labor rights that would give workers real collective voice in the terms of their employment. He endorses a more aggressive antitrust stance against the concentrated platforms and the consolidated industries, drawing on the trust-busting tradition American progressives and Republicans once shared. He endorses stronger legal protections for tenants and debtors, limiting the arbitrary power of landlords and creditors to disrupt ordinary lives. We will not, in this volume, adjudicate these proposals one by one. Some we find persuasive; some we find too blunt; some belong to debates that are beyond the scope of this book. Our purpose here is different. Our purpose is to take the diagnosis — that most Americans experience their unfreedom as arising from economic rather than governmental actors, that the symmetry-of-contract fiction is a poor description of their bargaining position, and that the condition this produces is a kind of domination the older republican vocabulary was built to name — and to set it alongside Sandel's diagnosis of merit and Lasch's diagnosis of secession. The three together describe something none of them alone describes, and it is that composite picture we now need to see.

\*\*\*

The composite is uglier than any of its parts. It is the condition in which the merit rhetoric tells the bottom of the distribution that their failure is their own fault; in which the market's actual distribution of rewards has, over the same four decades, skewed so steeply that being at the bottom of the distribution has become materially harsher than it was within living memory; and in which the class that has pulled away from the rest has simultaneously retreated from the common life in which it might once have been called to account for the condition of its neighbors. Merit plus market plus secession equals humiliation — a specific, distinctive, historically novel form of humiliation in which economic decline is accompanied by moral blame and by the mannered condescension of a class that is doing fine.

This is not the condition of the medieval peasant. It is not the condition of the nineteenth-century industrial worker. Those earlier forms of hardship were accompanied by a public doctrine that explained, to the person at the bottom, why he was at the bottom: inheritance, providence, fortune, the structure of the world. Whatever else one thinks of those doctrines, they did not require the person suffering under them to regard his suffering as the verdict of his own insufficiency. The modern form is different. The modern form tells the suffering person that he is suffering because he has been weighed on a fair scale and found wanting. It then adds insult to injury by having the weighers — the people whose favored station was delivered to them by the same scale — speak about the weighed in the clinical, euphemistic, slightly pained voice of professionals describing a difficult case.

It is this condition, more than any of the conditions we have described in previous chapters, that supplies the reservoir from which mass movements in contemporary America draw their supporters. Arendt's lonely atomized individual is not, in twenty-first-century America, merely a person who lives by himself in a studio apartment and has no one to call. He is often a person who is also materially precarious — working in a gig job that provides no health insurance and no retirement contribution, living in a community whose economic base has been hollowed out over three decades, receiving medical care through an insurer whose logic is opaque to him and whose customer service function is designed to exhaust his will to appeal. Hoffer's "frustrated" — the person who experiences his life as irredeemably spoiled, who is therefore ready to trade his individual identity for membership in a collective that will give him one — is not a random type who shows up with roughly equal frequency across the American landscape. He is concentrated, in specific places and in specific demographics. The concentration is not random. It tracks deindustrialization. It tracks educational stratification. It tracks the patterns of wealth concentration of the past forty years, which have moved resources and opportunity from broad geographies and demographics into narrow ones. The mass-movement susceptibility is highest where the economic and the cultural humiliations overlap — where a person lives in a place that has been stripped of its work and told, in the same breath, that the stripping was the unfortunate but necessary consequence of the working of a fair system.

The populists of the current moment have understood this, if unevenly and often in demagogic form. The right-populist understands that the people he speaks for have been told they are losers on the meritocratic scale and that the losing is their fault, and he offers them a counter-story: you are not losers, the scale is rigged, your loss is the consequence of a betrayal and the betrayers can be named. The left-populist understands that the workers she speaks for are being squeezed by economic actors whose power is not adequately checked, and she offers them a counter-story: you are not insufficient, the system is predatory, and the predators must be broken. Both of these counter-stories contain a real diagnostic kernel. Each is also partial; each is susceptible to the demagogic temptation that mass movements always hold out to leaders who discover a reservoir of the frustrated; each can curdle, and in several places on the contemporary scene is curdling, into the familiar Hofferian shape in which the movement promises the individual that he can cease to be himself if he will only lose himself in the collective. The populist prescriptions may be wrong in their specifics and dangerous in their drift. The grievance they are organized around is real. Mistaking the one for the other — treating the grievance as pathology because the prescription is pathology, or treating the prescription as justified because the grievance is justified — is the characteristic error of commentary that will not stay on the terrain long enough to tell the two apart.

\*\*\*

We have not yet said what we most need to say about work. The economic dimension of the crisis is not only a story about distribution, though it is also a story about distribution. It is a story about what has happened to the idea that work is a calling, a form of service, a contribution to a shared life, a fit object for a person's pride and the basis for his standing in his community. The decline of that idea — the vocational ethic, in the older religious vocabulary, or the craft ethic, in the older secular one — has been steep, and it has been steepest in the jobs that used to carry the heaviest vocational weight and have since been "rationalized" into platform-gig relationships.

Consider what has happened to driving for a living over the past fifteen years. The taxi driver, the trucker, the delivery driver of the pre-platform era had a job. It was often difficult and sometimes dangerous; it paid modestly; it was not, in most cases, glamorous. But it was in its form a job. The worker had an employer. The employer had a duty, backed by law and often by union contract, to provide him with certain conditions of labor. The worker had a relationship with a dispatcher or a regional manager whom he could, in principle, speak to about his working life. He had coworkers he saw regularly. He had a recognizable place in the economic ecology of his city or region, and that place was visible to his neighbors, who could name him and his work. He could teach his son or daughter what he did and why, and the son or daughter could see a path by which they might do something similar, or better. The work was, in the most ordinary sense of the word, dignified.

The platformed successor of this work is not dignified in the same sense, or often in any sense. The worker is not an employee but an "independent contractor" — a category designed specifically to relieve the platform of the duties an employer would owe an employee. There is no dispatcher he can speak to, no manager whose office he can walk into. The algorithm that assigns him work is invisible to him, as is the algorithm that rates him and decides whether to keep giving him work. The relationship has been engineered to extract as much of his labor as possible at as low a marginal cost as the system can sustain, while giving him as little purchase on the relationship as the law can be made to tolerate. He can, in theory, leave. He cannot, in practice, change the terms. He does not know his coworkers; there are no coworkers; there is only an expanding and contracting pool of strangers, each also driving, each also rated. He has difficulty teaching his children what he does, because what he does is barely a what. He is a node in a network whose purposes lie elsewhere. His neighbors cannot easily name his work because the work has been engineered to be named by nothing but its platform. The vocational substance of the older job has been drained from the newer one, and what has been left in its place is thin.

This is one example, a vivid one. It is not unique. It is a particular case of a general transformation that has hollowed many middle-skill occupations — retail, clerical, warehousing, many forms of service — into thinner, more surveilled, more algorithmically managed, less relational, less narratively coherent forms of labor than the jobs they replaced. It is also the particular case that David Graeber, from a different angle, was describing in his book on what he called "bullshit jobs." Graeber's phenomenon — the experience, reported in his survey data at disturbingly high rates, that one's work is pointless, ritualized, or actively harmful — was, interestingly, not confined to the bottom of the occupational distribution. It was in fact most intense among white-collar workers with ostensibly good jobs: compliance officers whose compliance accomplished nothing, middle managers whose management managed nothing, consultants whose consultations were unread. The vocational hollowing, in other words, is not only a problem for those at the bottom. It has crept up the ladder. The credentialed class who are, on the meritocratic story, the winners are in large numbers reporting that they suspect their winning has no content.

Behind this is a story the political economy of the past forty years has told itself, and told the rest of us, with great confidence. The story is that the disappearance of the middle-skill jobs of the older industrial economy was unfortunate but necessary, that the displaced workers should retrain for the better jobs the new economy would create, that the retraining infrastructure would be there, and that the new jobs would be better — more interesting, better paid, more flexibly scheduled — than the ones they replaced. We are now forty years into this story, and it is time to notice that large portions of it have not come true. The retraining infrastructure is in most places rudimentary or absent. The jobs that have been created for the non-credentialed are, on several dimensions that matter to the people working them, worse than the ones they replaced: less stable, less relational, less visible, less amenable to collective voice, less tied to a place, less teachable to children. There are exceptions; there are individual workers whose lives have improved; there are sectors where the story has worked roughly as promised. But there are many more places where it has not, and the aggregate effect on American civic life of a generation of unkept promises on this scale is not small. We should not be surprised that people whose lives were restructured on the basis of a story are unhappy when the story turns out to have been partly untrue.

\*\*\*

We should say, because this book is built to take counterarguments seriously, what the strongest versions of the response to the argument we have been developing look like. Two are worth naming.

The first is the argument from the numbers: that things are in fact not so bad as the argument implies, that real wages are up on most measures, that the rhetoric of economic despair outstrips the data, that this is another case of vibes overtaking evidence. There is real force in this objection. On several standard measures, median real incomes in the United States have risen over the past thirty years, in some periods faster than before. Consumption of many goods has expanded. Technology has brought new capacities to ordinary lives that older generations could not have dreamed of. It would be dishonest to claim that the American worker of today is materially worse off across the board than his grandfather was. He is not.

But the standard measures do not capture the relevant transformation. The composition of consumption has shifted in ways that make the traditional pathways to adult stability — a house one can afford on a median income, a family one can raise on that house, a community one can afford to stay in because one's work and one's neighbors and one's congregation are all within reach — systematically harder to attain, even as nominal incomes rise. Housing, healthcare, education, and childcare — the four major expenditures that stand between the median worker and the stable adult life the older economy made accessible to him — have risen much faster than the prices of consumer electronics and inexpensive clothing. One can own a cheaper television and a more expensive life. The argument that real wages are up, taken alone, counts the television and misses the life. It also misses the civic point. The complaint is not only that the numbers have gone the wrong way. It is that the structure of the lived economy — the path from a first job to a household, from a household to a community, from a community to a stable adulthood — has been disrupted even where the numbers look acceptable, and that the disruption is what the grievance is actually about.

The second objection is the charge of nostalgia. It runs roughly this way: the argument we have been making is a dressed-up yearning for a lost mid-century industrial America, for factory jobs with union cards and pensions and wives at home. The yearning, the objection continues, is both politically unavailable — that America is not coming back, nor could it be made to — and morally compromised, given the sex-, race-, and class-based exclusions on which the stability of that earlier arrangement rested. To argue for its return is to argue, perhaps unwittingly, for the return of its injustices.

We take this objection seriously, and we want to say clearly what we are not arguing and what we are. We are not arguing for a return to mid-century industrial America. We are not arguing for a return to the coverture-shaped household, the racially segregated workplace, the closed shop whose closure was in part a function of whom it excluded. The injustices of that arrangement were real, and their partial dismantling in the second half of the twentieth century was moral progress. What we are arguing — and we think Sandel, Lasch, and Ahmari in their different voices are each arguing — is something more limited and more future-oriented. It is that whatever economy we build next must include forms of work that offer dignity, voice, and security to the non-credentialed majority of Americans. No economic arrangement that leaves most of its participants feeling disposable is politically sustainable in a democracy, whatever its numbers look like on aggregate measures. The task is not to recover the forms of the mid-century economy but to recover its best features — its broadly shared dignity of labor, its accessible pathway to adult stability, its local rootedness — in a form that does not require the exclusions on which its historical instance partly rested. That is hard. It is not impossible. It is, in any case, the task the present demands.
