The alternative — a frictionless labor market topped by a credentialed winners' class presiding, at a distance, over a managed servitoriat — is a political arrangement no self-respecting republic can long endure. The populism now eating our creators is the first visible symptom of what it will not endure.

\*\*\*

We can now return to the master argument and see where this chapter has brought us. We said in the opening of Volume II that the disease afflicting the American citizen has multiple faces. We promised to examine each of the leading accounts — the mass-movement diagnosis, the social-capital diagnosis, the institutional-decay diagnosis, the polarization diagnosis, the public-sphere diagnosis — and to add, at the end, the one the first five leave underplayed. That is what this chapter has done. The material-cultural dimension has to be put on the table and its relation to the others has to be specified.

The relation is not competition. It is composition. Atomization, mass formation, institutional decay, polarization, the shattered public sphere, and the merit-and-market humiliation we have examined here are not six separate diseases afflicting the same patient. They are the symptoms of a civic order that has been eaten from several directions at once by a common logic — the logic that reduces persons to individuals-as-consumers, and then blames the individuals when the lives they end up with do not satisfy. The lonely person and the humiliated person are often the same person. The platformed citizen and the precarious worker are often the same citizen. The member of the affectively polarized tribe is often someone whose tribe has, among its other functions, the function of answering the humiliation with pride — of telling him, in a vocabulary of resentment or revolution, that the verdict rendered on him by the merit regime is false. Each of these aspects of the crisis feeds every other. They are not sequential. They are simultaneous, and they reinforce.

This is why the responses we have seen offered in recent years — each picking up a single strand of the composite disease, each pulling on it as if pulling on it alone would untangle the whole — have fallen short. The response that targets social capital alone is real but insufficient; the response that targets the algorithms alone is real but insufficient; the response that targets economic inequality alone is real but insufficient; the response that targets cultural decadence alone is real but insufficient. The disease is composite. The response must be composite as well.

We must also say what this chapter does not, by itself, tell us how to do. We are diagnostic writers in Volume II; we are not yet prescriptive. It would be the final irony of a book about the pathologies of the present to end its diagnostic volume by offering a policy paper. That is not the shape of the response we believe is required, and it is not the shape Volume III will take. The managerial temptation — the idea that we simply need better policies designed by better experts and we will be fine — is precisely one of the temptations Lasch warned against; it is the therapeutic reflex of the class whose secession produced the crisis. The revolutionary temptation — the idea that the system is beyond repair and must be broken so that something better can be built on the wreckage — is precisely the mass-movement reflex Arendt and Hoffer warned against. Both temptations are wrong for reasons this book has been developing from its first pages. We will not offer either in Volume III.

What we will offer, instead, is a program that operates at several scales at once — personal, local, institutional, and national — and on all the faces of the composite disease simultaneously. We will begin from the recognition that this is a task for citizens, which is what the book has been arguing throughout. The merit-and-market humiliation cannot be dispelled by a gift from the humiliators; it can only be dispelled by the reconstitution of a civic life in which persons are regarded as fellow citizens before they are regarded as economic achievers. The secession of the elites cannot be reversed by a declaration; it can only be reversed, if it can be reversed, by deliberate choices of where to live, how to educate one's children, what associations to join, whom to regard as a fellow. The economic domination of ordinary Americans cannot be ended by a speech; it requires the slow rebuilding of countervailing powers — labor, community, local government, mutual-aid associations, real political parties — that the atomizing logic of the past half-century has drained. The atomization cannot be reversed by the mere publication of a book; the recovery of a public life requires citizens willing to undertake the practices of one. And the mass-movement susceptibility that follows from all of the above cannot be dispelled by denouncing the cults; it can only be dispelled by rebuilding the local, associational, vocational, and civic goods whose absence makes the cults attractive in the first place.

That program is what Volume III sketches. It will not be a single policy. It will not be a single movement. It will not be a single cultural turn. It will be a set of interlocking practices, some personal, some local, some institutional, some political, each modest on its own, each pointing toward a common direction, all informed by the composite diagnosis we have now completed. We will draw on the growing empirical literature on what actually works to depolarize and to rebuild civic life; we will draw on the philosophical foundations Volume I laid down; and we will draw on the long American tradition of civic renewal from below, of which the Progressive Era, the civil rights movement, and the mutualist and cooperative traditions are only the most visible instances. None of these was begun by the humiliators. All were begun by citizens, under conditions worse than ours, who believed that the republic was worth the labor of its restoration.

We close Volume II on the hardest of the diagnostic chapters because the dignity of the non-credentialed American is the place where our current civic failure is most visible, most undeniable, and most politically consequential. No recovery of the citizen that does not address the merit regime, the secession of the elites, and the hollowing of work is a recovery that will reach the Americans who most need to be reached. A book of this kind, addressed to the citizen, cannot afford to speak over their heads. It must speak to them as fellows, as equals in the work of the common thing. That is the register in which the final volume will be written, and it is the register in which this book has tried, from the first page, to earn the right to be read.

We began the diagnostic volume with a puzzle: why, in the wealthiest and safest moment in human history, has American public life turned hollow, hostile, and unserious. We have, over seven chapters, tried to show that the puzzle has a composite answer, and that the final piece of the answer is the transformation of work and merit and status that the previous chapters underplayed. We have argued that the lonely, the furious, the platformed, the polarized, and the humiliated are often the same person. We have argued that the mass-movement susceptibility the book has tracked is not a distant threat but a condition already acting on the American polity, concentrated in the places where the composite disease has done its worst work. And we have argued that any response adequate to the situation must be as multi-dimensional as the situation itself — must work at multiple scales, on multiple faces, over multiple years, by the hands of actual citizens.

That response is the subject of the book's third and final volume. It is a response that will be neither managerial nor revolutionary. It will ask more of the reader than either, and it will offer, we think, more in return. The citizen whose return the book has been arguing for is not a figure of nostalgia. The citizen is the only figure under whose aegis the public life of a modern republic can be put back together. Everything the first two volumes have tried to establish has been a clearing of the ground for the argument that begins in the pages that follow. The diagnosis ends here. The work of recovery begins.


---

# VOLUME III
## The Citizen Revitalized

*Solutions: what individuals, institutions, and societies can actually do.*

---

# Chapter 15
## Why Most Solutions Fail

We have arrived at the volume that is hardest to write. The two volumes behind us asked what the citizen is and what has happened to him. The volume in front of us has to say what can be done. This is the volume in which books like ours almost always fail, and we think the reader deserves to know, before we start, why they fail and how we are going to try not to.

The failure is a genre failure, and it has a recognizable shape. An author spends three hundred careful pages demonstrating that the problem is deep, structural, civilizational, tangled in the weave of modern life. She convinces us. She earns our assent by the weight and specificity of her diagnosis. Then, in the last chapter, a different voice takes over. The sentences get shorter. The examples get smaller. The confidence goes up and the texture goes out. We are told to talk to our neighbors, to form a reading group, to pass a campaign finance bill, to log off of one app and log on to another, to flee to a farmhouse with the like-minded. The book ends on a note of either frantic optimism or displaced piety, and we put it down with the uncomfortable feeling that the author ran out of the thing she had plenty of two hundred pages ago — namely, the willingness to look the situation in the face.

We have put down a number of those books. We intend not to be one.

The first honest step is to name the three stock moves the genre tends to make in its closing act, and to say plainly why none of them is sufficient.

The first is the platitude. "Have a civil conversation with someone who disagrees with you." "Listen to understand rather than to reply." "Recognize the humanity of those on the other side." We want to be careful here, because each of these sentences is true, and each of them describes something a renewed citizen would in fact do. They are not wrong. They are inadequate. They are inadequate because they ask of an individual what is in fact the result of institutional conditions, and because they do not tell her how to do the thing they are asking her to do, or why, with overwhelming probability, her first few attempts will fail. Most Americans, given a weekend and a well-meaning book, cannot have a civil conversation across the partisan divide, and not because they are bad people. They cannot because they have not been formed by institutions that teach the specific disciplines such a conversation requires — the patience to let a sentence finish, the willingness to grant the opposing claim its strongest version, the habit of treating one's own certainty as itself a datum to be examined. These disciplines are not personality traits. They are practices. And practices, as every serious tradition has known, require structures — apprenticeships, role models, rituals, iteration, correction. A book that ends with "be more open-minded" has skipped over the question of where open-mindedness as a practice is supposed to come from in a society whose dominant institutions reward the opposite. The platitude is not false. It is a sermon delivered to people who have no church.

The second stock move is the policy wishlist. Every author of a book like this has, by the final chapter, a drawer full of favored reforms. Change the voting system. Break up the platforms. Ban the targeted ad. Mandate the civics curriculum. Overhaul the primary process. Pass the disclosure rule. Reinstate the fairness doctrine. Require, forbid, restructure, subsidize. We have our own drawer; every reader does. Some of these proposals are good. Some would almost certainly help. None of them is a solution the reader of the book can implement, and that is the first problem. The second problem is worse. As Volume II argued at some length, the political system that would have to adopt these reforms is the political system whose incentives produced the pathology in the first place. A legislature dominated by the logic of the platform is not going to pass a reform that lessens the pay-off to platform behavior. A media economy that depends on outrage is not going to enact rules against its own dependency. A party whose coalition is built on affective polarization is not going to legislate against it. To say "here is what Congress should do" is, under present conditions, roughly to say "here is what Congress will not do." It is an intellectually respectable exercise, but it is not a solution. It is a bet on conditions that do not obtain.

There is a further problem with the policy-wishlist ending, which is that it tends, by a strange coincidence, to recommend the exact set of reforms that would advantage the author's own side. The neutral observer notices that the author of the progressive book about polarization thinks the answer is proportional representation, the conservative book thinks the answer is subsidiarity and federalism, and the populist book thinks the answer is antitrust enforcement against the other guy's industry. Each policy set is, of course, defensible. Each, as presented, is also indistinguishable from a platform plank. The reader who is not already in the author's camp is not persuaded. The reader who is already in the author's camp does not need the book. The wishlist ending is, in practice, a way of selling a partisan agenda under a bipartisan cover, and thoughtful readers can tell. It poisons the project of civic renewal by making it look like one more weapon in the war that civic renewal is supposed to end.

The third stock move is the retreat. This is the most intellectually serious of the three, and therefore the one that requires the most care to criticize. It is associated, in its various forms, with a number of thinkers we respect: Rod Dreher's Benedict Option, the more ascetic strains of Wendell Berry, certain lines of Alasdair MacIntyre, the post-liberal longing for a return to particular communities with particular goods. The argument is that the polity is too far gone, that the work of maintaining a decent public life under present conditions is not worth doing, and that the honorable response is to withdraw — to the family, to the congregation, to the small intentional community, to the plot of land — and to keep the flame lit there while the darkness does its work outside.

There is something in this that we honor. There is an honesty in admitting that one cannot fix everything, and a dignity in tending carefully what one can tend. The person who quietly raises a good family, keeps faithful company with a congregation, does honest work, and does not look to politics for meaning is not failing at citizenship. She is, in some deep sense, practicing it. A polity of such people would be one we would be glad to live in.

But retreat cannot be the program of a polity. It can be a choice within one. A civilization that retreats collectively from its public life does not survive long enough to see whether the retreat succeeded, because the public life, abandoned, is inherited by the worst elements willing to occupy it. The monasteries that preserved a civilization during the collapse of Rome did so because a new civilization eventually absorbed them; the individual monks were not running a political program, and their survival as a tradition depended on something other than the retreat itself. A contemporary version of the Benedict Option, proposed as *the* answer, cannot be that. It implicitly relies on the continuing existence of the polity it is fleeing, and it has nothing to say to the fact that the polity it is fleeing contains almost everyone the retreater loves, including the members of his own family who will not retreat with him. As a personal vocation, retreat is sometimes wise. As a civic program, it is a surrender dressed up as a virtue.

Those are the three stock moves, and those are the three reasons each of them, on its own, is not enough. We have taken time on them because we want the reader to see that we have read the books she has read, been disappointed by them in the same ways she has, and are not going to repeat their moves. If this chapter does nothing else, we want it to set an expectation. The rest of Volume III will not close with a platitude; it will not close with a policy wishlist; it will not close with a call to head for the hills.

***

What, then, is left?

The harder, more honest answer is that there are really only two categories of proposal that take the diagnosis of Volume II seriously, and that any serious program has to include both.

The first is what we will call localist renewal. The thinkers here are Robert Putnam, Yuval Levin, the thoughtful communitarians, a generation of practitioners whose names do not appear on bestseller lists because they run Little League programs and neighborhood councils and congregational committees. Their claim, amply supported by Putnam's data and by the whole Tocquevillian tradition, is that the bedrock of a free polity is a dense fabric of small, local, non-political, face-to-face associations — families, congregations, clubs, teams, guilds, professional associations, volunteer organizations, neighborhoods with knowable sidewalks — and that where this fabric is thick, people develop the dispositions a republic needs, and where it is thin, they do not. The prescription that follows is to rebuild the fabric. Join something. Start something. Show up. Take a seat at the table of an institution that forms people. The work is slow, small, and largely invisible. It does not photograph well. It does not score partisan points. It is what the Progressive Era, Putnam argues in *The Upswing*, was in fact built upon — three or four decades of civic invention beginning in the 1870s that laid the social groundwork for every political achievement that came later. The people who started it did not live to see its fruition.

This is a real program. It is not a platitude, because it is operationalized in specific institutions that have track records, and because it attends to the conditions — not just the exhortations — under which civic virtue gets formed. It takes the diagnosis of atomization seriously, because it rebuilds, at human scale, the associations whose disappearance the diagnosis mourns. We will draw on it heavily in the chapters that follow.

But localist renewal, by itself, is not enough. This has to be said, because a certain kind of communitarian writing suggests that it is, and the suggestion will not survive contact with the structural forces arrayed against the local. A neighborhood of kind, churchgoing, civically engaged families is still a neighborhood whose teenagers spend six hours a day on platforms engineered to maximize engagement through outrage. A congregation full of decent people is still a congregation whose members consume their politics from a media economy that profits from their mutual hatred. A Little League run by thoughtful parents is still a Little League in a country whose electoral system rewards candidates who perform grievance for a national audience. The local can be rebuilt, and still be swamped, if the structural conditions that are hollowing it out continue to operate at a larger scale. Putnam is right. He is not enough.

The second category we will call structural reform. The thinkers here include a somewhat different group: the institutionalist voices on the left who have been writing about platform capitalism, antitrust, labor law, and media concentration; Sohrab Ahmari in a different register on economic unfreedom; certain of the populist critics of the merit regime; a range of democratic reformers interested in the machinery of elections and deliberation. Their claim is that the structural incentives of late-modern American life — in the platform economy, the media economy, the campaign-finance system, the labor market, the electoral system, the monopoly structure of key industries — do so much of the work of producing the pathology we have diagnosed that no amount of local rebuilding will outrun them unless the structures themselves are altered. A civic renaissance in a country whose public discourse is organized by outrage-optimizing algorithms, whose political careers are funded by concentrated donor classes, whose primaries select for maximal partisan intensity, whose workers are pressed into unfreedom by concentrated employer power, is a civic renaissance being run uphill against a waterfall.

This, too, is a real program. It is closer to the scale of the problem than localist renewal alone. It takes seriously that there are causal structures, not just cultural habits, behind the atrophy of civic life. And it opens the possibility, at least in principle, of changes large enough to alter the gradient the localist has been running against.

But structural reform, by itself, also fails, and for a reason that mirrors the failure of localism. The structural reforms that would actually help require a political coalition capable of enacting them. That coalition does not currently exist, because the citizens who would have to form it have been formed — by the very conditions the reforms would address — into partisans incapable of recognizing common interests with one another. You cannot build the coalition for the structural fix without the civic substrate that the structural fix is supposed to create the conditions for. The structural reformer and the localist are each, separately, attempting to step onto a ladder whose other end the other is holding. Neither alone can climb out.

The honest answer, therefore, is both. It is not a compromise between the two, nor a split-the-difference synthesis. It is the recognition that they are not rival programs but complementary ones, and that a book which proposes only one is leaving out half of what is required. Putnam was right. Ahmari, on the points where he is right, is also right. The localist is right that no reform will take in a country whose citizens have been hollowed out, and the structural reformer is right that no amount of local rebuilding will withstand structural forces engineered in the opposite direction. The program we are going to propose, across the remaining chapters of this volume, will attempt to hold both, not because we are trying to please both camps but because neither camp's program, on its own, will work.

***

That is the high-level commitment. It produces a set of criteria for everything that comes after it, and we want to name those criteria here so that the reader can hold us accountable to them as we go.

The first criterion is that our proposals will be evidence-based where evidence is available. The single most exciting development in the literature of civic renewal over the last decade is that there is now an actual literature — randomized trials, megastudies, program evaluations — on what interventions actually reduce affective polarization and build civic capacity. The Strengthening Democracy Challenge, the Braver Angels evaluations, the perception-gap interventions, the deliberative-polling studies, the work of the Civic Health Project and the #ListenFirst coalition — these are real bodies of empirical work with measurable findings. Where a proposal of ours has been tested, we will cite the tests, including the null results. Where a proposal has not been tested but is consistent with the theoretical literature, we will say so. Where a proposal is frankly speculative, we will say that too. We have no interest in making our own intuitions sound more validated than they are.

The second criterion is that the program will be multi-scale. We will propose things an individual reader can do tomorrow, things she can organize or advocate for in her community this year, things that would require institutional buy-in over a decade, and things that would require political coalitions over a generation. Any book that proposes only one scale of action has, by that restriction, admitted its own inadequacy. The individualist book that tells you to think more critically and read more widely is not taking seriously the scale of the structural forces pressing against the individual. The national-policy book that tells you to pass a reform is not taking seriously that the reform will not pass and, even if it did, would not form a single citizen on its own. The citizen is formed and deformed at every scale simultaneously. The program has to operate at every scale simultaneously. We will say, at each proposal, what scale it addresses and why.

The third criterion is accessibility. Most of our proposals will be things a reader can in some fashion begin. Some will be things she can advocate for. A few will be addressed to those in positions of institutional leadership or political power, because some things genuinely cannot be done at any smaller scale. But we have tried to weight the program toward the actionable. We have tried not to put the center of gravity on what the government should do, both because the reader is mostly not the government and because a book whose main recommendation is what someone else should do is not really a book for the reader.

The fourth criterion is that the program will be non-utopian. We are not going to propose a perfect polity. We are not going to claim that if the reforms we describe were all adopted, America would become a republic of virtue. The proposals we will make are directional. They attempt to describe the direction in which things would have to move to become more habitable, without pretending to know the exact destination or the precise shape of the road. A program that promises the perfect answer is selling something. A program that describes a direction one can defend and the concrete next steps on it is offering something. We mean to offer.

The fifth criterion, and the one we think is most important and most often violated by books like this, is that our program cannot be partisan-coded. If the sum of the proposals, read together, sounds like the platform of one of the two American parties, we will have written the book wrong. The test is specific. A program of civic renewal that only progressives can endorse is not a program of civic renewal; it is a progressive policy agenda with civic-renewal branding. A program that only conservatives can endorse has the same problem in the other direction. The actual work the book is trying to do — the rebuilding of a shared civic life across the people who currently cannot live together — is definitionally impossible to do from inside one camp. We expect to recommend some things that will make readers on the left uncomfortable and some that will make readers on the right uncomfortable, and this will be the sign that we are trying. It is not our job to produce a comfortable book. It is our job to produce a usable one.

There is a corollary to this criterion we should name. The symmetry that makes the book non-partisan-coded also makes it impossible for us to be ideologically satisfying to either side. A reader who wants the book to be a weapon in her own faction's war will not find it. A reader who wants the book to validate the judgment that the other side is the cult and her side is the reasonable one will not find it, because that judgment is itself one of the symptoms we have spent Volume II diagnosing. We know this will cost us some readers. We believe it is the cost of the thing being true.

***

There is one more commitment we want to state plainly before the positive program begins. It is the commitment to resist despair.

Despair is the mood of our moment. It is the substrate on which both the populist right and the revolutionary left grow. It is the structure of feeling that makes platform capitalism profitable, because the platforms feed on the conviction that something is horribly wrong and someone deserves to be punished for it. It is the mood the mass-movement diagnosis of Volume II was trying to describe: the sense that the ordinary institutions of a decent life have failed and that only something drastic, cathartic, total can restore meaning. A person in the grip of this mood believes that nothing short of revolution or restoration will do. Reform is for the naive; participation is for the complicit; the slow work of rebuilding is for people who have not understood how bad it is.

We want to say, calmly and without rhetorical flourish, that this mood is itself a symptom of the disease we have been trying to describe, and that its grip is the single most important obstacle to any program of renewal. A population convinced that nothing can be done will, by a self-fulfilling logic, do nothing. The belief that the polity is beyond repair is the condition under which it becomes beyond repair. The first political act, for any citizen who wants to be one, is the refusal of this belief.

We are not asking the reader to be naive. We are not asking her to believe that the problems we have described are minor, or that they will resolve themselves, or that an election or a policy or a cultural vibe shift will restore the public sphere to health. They will not. The diagnosis of Volume II is the diagnosis we believe. The situation is serious. What we are asking is that the reader distinguish between the seriousness of a situation and the impossibility of acting well within it. These are different things, and confusing them is exactly the confusion the conditions of our moment are engineered to produce.

Putnam's *The Upswing* — we will return to it repeatedly — makes an argument that is the empirical counterweight to our moment's despair. The turn of the twentieth century in America looked, from the inside, very much the way our moment looks to us now. Inequality was obscene; institutional trust had collapsed; the party system had degenerated into machine politics; a few men of vast wealth exercised powers incommensurate with democratic self-government; immigration, technology, and urbanization had dissolved the old local associations; the public sphere was fragmented between yellow journalists and muckrakers; politics was coarse, violent, and performed for entertainment. Thoughtful people at the time concluded that the American experiment was over. They were wrong. Over the following four decades, through a vast, uncoordinated, bottom-up civic invention — the settlement houses, the women's clubs, the Boy Scouts, the Rotary, the PTA, the unions, the trade associations, the good-government leagues, a thousand smaller things — a different America was built on top of the wreckage of the old one. By the middle of the twentieth century, the country had more bridging social capital, more civic participation, and more mutual trust than at any point before or since.

We are not saying that America will automatically climb out of its current trough the way it climbed out of that one. Historical analogies do not guarantee outcomes. We are saying that there is no law of nature that says this trough is bottomless. The people who started the climb last time did not know they were starting it. They did not have a book telling them the sociological literature predicted they would succeed. They did not have any guarantee. They did have, as far as we can tell, a certain refusal — a refusal of the idea that because the situation was bad, the work of making it better was not worth doing. That refusal is the premise of Volume III.

***

The rest of this volume is an attempt to take that premise seriously by filling it in. Six chapters remain after this one, and we want to preview them briefly so the reader can see the shape of what we are attempting.

Chapter 16 turns to the empirical literature we have mentioned. It describes what has actually been tested and shown to work to reduce affective polarization and rebuild capacity for civic reasoning. The Strengthening Democracy Challenge megastudy; the Braver Angels Red/Blue workshops and the couples-therapy methodology behind them; the perception-gap interventions from More in Common; the specific findings on structured contact, cross-partisan signaling, and reciprocal group reflection. The goal of the chapter is to give the reader an empirical floor she can stand on when the rest of the conversation around her is hortatory or cynical.

Chapter 17 takes up the question of critical thinking as a civic discipline. This is the terrain of Jonathan Baron's and Keith Stanovich's work on actively open-minded thinking, the AIR THINKING PRO curriculum, the Center for Civic Education's Project Citizen, and the broader question of what critical thinking actually is when it is taught well and why so little of what is called "critical thinking" in American schools is worthy of the name. Critical thinking in our account is not a cognitive style and not a personality trait. It is a practice, a discipline, and an obligation one owes fellow citizens.

Chapter 18 turns to the question of institutional renewal from the inside. The chapter takes up Yuval Levin's question — "given my position here, how shall I act?" — as the core of a usable virtue ethics for modern professional life, and argues that the platform pathology Volume II described can only be answered by people who refuse to use their institutions as platforms. The chapter attends to the ethics of role, the discipline of formation, and the cases in which the institution's demand on its members is at odds with what its members would prefer personally. This is the slow, structural chapter of Volume III: what it would mean for enough people, across enough institutions, to decide that the institution's work was worth more than the personal performance.

Chapter 19 turns to deliberative democracy in practice. This is the empirical literature on citizens' assemblies — James Fishkin's deliberative polling, the Irish assemblies on abortion and same-sex marriage, the French citizens' convention on climate, Hélène Landemore's broader argument for open democracy. The chapter makes the case that there is a real-world alternative to cable-news-as-a-model-of-public-discussion, that the alternative has been road-tested in serious democracies, and that it ought to be a far larger part of the American civic imagination than it currently is.

Chapter 20 turns to the citizen's own practice — the personal disciplines of a public life. Friendship across lines. Membership in a real association that meets. Service in an office small enough that the reader can plausibly occupy it. The refusal of the outrage loop. The recovery of attention. The discipline of sources. These are the things the reader can begin this week, and the chapter takes them seriously as the individual-scale component of a program whose other components operate at larger scales.

Chapter 21 is the final chapter of the book proper, and it is the one most at risk of the stock-move ending we have spent this chapter trying to disavow. We will try to earn it. The chapter attempts to describe what a polity of renewed citizens would actually look like, and to make the argument against despair one more time — not as a mood but as a choice. A country can climb out of a trough like ours. It has done so before. Whether it does so this time is a function of what its citizens, in the aggregate, decide to attempt.

***

A final note before we proceed. We want the reader to hold in mind the question of scale and time, because almost everything we are going to propose depends on getting it right.

The interventions of Chapter 16 can be adopted in a weekend. The personal practices of Chapter 20 can be begun tomorrow. The institutional renewal of Chapter 18 takes a career. The structural reforms implied throughout take decades and political coalitions that do not yet exist. The civic rebuilding that Putnam describes, in *The Upswing*, as the foundation of the Progressive Era took roughly forty years of patient, small-scale, mostly invisible work, begun by people who did not live to see the polity their work produced. It is possible — it is likely — that the work this book describes will be that kind of work. The reader who begins it may not see its completion. Her grandchildren may.

This is not a reason not to begin. It is, in some ways, the reason to begin. The civic work of a free people has always been this kind of work. It is the kind of work that is its own reward before it is its own success, because it is the work civic life was always supposed to consist of. The citizens of a republic — this has been the thesis of the whole book — are people who belong to a common thing, and belonging to a common thing means taking some responsibility for its maintenance, even when its completion is outside one's lifetime. The medieval cathedral-builders have become a cliché on this point, but the cliché is earned. They knew what they were building even though none of them would see it finished. So did the generation that launched the settlement houses and the women's clubs and the Progressive civic associations. So, we suspect, will the generation that begins the climb out of the current trough, if there is one.

We do not know, and will not pretend to know, exactly which of the interventions we are about to describe will work. We do not know the precise shape of the polity on the other side. We do not know how long the climb will take. What we believe — what the following six chapters are an attempt to make plausible — is that the direction is recoverable, that the first steps are identifiable, that the work can be begun this week by any reader willing to begin it, and that the belief that it cannot be begun is itself the principal obstacle to its being begun. We mean to refuse the two most common closings of books like ours. We will not offer the five easy steps that will fix everything, because there are no such steps, and every book that offers them is a form of soft despair masquerading as hope. And we will not offer the monastic retreat, because there is nowhere to retreat to that is not already inside the polity whose fate is the book's subject.

The honest closing is harder than either of those. It sounds like this: here are things that would actually help. Here are the limits of each. Here is what a single reader can do this week. Here is what will take thirty years. Here is how to tell the difference. And here is why beginning — even in uncertainty about whether one will see the finish — is the thing that makes the difference between a society that climbs out of its trough and one that does not.

We believe ours can climb out. We believe the climb starts not in Washington, and not in one's own head, but in the patient co-presence of both — the individual with the inner discipline to think, and the polity with the institutional substance to make thinking worth something. Volume III is about that co-presence. It is about what it would mean to begin.

Let us begin.


---

# Chapter 16
## What Actually Works: The Evidence Base

We begin this volume with an advantage that Hannah Arendt and Eric Hoffer did not have. They wrote in 1951 from within the wreckage of the mass movements they were trying to understand, with almost no empirical literature to draw on and no experimental research to test their intuitions against. The claims they made — that loneliness was the common ground of terror, that mass movements recruited from the politically indifferent rather than the politically engaged, that the remedy lay somewhere in the recovery of a non-totalitarian form of belonging — were arguments from observation, from history, from the testimony of men and women who had lived through what they were describing. We owe those arguments an enormous intellectual debt. They are among the most important things written in the twentieth century. But they were hypotheses. They had to be.

Three-quarters of a century on, we can do better than hypothesize. A real empirical science of political polarization and its reduction has emerged in the last two decades, with large samples, preregistered designs, competing interventions tested head-to-head, and replication attempts that either confirm or (just as importantly) fail to confirm headline findings. The findings of this literature are sobering, surprising, and genuinely useful. They do not say that polarization can be fixed by a clever trick. They do say that some interventions work, some do not, and some that sound appealing in principle make things worse. A solutions chapter written as though this research did not exist has failed before it begins. A solutions chapter that engages with it honestly — naming what it supports and what it does not — can tell the reader something true about what we have to work with.

What we want to do in this chapter is walk the reader through the evidence as we have come to understand it. We will name specific studies, specific researchers, specific organizations, and specific findings. We will be careful about the difference between what has been shown at scale and what has been shown only in controlled settings, between attitude change and behavior change, between short-term and durable effects. We will end with what we take to be the meta-finding — the pattern that emerges when you look at the literature as a whole — and with what that meta-finding implies for the chapters to come and for the reader's own life.

\*\*\*

The single most important piece of work in this literature is the Strengthening Democracy Challenge, organized by Robb Willer at Stanford together with the Civic Health Project, and reported most fully in a paper in *Science* in 2024 with Jan Voelkel as lead author and a large team of collaborators. The design was what researchers now call a megastudy. The team issued an open call for proposed interventions aimed at reducing anti-democratic attitudes, partisan animosity, and support for political violence, and they tested twenty-five of them head-to-head on a representative sample of tens of thousands of Americans, using the same outcome measures and the same follow-up windows. This is a different thing from the usual approach, which is to report a single intervention in a single study, inevitably framed in terms that favor the intervention tested. The megastudy forces interventions to compete. It shows you not only which ones work but which ones work compared to the others.

The broad findings can be stated plainly. Several interventions produced statistically significant reductions in anti-democratic attitudes, partisan animosity, and support for political violence, and several of those effects persisted at the follow-up window two weeks later. The winners were not the interventions most people would have predicted. They were not the generic calls to moderation, not the abstract appeals to democratic values, not the simple civics lessons, and not the "expose yourself to the other side's content" interventions which, in Chris Bail's earlier work, had been shown to backfire. The interventions that did best were more specific than any of those.

First, and most importantly, *corrective information* about the other party's actual views performed well across all three outcomes. An intervention in which Democrats and Republicans were shown accurate data about what the other side actually believed — on immigration, on racial issues, on support for political violence itself — reduced animosity toward the other side and, notably, reduced one's own willingness to endorse undemocratic measures. The mechanism the researchers inferred, and which is consistent with a large body of supporting work, is that Americans have been operating on a mental picture of the other side that is substantially worse than the actual other side. When confronted with the real distribution of opinion, they recalibrate. They stop treating the opposing party as the caricature and start treating it as a somewhat-different population of fellow citizens, which, statistically, is what it is.

Second, *elite cues from within one's own party* performed surprisingly well. Here the design was clever. Republican respondents saw a prominent Republican — a senator, a former governor, a well-known commentator — endorsing democratic norms and condemning political violence. Democratic respondents saw the same kind of cue from a prominent Democrat. The intervention was not asking anyone to listen to the other side. It was asking respondents to hear their own side's elites saying that the rules of the game matter and that certain tactics are off-limits. This worked. The effects on anti-democratic attitudes were among the largest and most durable in the study. The research literature has begun to call this strategy "depolarizing within the comfort of your party," and a separate experimental program by Blair, Gottlieb, Schenk, and Woods has replicated the core finding in several settings. It turns out that one of the most powerful depolarizing interventions is simply hearing your own tribe's authorities reaffirm that the opposing tribe is still composed of fellow citizens.

Third, interventions that *humanized* out-group members through shared-identity framing also performed well. These interventions emphasized common ground — being an American, being a parent, being someone who has lost a loved one, being someone who worries about one's children's future — before engaging with partisan difference. The structure of the intervention mattered. Simple exposure to out-group members, without the shared-identity scaffolding, did not produce the same effects.

The interventions that performed poorly are as interesting as the ones that performed well, because they map onto the standard playbook of American civic reform. Generic civics information, without corrective framing, did not move the needle. Abstract appeals to democratic values — "we should all defend democracy" — did not move the needle. Unstructured exposure to the other side's political content, without facilitation or ground rules, moved the needle slightly in the wrong direction, consistent with the Bail findings we discuss in Volume II. Call-your-representative exercises, without accompanying structure, did very little. Most sobering: many of the interventions beloved of reformers — the ones funded, the ones in civic-education curricula, the ones that turn up at every foundation-sponsored conference on depolarization — did not perform as well as the three specific mechanisms we have just named.

What this tells us is that generic bipartisanship appeals do not work, and specific corrections of specific misperceptions do work. It is a result with teeth. It implies that a solutions program built around "let us all just try to get along" will fail, while a solutions program built around concrete informational and relational structures has a real chance of moving people. This is a more demanding prescription than the standard one, but it is also a more respectful one. It takes the citizen seriously as someone who will respond to evidence, not to exhortation.

\*\*\*

The perception-gap finding deserves its own treatment, because it has turned out to be one of the most replicable and scalable results in the entire literature. The work was done largely by the organization More in Common, and the key report, "The Perception Gap," appeared in 2019 and has been updated several times since. The researchers asked Americans of both parties to estimate the percentage of the other party who held various views — about immigration, about race, about policing, about whether a woman should be able to work outside the home, about whether Americans should be proud of their country. They compared those estimates to the actual distribution of opinion on the other side. The gap between perception and reality was striking. Democrats estimated that a majority of Republicans held extreme positions that only a small minority actually endorsed. Republicans estimated the same about Democrats. On a typical question, each side's perception of the other was off by twenty to thirty percentage points.

The single most important secondary finding — and the one Volume II drew on heavily — is that the size of the perception gap correlates with political engagement. The most engaged partisans, the ones who consume the most political media, who talk about politics most often, who vote most reliably, are the ones with the *least* accurate picture of the other side. This is not what one would expect if exposure to political information were producing understanding. It is what one would expect if the dominant sources of political information — cable news, partisan websites, algorithmically sorted feeds — were functioning as distortion rather than illumination. The citizen who watches the most cable news is the citizen most likely to think that the other side is madder, more extreme, and more dangerous than the other side actually is.

The intervention that follows is almost embarrassingly simple. Show people accurate data about what the other side believes. Tell a Democrat what percentage of Republicans actually support a path to citizenship for long-settled undocumented immigrants, actually believe that racism exists as a real problem, actually reject the more extreme positions the Democrat has been told to fear. Tell a Republican what percentage of Democrats actually support secure borders, actually believe that free speech on campus is valuable, actually reject the more extreme positions the Republican has been told to fear. The effects are real and replicable. They are modest in magnitude — we should be honest about this — but they are consistent across samples, they are cheap to deploy, and they persist. A perception-gap intervention costs roughly nothing compared to the cost of producing the misperception in the first place, and it can be delivered at scale through almost any channel: a quiz, a newsletter, a classroom exercise, a social-media prompt. It is the single most promising low-cost intervention in the literature.

We dwell on this because it matters for the way we think about the larger problem. The polarization crisis is not, or is not primarily, a crisis of actual disagreement. Americans disagree about policy, of course, as they always have. The distinctive feature of the current moment is that the disagreement has been superimposed on a field of mutual caricature, in which each side believes the other to be worse than it is. Reduce the caricature and you have not solved the disagreement, but you have returned it to the scale at which disagreement among fellow citizens is tolerable. That is a real thing to do.

\*\*\*

The most important American civic organization working in this space is Braver Angels, and the detail of its founding is worth dwelling on because it contains the conceptual innovation that has made the work effective. Braver Angels was founded in 2016 by three people: David Blankenhorn, who came out of the political left; David Lapp, who came out of the political right; and Bill Doherty, who was not a political operative at all but a professor of family therapy at the University of Minnesota with decades of clinical experience running couples therapy. The organization's methodology was drawn, explicitly and deliberately, from couples therapy rather than from the standard playbook of political advocacy. This is not a rhetorical flourish. It is the operative design choice that has made the work different from what came before it.

The reasoning was Doherty's. When a married couple comes into a therapist's office at the edge of divorce, the therapist does not try to convince them to agree on the contested issues. That would fail, because the contested issues are not the real problem. The real problem is that the couple has lost the capacity to hear each other, to feel heard, to imagine the other as a good-faith actor rather than as an adversary. What the therapist does is restore the conditions of hearing. The therapist imposes structure. One person talks while the other listens without interrupting. Then they reverse roles. Each is asked to say back what the other said, in the other's terms, before responding. Common ground is named before difference is named. The session ends at a designated time, and the couple is given a small, specific assignment for the week. Over weeks and months, the couple rebuilds the capacity to argue productively without the argument being an existential threat to the relationship. The therapist has not made them agree. The therapist has taught them how to disagree.

Doherty's insight was that affective polarization looks much more like marital conflict than it looks like ordinary policy disagreement. Two groups of Americans have been living together in a single country, arguing about contested issues, for generations without dissolving into mutual contempt. Something has broken in the last twenty years that is less about the issues than about the felt experience of the other side. Couples in failing marriages also can recite the contested issues at length, and the contested issues are also not the real problem. If the analogy holds, the techniques that work in couples therapy might work, with adjustments, on partisan divides. Braver Angels set out to test this.

The organization's core product is the Red/Blue Workshop. An equal number of self-identified conservatives and self-identified liberals gather in a room, usually a community space in a small city, with a trained facilitator. The workshop runs about three and a half hours and follows a tight structured sequence. In the opening exercise, each side writes down the stereotypes of themselves that they believe the other side holds, and then each side reads those stereotypes aloud. This disarms both sides immediately, because it turns out that each side knows perfectly well what the other side thinks of them, and the acknowledgment is received as a kind of gift. In the centerpiece exercise, which Braver Angels calls the "fishbowl," one side sits in a circle and discusses among themselves, in the hearing of the other side but without interruption from them, a set of structured questions: What are your side's core concerns? What do you wish the other side understood about you? What are the reservations you have about your own side? Then the sides switch. The other group sits in the inner circle, speaks among themselves, and is listened to in silence. The structure makes it impossible for the interaction to become a debate. Debate is the thing the participants have been doing their whole lives, and it has been the problem; the structure forbids the problem.

After the fishbowls, the workshop moves to small, mixed, one-on-one conversations built around specific prompts. The day ends with a group exercise on common ground. Nobody is asked to change his or her mind. Nobody is told that the issues they care about do not matter. Nobody is asked to meet in some bland imaginary middle. What they are asked to do is practice, for three and a half hours, the experience of hearing and being heard across the line.

The numbers are meaningful. Since 2016, Braver Angels has run roughly 5,600 events of various formats, engaged about 70,000 direct participants, and established 125 local alliances across the country. External evaluations, including studies by Francesca Gino and Julia Minson at Harvard and by independent researchers engaged by the organization itself, show measurable reductions in affective polarization among participants, with some persistence at follow-up. This is not the kind of result that generates newspaper headlines. It is the kind of result that suggests the method is doing something real.

The caveats, which we owe the reader, are substantial. Participants in Red/Blue Workshops are self-selected, and they skew older, whiter, more educated, and more civically engaged than the American population as a whole. The people in the most polarized positions, the ones who would not sit for three hours in a room with members of the opposing tribe, are the ones least likely to show up. The effects at the population scale, if such a program were somehow made universal, are unknown and would likely be smaller than the effects on the self-selecting sample. Braver Angels does not solve America's polarization problem, and it does not claim to. What it shows is that a method exists, that the method can be taught and replicated, and that when people do engage with it, something measurable changes in them. This is a piece of good news that should not be overstated and should not be ignored.

The larger conceptual contribution is the one we want to carry forward. Polarization, at the level of the individual citizen, is less like a disagreement to be resolved and more like a relational injury to be repaired. The repair follows the pattern that has been worked out over half a century in the clinical literature on conflict. Listening, acknowledgment, structure, common ground, small practical actions over time. These are old techniques in the therapeutic tradition. They are being reapplied, with adjustments, to a larger-scale form of the same underlying problem. The reapplication is, in its modest way, one of the more hopeful developments in recent American civic life.

\*\*\*

A recent academic paper extends this logic into a formal experimental setting. Hannes Baron and colleagues published "Couples Therapy for a Divided America: Assessing the Effects of Reciprocal Group Reflection on Partisan Polarization" in *Political Behavior* in 2024. The design pairs Democratic and Republican respondents in structured exercises in which each partisan articulates his or her views and is then heard, without interruption, by the other — with specific prompts that track the couples-therapy protocol. The researchers measured affective polarization before, immediately after, and at a follow-up several weeks later. The finding: reciprocal group reflection produced statistically significant reductions in affective polarization, with effects that were still detectable at follow-up. The magnitude was modest — roughly the size of the effect produced by the corrective-information interventions in the Strengthening Democracy Challenge — but it was real, and it replicated an earlier pilot. The paper's title is deliberate: the authors are explicitly connecting their experimental protocol to the Braver Angels methodology and arguing that the couples-therapy analogy, which could easily have been taken as a rhetorical conceit, is doing actual work. The structured practice of articulating and being heard, even in a brief artificial setting with strangers, reduces the affective hostility that the research literature has identified as the central polarization problem.

\*\*\*

Braver Angels does not exist alone. It is part of an ecosystem, larger and more sophisticated than most readers realize, and one of the things we want this chapter to do is introduce that ecosystem to readers who have never heard of it. The umbrella network is the #ListenFirst Coalition, which at last count includes roughly four hundred depolarization, bridging, and civic-renewal organizations working across the country.

Several members of the network are worth naming. More in Common, whose Perception Gap research we have already discussed, is also responsible for the "Hidden Tribes" study, an empirical taxonomy of the American political population that identified a large, exhausted, non-aligned majority whose views are consistently misrepresented by the most active wings of each party. Essential Partners, founded at a Massachusetts nonprofit in the 1990s, runs a structured-dialogue protocol derived from family-systems theory — another case of therapeutic techniques migrating into civic work. Urban Rural Action specializes in bridging the geographic divide that Bill Bishop diagnosed in *The Big Sort*, pairing participants across the county lines that American politics has turned into a near-hostile frontier. Living Room Conversations offers a simple, scalable format for small-group conversation across difference, designed to be held in people's homes without professional facilitation, using published conversation guides. StoryCorps' "One Small Step" initiative extends the organization's well-known oral-history format to pair strangers across the political divide for recorded conversations that become part of the Library of Congress archive — a beautiful piece of civic craftsmanship that treats each conversation as worthy of institutional preservation. Civity organizes cross-difference relationships in specific cities, built on the premise that the unit of repair is the particular place rather than the national conversation. BridgeUSA is the campus arm of the movement, now operating on more than a hundred college campuses, where it runs moderated discussions across political lines in the population that the polarization research suggests is most at risk of ossifying into lifelong partisan identity. And the Civic Health Project, which co-organized the Strengthening Democracy Challenge, maintains a public database of the full network and supports much of the evaluation work that lets us say anything empirical about what is working.

The existence of this ecosystem is itself a piece of good news. Books in this genre tend not to report it, partly because the organizations are not glamorous, partly because the work is not dramatic, and partly because the reporter's instinct is to cover conflict rather than the painstaking work of conflict repair. But it is a real thing. Four hundred organizations, many of them staffed by small teams on small budgets, running tens of thousands of events a year, producing measurable effects. It is not a movement yet — the constituencies do not reach across the full political spectrum in representative numbers, and the participants still skew toward the civically engaged professional class — but it is the infrastructure from which a movement could be built. We will have more to say about how to build on it in later chapters.

\*\*\*

If we step back from the individual studies and ask what the literature as a whole is telling us, a short list of reasonably well-supported findings emerges. It is worth stating them together, because it is this convergence, not any single study, that carries the evidential weight.

First, correcting the perception gap works. Showing people accurate data about what the other side actually believes reduces affective polarization reliably, modestly, and cheaply. It is the most scalable intervention in the literature. It does not depend on a facilitator, a workshop, or a relationship. It can be built into a curriculum, a quiz, a newsletter, or a social-media prompt. If one were to prioritize a single intervention for mass deployment, this would be the one.

Second, structured small-group, face-to-face contact works. Unstructured exposure does not, and frequently makes things worse; this is the Bail finding from *Breaking the Social Media Prism* and a corroborating result from multiple smaller studies. What works is contact with ground rules — who speaks when, in what order, on what topics, under what moderation. The ground rules do the work, not the exposure as such. This is the core insight Braver Angels operationalizes and Baron and colleagues formalize.

Third, cross-partisan signals from within one's own party work. Hearing one's own tribe's elites reaffirm democratic norms and denounce political violence is among the most powerful depolarizing interventions available, more powerful than hearing the other side's elites do the same thing, which typically fails. The research phrase is "depolarizing within the comfort of your party" — an ugly phrase but a real finding. The political implication is substantial: it suggests that a great deal of the work of democratic repair has to be done inside each party's own camp, by voices the camp recognizes as its own, rather than across the partisan line.

Fourth, reciprocal group reflection modeled on couples therapy works. Articulating one's views and being heard in return, in a structured format, reduces affective polarization with durable though modest effects. The Braver Angels program and the Baron et al. experimental work converge on this.

Fifth, institution-building and the thickening of local civic life work — more slowly than any of the above, but probably more durably. This is the Putnam and Levin message, and it is where the depolarization literature meets the social-capital literature. You can run a workshop and produce a measurable effect over four weeks. You cannot, at that timescale, rebuild a congregation, a neighborhood association, a union local, a PTA, a bowling league, a volunteer fire company. But the slower work is the work that sustains the fast work. Without a civic substrate, the workshop's effects decay; with one, they compound. We will take this up in detail in Chapter 20.

Each of these five findings is independently supported. But we want to underline that the more interesting observation is their convergence. If a single method had emerged from the literature as the answer, one would reasonably suspect overfitting, publication bias, or the effect of a charismatic research program around a fashionable idea. What has actually emerged is a cluster of methods, developed by different research teams using different theoretical frameworks, that nevertheless share a set of structural features — concrete rather than abstract, specific rather than generic, relational rather than informational, facilitated rather than passive. When independent lines of research converge on a pattern of features rather than on a single favored intervention, the finding is more likely to be tracking something real about the underlying problem. That is what we think is happening here.

\*\*\*

It would be dishonest to close out a survey of the evidence without acknowledging its limits, and we want to do that now rather than leave it for a reviewer. The literature is real, but it is young, and its reach is bounded in at least five important ways.

First, most studies measure attitudes, not behavior. An intervention that reduces a respondent's rating of the other party on a feeling thermometer is producing a real psychological effect, but it is not, or at least not directly, producing a change in voting behavior, civic participation, or willingness to live next to someone from the other party. The chain from attitude change to durable behavioral change is not well-mapped in this literature. Some of it is presumably there — attitudes constrain behavior at the margin — but the strongest version of the findings we have reported is: these interventions change what people say when asked, over some period of time, and that matters less than changing what people do. A more mature version of this research program would measure behavioral outcomes directly. We are not there yet.

Second, the problem of self-selection is everywhere in this work. Red/Blue Workshop participants are not a random sample of Americans. Survey-experiment respondents who sit through a perception-gap correction have, at minimum, consented to sit through it; the most polarized Americans, the ones whose radicalization most concerns us, are the least likely to consent. A finding that an intervention works on a cooperative sample does not entail that it will work, or be accessible, to the non-cooperative. At population scale, effect sizes would likely be smaller and the distribution of effects more uneven.

Third, effect sizes are typically modest. We are not reporting interventions that turn partisans into post-partisans. We are reporting interventions that nudge feeling thermometers down by a few points, that move endorsements of antidemocratic measures from one part of a scale to a slightly less bad part, that reduce but do not dissolve the experienced hostility toward the out-group. The most honest framing is that these interventions chip at a problem; they do not solve it. This is not a criticism of the interventions. It is a caution against the kind of solutionism that would take a modest finding and announce a cure. There is no cure.

Fourth, the research base is overwhelmingly American. Almost all of the studies we have named were conducted in the United States on American samples with American political categories. Cross-cultural generalization is limited. Some of the core findings — the perception gap, the efficacy of structured dialogue — show up in smaller studies in other political systems and appear to travel, but the specific techniques and the specific effect sizes should not be assumed to be universal. Polarization is not the same phenomenon in a multiparty parliamentary democracy as it is in a two-party winner-take-all system, and the interventions may need different designs.

Fifth, most interventions have been tested at short-to-medium time horizons. The follow-up windows in the typical study are weeks to a few months. A handful of studies have extended follow-ups to a year, and the effects tend to persist at reduced magnitude. But we do not have good evidence about what happens over years. The citizen who completes a Red/Blue Workshop in 2024 and feels measurably less hostile to the other party in 2025 may or may not feel the same way in 2035, after a decade of continued immersion in the political environment that produced the hostility in the first place. One would expect decay, but we do not know how much or how fast. Durable civic repair at the individual level is not something the literature, at present, can confirm.

These caveats are real. We want the reader to hold them. But they do not support the stronger claim that "nothing works." Many interventions produce replicable, measurable reductions in polarization. The research has given us real tools. They are modest, they are bounded, they are not magic. They are, nevertheless, ours to use.

\*\*\*

There is an institutional question that sits on top of all of this, and we want to name it here because the rest of the volume will be working inside it. Most of the interventions we have described are aimed at individuals. A Red/Blue Workshop changes individual participants. A perception-gap correction changes individual respondents. A Baron-style reciprocal-group-reflection protocol changes individual partisans in small groups. The larger question is how interventions of this kind can be integrated into the institutions that actually shape people's lives — schools, workplaces, religious communities, municipal government, media organizations, political parties themselves.

Where this integration has happened, the evidence is promising. Some school districts have built perception-gap exercises and structured-dialogue protocols into civics curricula, and the preliminary evaluations suggest the effects compound across repeated exposure in a way that a one-off workshop cannot. BridgeUSA's campus work operates inside the institutional structure of higher education, which gives it continuity that a freestanding workshop program does not have. Certain community foundations in certain cities have built local alliances across political lines that persist across election cycles because they are institutionally rooted rather than event-based. In each case, the individual-level interventions are being used as inputs to institutional practice, rather than as substitutes for it.

Where integration has not happened, individual-level interventions remain useful but small-scale. A workshop that reaches seventy thousand Americans in a decade is a remarkable thing, and we celebrate it, but seventy thousand is one in four thousand adults. Scaling depolarization is not something a nonprofit can do alone. It requires the cooperation of the institutions that already, inescapably, structure most of American civic life. The question of how those institutions might be persuaded to take depolarization seriously — how the school board, the workplace human-resources department, the synagogue, the veterans' post, the city council might each come to see this work as part of what they do — is the institutional question we will take up in Chapter 18.

\*\*\*

We want to close this chapter by naming what we take to be the most important meta-finding of the entire literature, the pattern that emerges once you have read enough of these studies to see what they have in common. The pattern is this. Intention beats algorithm.

Nearly everything that has been shown to reduce polarization is relationally structured, intentionally facilitated, and face-to-face or close to it. Nearly everything that has been shown to worsen polarization is passive, algorithmically structured, and mediated. You can say this about the Braver Angels workshop versus the cable-news hour; about the reciprocal-group-reflection protocol versus the algorithmically sorted feed; about the perception-gap exercise in a classroom versus the partisan meme delivered to a phone; about the deliberative poll versus the outrage notification. The former in each pair produces modest, real, durable reductions in hostility. The latter in each pair produces large, real, durable increases in hostility. The asymmetry is one of the central empirical facts about civic life in our moment.

This is not a counsel of despair about technology, and we want to be careful with it. The mediated forms of public communication are not going away, and the work of depolarization cannot be confined to small rooms forever. But the asymmetry is telling us something about the shape of a serious civic-renewal strategy. We have to privilege the forms of communication that have been shown to heal, and we have to resist the forms that have been shown to harm, and we have to do both at the level of personal practice, of institutional design, and of policy. A citizen who makes the individual-scale version of this choice — who spends more of her week in a room with other citizens and less in the feed — is doing something that, according to the evidence, matters. A school that makes the institutional version of this choice is doing something that matters more. A platform that made the structural version of this choice would be doing something that matters most, though we are not holding our breath.

There is one further observation about the personal-practice version of this that we want to carry into Chapter 20, which is the chapter on the citizen's own practice. The evaluations of programs like Braver Angels consistently turn up a finding that is almost never emphasized in the popular discussion. The workshop changes the participants. But the participants also change themselves by participating. The act of showing up — of accepting the discipline of the structure, of speaking when it is one's turn, of listening when it is someone else's turn, of spending the afternoon on this particular work instead of something else — has effects on the person doing it that are as significant as its effects on the people he engages with. Civic action is civic formation. You do not show up to a workshop because you are already a citizen; you show up because showing up is how you become one. This is, in the old republican vocabulary, what a practice is: an activity whose purpose is partly to produce a particular kind of person in those who undertake it. The research is catching up to what the old republican thinkers knew by intuition. The citizen is made by what the citizen does.

\*\*\*

Two previews before we close. The next chapter, Chapter 17, takes up the question of critical thinking as a civic discipline. The literature on actively open-minded thinking, on the THINKING PRO curriculum, on Project Citizen, on the scandalous numbers about civic knowledge among American high school students, is a different strand of the same evidence base we have been walking through here. Depolarization and critical thinking are not the same intervention, but they share a structure: both require a practice, both resist the lure of the quick fix, both produce modest but real effects when taken seriously as civic disciplines. Chapter 19 will take up deliberative democracy in practice — the citizens' assemblies and deliberative polls that extend the logic of the small-group work we have described in this chapter to the scale of actual public-policy decision-making. These are different strands of the same evidence base, and together they form the empirical floor on which the rest of the volume's arguments stand.

What we have tried to do in this chapter is clear the ground for those arguments. The cynic's claim that nothing works is empirically wrong. The utopian's claim that the right program will fix America is empirically wrong too. Between those claims is a larger, more useful territory: a body of research that has given us real, modest, replicable tools for the work of civic repair, and a set of organizations already using those tools, and a method for doing more of what works and less of what does not. We do not have a cure for what has happened to the American citizen. We have a practice. The rest of this volume is about how to live inside it.


---

# Chapter 17
## Critical Thinking as Civic Discipline

For more than a decade now, a research group at Stanford has been quietly gathering what is, we think, the most damning single body of evidence about the condition of American civic life. The Stanford History Education Group began in the mid-2010s to ask a question that had until then been asked mostly in the abstract: can young Americans, who have grown up on the open internet, actually tell whether what they are reading online is true? The researchers devised a set of assessments in which students were shown real-world web material — photographs, news stories, sponsored content, advocacy sites, social-media posts — and asked to judge its credibility. The students came from high schools and colleges across the country, from elite institutions and from ordinary ones. The samples ran to thousands.

The results, published in a series of papers and in a 2019 national study of nearly 3,500 high-school students, are the kind of thing a researcher hesitates to write down because writing it down makes it real. Two thirds of the students could not distinguish a sponsored advertisement labeled "sponsored content" from a regular news article on the same page. More than ninety percent accepted a blatantly political advocacy site, which concealed its funding, as a credible source of information on the topic it was advocating about. Shown a photograph that purported to document a real-world event but was in fact unrelated to it, nearly all failed to perform any verification; the overwhelming majority took the image at face value. Asked to evaluate a Twitter post from an organization with a professional-sounding name, the students accepted the professional appearance as sufficient — they confused the aesthetic of authority for the substance of it. Undergraduates, in a parallel study, did slightly better on average and were still, by any reasonable standard, terrible. Stanford's own students, the researchers noted with a pained sort of candor, were not appreciably better than students at open-enrollment community colleges at the basic task of checking who was behind a website before deciding whether to trust it.
