What the researchers called "lateral reading" — the habit of opening a second tab and looking up the source rather than staying on the source's own page — was essentially absent from the student repertoire. The students clicked, scrolled, stayed, and judged; they did not, on the whole, verify.

We dwell on this because the first thing to say about critical thinking in American life is that, as a mass competence, it is not present. It is not that we have it and are failing to use it. We do not, at scale, have it. And the failure is bipartisan — it is not that students on one side of the political spectrum are taken in by bad sources while students on the other see clearly. The credulity is general. The right-leaning student who believes the advocacy site on immigration and the left-leaning student who believes the advocacy site on climate are performing the same cognitive operation and performing it equally badly. Whatever else we say about the condition of the American public mind, it is not that half of it has been trained well and half has been trained badly. The training is absent. The information environment, which punishes those who have not learned, presses on everyone.

Put this finding next to another one and the civic picture comes into focus. In a 2016 paper in the *Journal of Democracy* that has since become a reference point in political science, Roberto Stefan Foa and Yascha Mounk reported on a trend in the World Values Survey that almost no one had noticed. They asked respondents across established democracies how essential it was to live in a democratically governed country, on a ten-point scale. Among Americans born in the 1930s, about seventy-two percent rated living in a democracy a "ten" — absolutely essential. Among Americans born in the 1980s, the figure was about thirty percent. A roughly forty-point generational collapse in the importance attached to the democratic form itself. Similar declines, though smaller, appeared in most of Europe. Openness to authoritarian alternatives — "a strong leader who doesn't have to bother with parliament and elections" — rose across the same generations. Other researchers have pushed back on the steepness of the slope and on the measurement; even under the gentlest reading, the gap is one of the largest attitudinal gaps ever reported in public-opinion research on democracy, and it points in one direction.

This is not a private failure. It is a structural one. A civic education — here meaning the whole apparatus of schools, media, families, and civic organizations by which a polity transmits itself to the next generation — that produces a population unable to evaluate sources and increasingly willing to entertain the abandonment of the democratic form is a civic education that has failed at the only job it ever had. The question is what we can do about it.

We want to propose an answer. It is not a policy answer; it is closer to a cultural one, though it has policy consequences. The answer begins by reclaiming a phrase that has been ruined by overuse, and restoring it to the meaning it had before it became a slogan.

***

The phrase is *critical thinking*. It is now used, in the cheerful commercial prose of school districts and human-resources seminars and political op-eds, to mean almost anything the writer wants it to mean. A pundit announces that the public needs more critical thinking, by which he means that it needs to agree with him. A teacher tells her class to engage in critical thinking, by which she means that the students should summarize the reading with skepticism toward some parts and credulity toward others according to rules that are never quite specified. A political movement invokes critical thinking as its self-description and applies it with ferocity to the claims of its opponents and not at all to its own. Each use is a small act of laundering. The phrase comes out of these uses meaning, at best, *the kind of thinking I approve of*, and at worst, nothing at all. If we are to make anything of critical thinking as a civic category, we have to earn the concept. We will not use it the way the culture uses it.

The first thing to do is distinguish it from two neighboring things it is often confused with. The first is general cognitive capability — roughly, intelligence. Cognitive capability is measurable. It is partly heritable. It correlates with many good outcomes. It is not, and this is the point, a prophylactic against bad reasoning. The research tradition associated with Daniel Kahneman, Amos Tversky, Keith Stanovich, Dan Kahan, and others has made what ought by now to be a familiar finding: intelligence, on its own, does not protect a person from motivated reasoning. Under conditions of identity-protective cognition — when a question of fact becomes entangled with a question of who the person is — higher cognitive ability is associated with *more* sophisticated rationalization, not less. The clever partisan builds better arguments for what the partisan already believes. Kahan's studies of numerical reasoning on politically charged topics show the pattern directly: given the same mathematical problem dressed as a skin-cream trial or as a gun-control study, high-numeracy respondents produce the right answer when the result is politically neutral and fluff the analysis when the politically neutral answer would embarrass their side. Intelligence, under motivation, is a weapon. It is not a safeguard.

The second thing critical thinking is often confused with is domain knowledge. A person who has studied the history of the Civil War closely will reason about the Civil War better than someone who has not; a person who has built bridges knows more about bridges than a person who has only read about them. This is true and important. Serious reasoning about any particular subject requires serious acquaintance with the subject. But domain knowledge does not generalize. The Civil War historian is no wiser than anyone else about tax policy, and the bridge engineer has no special authority on public health. A good part of the epistemic damage of the past decade has been done by people with real expertise in one field confidently speaking in another and being treated, by themselves and by their audiences, as authoritative. Domain knowledge is necessary; it is not sufficient; and it is not transferable in the way that a civic education needs a capacity to be transferable.

The third thing — the thing we want to claim is the civic heart of the matter — is what psychologists Jonathan Baron and Keith Stanovich, over a long research career, have called *actively open-minded thinking*. The phrase has a technical meaning. It denotes a disposition rather than an ability — a habit of the mind rather than a feature of its horsepower. The actively open-minded thinker is willing to consider evidence that contradicts her prior belief; actively seeks out viewpoints different from her own; distinguishes the content of a claim from the identity of the person making it; tolerates the psychological discomfort of not yet knowing; gives genuine weight, when she assesses an argument, to the possibility that she is wrong. She has, in Baron's formulation, an aversion to premature closure and an appetite for disconfirming information that most of us, left to ourselves, lack. The trait is ordinary-language simple and technically precise. It is what we mean by open-mindedness when we are being serious.

What the psychometric literature has added to the ordinary-language understanding is, for our purposes, decisive. Three findings in particular.

The first is that actively open-minded thinking is the single best-identified cognitive correlate of accurate political reasoning, and that it predicts accuracy *independently* of intelligence and of education. Stanovich's *What Intelligence Tests Miss* (2009) and his subsequent work with Richard West have made a systematic case that the standard IQ tests do not measure this disposition at all; that the disposition and the ability are roughly uncorrelated; and that many of the failures of rationality we associate with the name of Kahneman are failures of the disposition, not of the ability. A brilliant person who does not want to be wrong is not, on the questions that matter to her identity, going to reason well about them. A modestly intelligent person who has cultivated the disposition will. Across a range of studies — on belief in conspiracy theories, on susceptibility to fake news, on the ability to update in response to evidence — actively open-minded thinking is the trait that moves the needle.

The second finding is more disquieting, and we have already gestured at it. Because intelligence under motivation tends to *better* defend the motivated conclusion, and because political engagement tends to intensify motivation, intelligent partisans are often *more* polarized than less-intelligent ones. Dan Kahan's work shows this directly. The most numerate respondents in his studies show the largest politically-driven gaps in numerical reasoning. Ezra Klein, in *Why We're Polarized*, summarizes the point by observing that the most politically informed voters are, in many ways, the most polarized and the most intractable. This is the paradox of the engaged citizen in a polarized age: the very traits we hope a good civic education would cultivate — attentiveness, informedness, political interest — turn out, without the disposition of active open-mindedness, to produce the more sophisticated partisan rather than the better citizen. More in Common's perception-gap research makes the same point from the other direction: the most politically engaged Americans hold the most distorted pictures of what people on the other side actually believe. Engagement, on its own, is not a solution. It is a multiplier. What it multiplies depends on what disposition it is attached to.

The third finding is the hopeful one. Actively open-minded thinking is teachable. It can be assessed, through standard scales that have been validated over decades. It can be measured before and after an intervention. It responds to interventions. The pedagogical literature is clear that you can, through the right kind of deliberate practice, increase the trait in individual learners, and that the increase produces measurable downstream effects on the quality of reasoning about politically charged topics. The trait is not a fixed feature of a person. It is a muscle.

That is the place where the psychological literature meets the civic one, and where our argument about critical thinking starts to become an argument about citizenship.

***

Consider what it means to call something a virtue. We took some care in Volume I to recover the classical meaning of the word, which the moralistic turn of the last two centuries has obscured. A virtue in the Aristotelian and republican tradition is not a feeling; it is not an inclination; it is not, in the first instance, a moral sentiment. A virtue is a trained disposition — a habituated readiness to act rightly under the circumstances of a particular role. Courage is the soldier's virtue not because soldiers feel braver than other people but because the soldier's role places him in situations where the pull of his own fear must be resisted in favor of the demands of the role, and because courage is the disposition that, once trained in, makes that resistance possible. Temperance is the pilgrim's virtue; patience is the teacher's; honesty, in the strong and costly sense, is the witness's. Each is acquired by practice. No one is born with it. One becomes courageous by repeatedly doing the brave thing in small instances until the disposition is set. One becomes honest by speaking the truth against interest, over and over, until it is the default and not the exception.

The civic virtue analogue — the virtue that belongs to the citizen as the courage belongs to the soldier — is, we think, exactly this disposition of actively open-minded thinking. The citizen's role places her in situations where the pull of her own partisan identity, her own group loyalty, her own desire to be right, her own discomfort with disconfirmation, must be resisted in favor of the demands of the role. The role demands that she consider the claims of her political opponents on their merits. It demands that she take seriously the possibility that she has been deceived by her own side. It demands that she distinguish what she wishes were true from what the evidence supports. None of this comes naturally. No human being is, by default, good at it. We are good at it only to the extent that we have practiced.

This is why it is misleading to call critical thinking, in the civic sense, a cognitive style. A style is a preference. A virtue is a discipline. The citizen who holds her beliefs provisionally, who reads writers she expects to disagree with, who tests her convictions against the best counterarguments she can find, who updates — publicly, at cost to her reputation — when the evidence demands it, is not doing something that comes easily to her any more than the soldier who charges the hill is doing something that comes easily. She is doing the hard thing that the role requires, and she is able to do it because she has cultivated the capacity over time. She has practiced. She has been shaped by institutions that drilled the practice into her. She has kept company with others who do the same. She has built, in the old language, a habit of the soul.

The Center for Civic Education, which for four decades has been the steadiest working institution for civic formation in the American schools, lists a set of what it calls civic dispositions that civic education should cultivate. The list, in the various formulations it has taken over the years, includes civility, respect for the worth and dignity of each person, open-mindedness, a willingness to compromise, tolerance of diversity, patience and persistence, compassion, generosity, loyalty to the nation and its principles, and — in the formulation we find most useful — critical thinking. The striking thing about the list, when you sit with it, is that every item on it is a disposition rather than a body of information. It is not that the citizen is supposed to memorize the Bill of Rights, though we hope she will; it is that she is supposed to have become, through her formation, a certain kind of person. The list is a description of that person.

It is also, if we are honest, a list of traits that the incentive structure of our age actively disfavors. Civility is not how you go viral. Open-mindedness is not what the algorithm promotes. Tolerance of diversity, in the demanding sense of actually finding oneself in sustained contact with people whose views one finds repugnant, is the opposite of the sorted media diet most of us consume. Willingness to compromise is coded, in our politics, as weakness. Patience loses to urgency. Respect for each person's worth is incompatible with the hourly practice of reducing strangers on the internet to objects of contempt. The dispositions civic education is supposed to produce are, at the level of habit, the dispositions our daily life subtracts from us. No wonder the output has deteriorated. We have been running the civic machine in reverse.

***

What would running it forward look like? Here the empirical literature begins to give us something concrete to hold on to. Over the past two decades, a cluster of evaluated civic-education programs has accumulated a track record, and the pattern of what works is remarkably consistent across them.

The American Institutes for Research, with U.S. Department of Education funding, developed a curriculum called THINKING PRO, aimed at grades nine through eleven, that integrates critical-thinking instruction with civic-efficacy training. The curriculum has been evaluated; it shows measurable effects on the kinds of outcomes one would hope a civic-thinking curriculum would move — students' ability to evaluate sources, their willingness to consider alternative viewpoints, their sense that their own civic action can matter. It is not a miracle. The effect sizes are modest, as effect sizes in educational research generally are. But it is something. There is a curriculum, tested against controls, that moves the needle on the outcomes we care about. That is not nothing; it is, in fact, the foundation from which scaled improvement could be built, if we decided to build it.

The Center for Civic Education's Project Citizen is an older and better-known example. The method is project-based. Students in a class identify a real, local problem in their community — the school zone without a crosswalk, the public library with diminishing hours, the park that has not been maintained. They research it. They map the stakeholders. They develop a proposed policy response. They present it to the relevant unit of local government — the school board, the city council, the county commission. The longitudinal evidence on Project Citizen and its international variants shows what we would expect it to show: students who go through the program retain civic skills years later, in ways that students taught the same civic content through lecture do not. Something happens in the doing that the telling cannot accomplish.

There are others worth mentioning. iCivics, the civic-education platform founded by Justice Sandra Day O'Connor in her post-Court years, reaches millions of students and has an internal evaluation record that is defensible even if the medium is sometimes lighter than we would want. Generation Citizen runs something close to a Project Citizen model in under-resourced schools in several major cities. The Harvard-affiliated Democratic Knowledge Project has built out deliberative-pedagogy materials that are in use in a growing set of districts. The Institute for Citizens and Scholars, formerly the Woodrow Wilson Foundation, coordinates a great deal of this work nationally. Taken together, these programs constitute a real infrastructure for civic formation. They are staffed by unglamorous people doing serious work, often in conditions that are harder than they should be. They rarely make the news.

What we find striking, looking across the evaluated programs, is that they converge on a small set of pedagogical commitments. These commitments are worth naming, because they are the operational content of the word *critical thinking* in the civic sense.

Authentic, local, participatory work rather than abstract instruction. The students investigate something that matters in the place where they live, with stakes they can feel. The classroom is not the boundary of the lesson; the community is.

Explicit instruction in source evaluation, with particular attention to the online environment. The students are taught to perform lateral reading — to leave the source and look it up elsewhere before deciding what to think of it. They are taught click-restraint — the habit of not clicking the first search result but scanning down the list. They are taught triangulation: confirming important claims against two or more independent sources. They are taught, by exercise rather than by slogan, that the aesthetic of a site has nothing to do with its reliability, and that the professional look of a headline or a tweet is not evidence of anything. The Stanford History Education Group's curriculum — the same group that documented the original scandal — is freely available and is one of the best things on offer anywhere.

Practice in articulating and defending positions one does not hold. Structured debate, Oxford-style or adapted, where students are assigned to argue the side they came in opposing. Role-play of contested policy questions. Deliberative exercises in which students must genuinely represent a view that is not their own. This is, in essence, the ideological Turing test as a classroom discipline: can the student describe the position of the other side well enough that a sincere holder of that position would recognize the description as fair?

Practice in changing one's mind publicly when the evidence demands it. The hardest item on the list, and the most important. Teachers who do this well model it themselves; they tell their students about positions they have abandoned, and why. The students learn that revision of belief is not a defeat to be hidden but a competence to be cultivated. Most of us go through school, and for that matter through adult life, without ever seeing an authority figure visibly change his mind in real time. Those who do see it, and who are invited to practice it in small matters, acquire a capacity others do not.

Mentorship by adults who themselves model the dispositions. This is the intangible but decisive ingredient. A civic disposition is caught as much as it is taught. The teacher who visibly takes seriously a student's objection, even when the teacher is certain the objection is mistaken, communicates something no worksheet can. The teacher who has a particular political view but who can be seen thinking against it, publicly, teaches open-mindedness more completely than the teacher who professes neutrality. Most of the best civic formation in America is being done by particular teachers in particular classrooms, often without institutional support, often without public recognition, simply because they understand that this is the job. We owe them more than we give them.

***

If civic education were the whole story, the argument would stop there — with the schools, and with what we would need to do to support the people already doing the work. But the public whose critical-thinking capacity we need to rebuild is not in school. Most of it has not been in school for decades. Any serious civic-education program has to ask what adult formation looks like, because the adults are the electorate, and the electorate is the polity.

The adult question is harder than the school question, because we cannot compel an adult to take a course. We can, however, notice where critical-thinking formation is happening in adult life, and we can name the institutions that do it well.

The first is serious journalism — what Jonathan Rauch has called, in *The Constitution of Knowledge*, the set of institutions that together constitute the public's epistemic infrastructure. A working newsroom, with an editor who demands sourcing and a reporter who practices triangulation, is a working factory of critical thinking. A reader of such a newsroom is being trained, day by day, by the discipline of its practice. This is not because journalists are cognitively superior to other people; it is because the institutional role of the journalist, when the institution is functioning, demands the dispositions we have been describing, and the habits rub off on the attentive reader. The decline of local journalism and the hollowing of many national outlets is therefore not only a problem for information; it is a problem for formation. When the public loses access to institutions that model good reasoning, it loses a teacher.

The second is the network of structured-dialogue organizations we discussed in Chapter 16 — Braver Angels, Essential Partners, Living Room Conversations, the #ListenFirst Coalition and the hundreds of smaller groups that do adjacent work. What these organizations do, behind the depolarization framing they lead with, is precisely critical-thinking pedagogy for adults. The ground rules of a Braver Angels workshop — speak for yourself, do not interrupt, seek to understand before seeking to be understood, steelman the other side before you critique it — are the operational definition of actively open-minded thinking in a social setting. A participant who completes the workshop has practiced the disposition for several hours under conditions designed to make the practice possible. It is not a course; it is a rehearsal. But rehearsals matter.

The third is the family of deliberative institutions — deliberative polling, citizens' assemblies, the evidence-based structured deliberations we will take up more fully in Chapter 19. A citizen who sits on a randomly selected deliberative body for a weekend, or for a series of weekends, is required to engage with evidence, to hear from experts with opposing views, to articulate and revise her own position, and to reach a considered judgment under conditions that punish bad reasoning and reward good. James Fishkin's twenty years of comparative results show that ordinary people placed in these conditions do, reliably, perform the disposition — they change their minds on the basis of new information; they narrow the gap between themselves and their opposites; they come out knowing more and caring more and often disagreeing less. Deliberation, in the technical sense that Fishkin and his collaborators have operationalized, is the adult-education form of critical-thinking pedagogy.

None of this scales yet. The school programs reach perhaps a few hundred thousand students in their evaluated forms in any given year, against a national school-age population in the tens of millions. The dialogue organizations reach a few hundred thousand adult participants over their histories combined. The deliberative bodies, in the United States, remain an experiment at the state level. What we have is proof of concept rather than proof of scale. But the proof of concept is, for our purposes, the material point. The dispositions can be cultivated. The cultivation can be taught. The teaching works. The only question is whether we will invest at the level the moment demands.

***

We have been writing at the level of institutions and programs. The reader who has gotten this far has a right to ask what we think she should do. We have some suggestions. They cost nothing and can be begun today.

Read, regularly, serious writers you expect to disagree with. Not social-media opponents; serious writers, at book length or long-essay length, on the other side of questions you care about. The point is not to refute them. The point is to understand them well enough that you could summarize the argument in terms the writer would accept. If you are progressive, read Deneen, or Scruton, or Douthat; if you are conservative, read Sandel, or Coates, or Levitsky. Read one every month or two. Keep a list. Do not buy only the ones you end up agreeing with.

Adopt, as a personal discipline, *steelman before critique*. Before you disagree with a position — in conversation, in writing, online — articulate the strongest version of it, in terms that a sympathetic holder of the position would endorse. If you cannot do this, you are not yet entitled to disagree; you do not yet know what you are disagreeing with. The discipline is simple. It is also, in practice, punishing. It is a discipline.

Before you post, before you argue, before you press send, write out — even in a private note — the best case against what you are about to say. If the best case is strong, consider whether you should say what you are about to say at all. If it is weak, you will have sharpened your own position. Either way, you will have practiced the disposition.

Keep a list of times you have changed your mind. This is the specific exercise we find most useful, and the one we recommend most strongly. Actually write it down. What positions did you hold five years ago that you no longer hold? What convinced you? What evidence crossed the threshold, and what argument did the work? A person who cannot produce such a list is not, strictly speaking, thinking. She is reinforcing. She is metabolizing inputs into the confirmation of prior commitments. The person who can produce such a list — ten items, say, from the last decade — has a track record of doing the hardest thing a reasoner ever does, and knows something about herself that she can rely on. If you have not kept the list, start it. You can recover much of it retrospectively.

Take the ideological Turing test, in Bryan Caplan's formulation. Can you state your opponent's position well enough that a sympathizer would recognize it as a fair description? Test yourself. Try to write an op-ed in the voice of someone you oppose — not a parody, a genuine attempt. Show it to someone on that side. See whether they flinch, and where, and why. The exercise is humbling. It is also an almost pure measure of the disposition we have been discussing.

Shift your information diet toward slower, less emotionally reinforcing sources. Newspapers over feeds. Books over clips. Primary documents over summaries. Long-form audio over rage-cycle television. The argument here is not aesthetic. It is neurological. The fast, emotionally laden, algorithmically selected diet exercises the part of us that is most susceptible to motivated reasoning and least susceptible to its correction. The slower diet exercises the part that can hold a proposition without immediately reacting to it. Over months and years, the difference compounds. It may be the single largest adjustment an individual reader can make at zero financial cost.

Find at least one person with whom you can practice honest argument. Not a sparring partner who shares all your premises; a friend whose premises are different, whose arguments you respect, with whom you have agreed — explicitly — to tell each other when you think the other is wrong and to listen when the other tells you. This is the oldest civic technology in existence. It is how Socrates thought, how Lincoln thought, how anyone who has ever thought well has thought. The solitary reasoner is a myth. We reason with others, or we do not reason at all. You need a person. If you do not have one, the search for one is itself an early practice of the discipline.

***

A warning before we close. A reader who absorbs an argument like this one is in a specific kind of danger, and we have seen it happen often enough that we want to name it. The danger is that the reader, inspired to think more critically, uses her new inspiration as a weapon against the other side, while leaving her own side's premises unexamined. She returns from this chapter sharper at finding the flaws in the arguments she already rejected, and no more rigorous about the arguments she already accepts. If that is the effect of what we have written, we have done harm. The reader who has been inspired to be more skeptical in exactly the direction she was already skeptical, and no more, has not become a better citizen; she has acquired a rhetorical accessory. She is more confident, not more open. The world has one more clever partisan in it.

The discipline we are describing cuts the other direction first. The most important application of actively open-minded thinking is not against the claims of the tribe across the aisle; it is against the claims of one's own. The perception-gap research is clear that the most politically engaged Americans are the most misinformed about their opponents. The corollary is that the most practiced skeptics in a given camp are often the ones least inclined to apply the skepticism where it would cost them something — where it would bring them into tension with the people they want to sit with at dinner, with the institutions they want to belong to, with the moral identity they have constructed for themselves. The test of whether a person has actually cultivated the disposition we are describing is not whether she can find the flaws in the arguments of the other camp. Anyone can find those flaws. The test is whether she can find, and acknowledge, the flaws in the arguments of her own. Whether she can hold her own side to the standards she holds the opposition to. Whether she can say, in public, *I was wrong about that*, or *my people are wrong about this*, and bear the cost. The cost is real. The social pressure to be a reliable ally to the people one likes is among the oldest and most powerful forces in the human psychology, and it is currently being amplified by every technology of our public life. To resist it — not to abandon one's commitments, but to hold them accountable to the same reasoning one demands of the other side — is the specifically civic form of the disposition.

We ought to say, plainly, that we find this hard. We have tribes of our own. We have dinner tables and group chats and reading lists and professional associations whose approval we want, and whose displeasure at disagreement we feel. We have written this chapter as much to remind ourselves as to propose anything to the reader. The discipline is not something one arrives at. It is something one practices, imperfectly, across a life.

***

We do not want to oversell what is on offer here. Critical thinking, even in the disciplined sense we have tried to give it, is not a magic bullet. Societies with excellent civic education and widespread actively open-minded thinking would still make mistakes, have factions, produce occasional demagogues, and argue about the things that are worth arguing about. A polity in which the disposition was widely cultivated would not be a utopia. It would be something both more modest and more valuable: a polity whose public disagreements were of higher quality than ours, whose public reasoning moved on the evidence and not only on the team-colors, whose citizens could be addressed as reasoners and not only as loyalists. It would be, approximately, the polity we have had, in patches, at particular times, in particular places — in certain town meetings, in certain newsrooms, in certain classrooms, in certain professional communities, at certain moments when the institutions were functioning well. It is not asking for something we have never done. It is asking for something we have done intermittently and could do more of, if we wanted.

It is worth saying clearly that the task of doing more of it will fall most heavily on people we do not currently honor enough. The American classroom teacher who runs a Project Citizen unit in a district that has cut her department's budget three years running; the community-college instructor who builds a lateral-reading module into her English composition course on her own time; the librarian who teaches a media-literacy workshop in a small-town public library on a Tuesday night; the journalist at a shrinking newspaper who still insists on triangulating before publishing; the volunteer moderator at a Braver Angels workshop who takes a Saturday out of the month to sit in a room with twenty strangers — these are the people doing, today, the work we are describing. Most of them will never be famous. Most of them will never be thanked adequately. Some of the best civic formation in America at this moment is being done by people whose names we will never learn, in rooms we will never see, with students and neighbors we will never meet. We know this is true because we have met some of them, and every time we have, we have come away with more hope than the general tenor of the public conversation would suggest is warranted. The civic body of this country still has the antibodies. They are not active everywhere. They are active in more places than the commentary suggests.

It remains, finally, to say how this chapter connects to the book's larger argument. The thesis of *Return of the Citizen* is that the modern person has been degraded by two linked forces — the atomization that strips her of the associations in which character is formed, and the mass movements that rush in to fill the resulting void. Critical thinking, in the sense we have tried to define, is the specific civic virtue that speaks to the second half of that degradation. It is the trained disposition that makes a person resistant to the offer of certainty that every mass movement extends. The cult — whether the cult is ideological, partisan, religious, technological, or identitarian — promises to lift from the adherent the burden of not yet knowing, in exchange for the adherent's willingness to stop thinking in the specific ways the movement finds inconvenient. The cult offers a world in which the questions are settled and the enemies are named. The adherent is spared the discomfort of open cognition; the movement is spared the inconvenience of dissent.

The person who has practiced holding provisional beliefs in the face of social pressure is the person the cult cannot recruit. It is not that she is immune to persuasion; she can be persuaded, and ought to be, by evidence and argument. It is that she cannot be persuaded by the particular package the mass movement sells, because the package is not evidence and argument; it is the offer of certainty in exchange for the surrender of inquiry, and she has practiced refusing that offer. The practice is not glamorous. It does not feel, day to day, like resistance. It feels like the ordinary intellectual housework of reading the other side, steelmanning the opposing case, keeping a record of one's changes of mind, tolerating the discomfort of open questions. But it is the housework, accumulated over years, that builds the structure that holds when the wind comes. The citizens who held, in the crises of the twentieth century, were the ones who had been practicing.

That is what we are asking of the reader and of ourselves. Not a certificate in critical thinking; not a declared allegiance to open-mindedness; not a posture of reasonableness to be worn in public. A practice. A discipline. A slow and unglamorous training of the mind toward the specific dispositions the role of citizen requires. We will not all become good at it. We will all become better at it if we work at it. And the polity is the sum of those small, private labors — the thousand daily acts of steelmanning, of source-checking, of self-correction, of honest argument with another honest mind — which, accumulated, make the difference between a public world capable of governing itself and one ready to be captured by whoever shouts the loudest.

We have the tools. We know what works. We have the teachers, if we will support them. We have, each of us, the capacity for the discipline, if we will practice it. The only remaining question is whether, in the moment we have been given, we will decide that the civic life is worth the work. The citizen, in the end, is the person who has decided that it is.


---

# Chapter 18
## Rebuilding Institutions from the Inside

Somewhere in the middle of *A Time to Build*, Yuval Levin puts his whole constructive argument into a single sentence that can be read past in a hurry and which contains more of a program than most books of three hundred pages manage. The sentence is a question. The person who inhabits an institution, he says, should ask herself: *given my position here, how shall I act?*

It does not look like much on the page. Read it again. The sentence contains, in compressed form, an entire theory of moral life, a diagnosis of the pathology we have been describing, and the seed of a program for reversing it. It presupposes that the position has content — that it is not a role one writes from scratch, but a role one enters, and that the role makes demands, imposes constraints, and, if one is honest, forms the person who accepts them. It presupposes that the person asking it is not at the center of her own moral universe, but is instead located — somewhere specific, among particular others, with particular obligations she did not author — and that locating oneself honestly is the first act of serious moral attention. It presupposes, above all, that the good life is not the life one constructs against the grain of one's positions, but the life one practices within them, with such attentiveness that the positions themselves are formed by the attention.

We think this question is the foundational question of a usable virtue ethics for the modern role-bearing person. We think that asking it, across institutions, with real seriousness, by enough of the people who inhabit those institutions, is the primary mechanism by which institutional decay is reversed. And we think — this is the hardest claim of the chapter — that the question is also the most practical answer we have to one of the most persistent and most fair objections to civic-republican thought, which is that "virtue" is a concept without usable content in a modern, pluralist society, and that a book proposing civic virtue as an answer is really proposing a return to a world we cannot rebuild and should not want.

That objection has force. The reader who is skeptical of virtue talk is not a philistine. She has seen virtue invoked, in the last two decades of American public life, as a cudgel — deployed by purity movements on the left and the right to silence dissenters, to weaponize private conduct, to convert politics into an endless tribunal of personal morality. She is rightly allergic. The last thing she wants to read, at the end of a book about the collapse of the public sphere, is that the answer is for everyone to be more virtuous.

We agree with the allergy. We want to describe a different kind of virtue entirely, and precisely enough that the reader can tell what we mean and what we do not.

Virtue in the classical sense — what Aristotle meant by *arete*, what Cicero meant by *virtus*, what the medieval moralists meant when they inherited both — was never a matter of abstract rules applied to atomized persons. It was always attached to *roles*. The virtues of a good soldier were not the virtues of a good judge. The virtues of a good citizen were not the virtues of a good father. Each role had its own excellences, and the task of the person was not to become virtuous in general but to become excellent in the specific ways that the roles he actually occupied required. Aristotle's *Nicomachean Ethics* is often read as a treatise on the virtues of the human as such. Read it alongside the *Politics* and it becomes clear that the virtues of the human are, for Aristotle, in large part the virtues of a human in a particular kind of community performing particular kinds of roles within it. Cicero, writing *De Officiis* as a letter to his son, is more explicit still. The book is organized around *officia* — duties — and the duties are the duties of the roles one occupies: of a son, of a citizen, of a friend, of a magistrate. The excellences of a life, for Cicero, are discovered by attending to the obligations of one's positions with a seriousness that eventually forms the person behind them.

This is virtue in the older and, we think, the more usable sense. It is local, specific, role-bound. It does not demand that a person become purified or exemplary in the abstract. It demands that she attend, with seriousness, to what her positions actually require of her, and that she let those requirements form her over time. The good citizen in Aristotle's sense is not a moral superhero; she is someone who has been formed by a particular kind of participation in a particular kind of political community into the dispositions that kind of community requires. The good judge is not a saint; he is someone who has let the practice of judging — its deliberative demands, its requirement of impartiality, its slow accumulation of case-wisdom — shape him into a person who can judge well. Virtue, in this register, is what happens when a person accepts her positions as formative rather than using them as stages.

The objection to civic-republican thought — that virtue is empty — assumes that modernity has eliminated roles and left only free-floating individuals in need of abstract moral codes. This is wrong. Modernity has not eliminated roles; it has multiplied them, scattered their formation, and severed many of them from the institutions that used to teach them. We have more roles now than any previous society: we are parents, children, spouses, siblings, friends, neighbors, employees, managers, citizens, voters, jurors, patients, practitioners, members of congregations, members of associations, users of platforms, participants in a dozen small publics we hardly have names for. The task of a usable virtue ethics in our moment is not to invent new virtues from scratch, nor to resurrect an ancient moral code. It is to recover the integrity of role as the vehicle of character, and to do that recovery one role at a time.

This is what Levin's question, taken seriously, asks us to do. *Given my position here, how shall I act?* The phrase "given my position" is the whole move. It tells the person that her moral life is not a general project of self-improvement but a local practice of fidelity. It tells her that the place to begin is not a theory but a location. And it tells her, by implication, that the great modern temptation — to treat every position as a platform for personal expression, every office as a stage, every role as an interruption of the real self — is not just a professional pathology. It is a moral failure of a particular kind, and there is a tradition that has known its shape for two and a half millennia.

***

What does the question look like, in practice, across the range of positions that a modern life actually contains? We want to be concrete, because the abstractions are easy and the specifics are where the chapter either earns its argument or does not. We will walk through a series of institutional roles. In each case the question is the same — *given my position here, how shall I act?* — and in each case the answer has a recognizable shape. The reader will not occupy all of these roles, but she will occupy some of them, and the family resemblance is part of the point.

Begin with the legislator. Volume II, in its chapter on the institutional decay of Congress, described the pathology that transforms a deliberative body into a platform. The member who treats her office as a social media presence rather than a legislative seat. The hearing conducted not as shared inquiry but as occasion for the harvesting of viral clips. The bill unread before the vote. The committee meeting attended by staff because the member is on television. The vote cast reflexively along party lines because the member has long since stopped forming an independent judgment about the substance. We described these patterns; we did not sneer at them; we tried to explain the incentive structure that produces them. It is not our place to moralize at the people trapped in that structure. Most of them did not set out to be what they have become.

But ask the question. *Given my position here, how shall I act?* The position is that of a legislator in a constitutional republic. The position has content. The legislator reads the bill. This sounds like a small thing; in present conditions it is a revolutionary thing. The legislator treats the committee hearing as an occasion for shared deliberation — for genuine questions whose answers she does not already know, for the airing of factual disagreements in a form that might let a reasonable listener decide between them — rather than as video content to be clipped and distributed. The legislator specializes. She learns the work of a particular policy area deeply, over years, in the way a craftsman learns a trade; she does not float above all subjects as a pundit does, opining on everything and understanding nothing. The legislator disagrees in private with her party on specific matters, and votes her considered judgment on some of them, knowing she will pay for it in the next primary and the next committee assignment and the next round of donor calls. This last is not heroism. It is the job. A legislator who has never disagreed with her party on a vote that mattered has not been performing the job; she has been performing the party. And the party, in turn, has been performing a national tribe. Somewhere in the chain, the legislative function has dropped out.

The legislator formed by her role is a different kind of person from the legislator formed by her platform. She has a subject matter. She has colleagues she works with across the aisle because the work, not the performance, is what she is trying to do. She is, in many cases, less famous than her platform-oriented colleague. She is also more useful to the republic, and she knows it, and the knowledge is part of what sustains her.

Consider the judge. A judge sits in a role whose entire content is impartial adjudication under law. The judge who asks the question finds that the role demands something specific of her prose. Her opinions explain the reasoning of the law in terms that a generalist reader could follow, and that the losing party could at least understand the logic of her loss. The opinions do not, whatever the temperature of the cultural moment, function as partisan signals, as dog-whistles to a political coalition, as content for the weekend talk shows. She recuses herself when recusal is appropriate; the question is answered by reference to the dignity of the office, not the preferences of the officeholder. She resists, patiently and without drama, the transformation of the judiciary into a content factory for partisan media. She knows that every opinion is a kind of teaching — that the republic learns, over the long arc of a judicial career, what the law is by reading the opinions its judges write — and she writes with the care appropriate to a teacher of citizens.

Both the judge formed by the practice of judging and the judge who uses the bench as a platform sit in the same chair and wear the same robe. Only one is doing the job.

Consider the professor. The professor occupies a position whose classical content is three-fold: the formation of the young, the advancement of knowledge, and the maintenance of a community of scholars across generations. The professor who asks *given my position here, how shall I act?* finds that the role demands something specific of her time. She treats teaching as sacred rather than as the burdensome administrative tax on her research career. She prepares — not because she needs to review material she knows, but because teaching is an occasion for thinking afresh, and the students deserve the thought. She reads broadly outside her specialization, because a scholar who is only in command of her specialty has, in some deep sense, forgotten what the university is for. She is willing to be changed by her students — to have her framings disturbed, her assumptions tested, her certainties softened — because the encounter that leaves both parties unchanged was not really a teaching encounter. She does not perform intellectual authority on social media as if the authority were a costume, detachable from the institutional practice that actually confers it. She is formed by the community of scholars rather than using her position to build a personal brand. She understands that a professor who has become, essentially, her own media outlet has left the profession even if she has kept the job.

This is not about fame or obscurity. There are famous professors who are still, recognizably, professors; there are obscure professors who have long since become something else. The question is whether the work is formed by the practice of the role or by the logic of the platform. The answer is visible to anyone paying attention, including, usually, the professor herself.

Consider the journalist. The position of the journalist — the reporter, in particular — has content the craft has been elaborating for a century. The reporter who asks *given my position here, how shall I act?* verifies. She acknowledges errors publicly and with specificity; she does not launder them into silent corrections or into the permanent amnesia of the feed. She protects her sources, at cost to herself if necessary, because the protection is what makes sources possible in the first place, and because the integrity of sourcing is the integrity of the craft. She writes against her own political sympathies when the facts demand it. This last is the discipline that distinguishes the reporter from the pamphleteer, and it is the discipline the current incentive structure of the press most relentlessly erodes. She resists the temptation to turn her Twitter following into her career ladder. She knows, or she learns, that the platform is not the profession — that a reporter who has accumulated a large social audience by performing her opinions has, whatever her official employment, ceased to be a reporter in any sense the craft has ever understood. She may be something else worth being. She is not that.

We are aware, writing these sentences in 2026, that this description of the reporter will strike many readers as antiquated. We are also aware that it describes a practice we used to have and could have again, and that the people who still attempt it in the diminished conditions of the contemporary press are among the most important civic practitioners in American life.

Consider the doctor. The physician occupies a role with one of the deepest and most carefully elaborated practice-traditions in Western civilization: the Hippocratic tradition, the long apprenticeship of medicine, the rounds, the morbidity-and-mortality conference, the consultation, the teaching hospital. The doctor who asks *given my position here, how shall I act?* continues to be formed by that tradition even after her training is officially complete. She participates in rounds; she consults colleagues when her knowledge reaches its limits; she attends the M-and-M conference not as a punitive ritual but as a practice of communal learning from error. She resists the brand temptation — the clinic that becomes a lifestyle outfit, the practice that becomes a social media channel, the treatment protocol that becomes a content strategy. She treats the physician-patient relationship as a vocation rather than a transaction, which is to say she is aware that the encounter imposes obligations on her that exceed the billable hour and are not negotiable.

The doctor so formed and the doctor who has allowed her practice to become a personal brand occupy the same profession in name. In practice they are doing different things, and the patients know it.

Consider the clergyperson. The pastor, the rabbi, the imam — each occupies a role whose content has been worked out over centuries, by traditions that have reflected on the relationship between office and person more carefully than almost any other institution in the civilizations they inhabit. The clergyperson who asks *given my position here, how shall I act?* serves the congregation rather than using it. She holds, with care and with humility, to the substantive claims of her tradition, rather than translating those claims into the therapeutic vernacular so that the congregation can hear them without being disturbed. She is willing to correct, gently and lovingly and persistently, because the pastoral role is not the role of an affirmer but of a shepherd, and the shepherd who never corrects has mistaken the job. She resists the temptation — acute in an age of platforms — to let the pulpit become a channel for her own politics or her own personality. The role is older than she is and is meant to outlast her.

This is not easy to hear for clergy formed in the last fifty years of American religion, which has, in many of its strands, drifted toward the therapeutic and the entrepreneurial. We are not scolding. We are naming a practice that the traditions themselves, at their best, have always known how to describe.

Consider the teacher. The K-12 teacher, in the American public school, sits in a role whose content is the formation of the young into persons capable of a shared life. The teacher who asks *given my position here, how shall I act?* regards the profession as that — formation — rather than as instruction-delivery, or as content curation, or as a front in the culture wars. She resists both the left-coded and the right-coded instrumentalizations of the classroom, because the classroom is not the place where one side wins. She is formed by a community of teachers across generations, by mentors and by the more experienced colleagues down the hall, rather than by the feeds in which teachers perform teaching for other teachers who will never meet them. She knows that the integrity of her practice is the integrity of her students' civic future, and she carries the weight of that knowledge into rooms where no one is watching her carry it.

Consider the parent. The parent occupies a role whose classical content is staggering and which our culture, for understandable reasons, has spent the last generation attempting to soften. The parent who asks *given my position here, how shall I act?* accepts that the role is binding — that it constrains her life, her choices, her time, her attention — and that the acceptance of the binding is itself a condition of doing the job well. She accepts that the work of parenting includes forming a child into a person capable of life in a free society: a person capable of self-government, of friendship across difference, of patience, of attention, of argument, of care. She is not primarily the child's friend, and she is not the child's brand manager. She is something older and harder: the adult the child is given so that the child can become an adult too. In our moment, this framing is countercultural. It used to be the default description of the role in every civilization we know of that endured long enough to write about it.

Consider, finally, the citizen. This is the role every reader of this book occupies, regardless of what else she does. The citizen who asks *given my position here, how shall I act?* votes informed, which is not a matter of voting more but of voting with some actual acquaintance with the choices in front of her. She serves on juries with gravity, treating the occasion not as an inconvenience to be evaded but as one of the few remaining institutions in American life that directly asks ordinary people to exercise judgment together. She attends a school board meeting, or a zoning meeting, or a county commission meeting, at least occasionally, because the small publics in which American self-government still happens at human scale do not staff themselves. She signs a petition after she has read it. She is willing to be a dissenter in her own camp when the case demands it — to say to the people who agree with her on most things that on this particular thing they are wrong — which is the specific discipline without which a free polity becomes a collection of tribes.

The citizen so formed is not a saint. She is, in a certain precise sense, a professional. She has taken the position seriously. She has allowed it to form her. That is the job.

***

Having walked through this catalogue, we must describe carefully what we are *not* saying. Two confusions are easy here, and the chapter will misfire if either is allowed to attach.

We are not preaching moralism. The person who asks "how should I act given my position" is not being told to feel guilty, to purify herself, to conduct a perpetual examination of her conscience, or to judge others in the language of the role she happens to occupy. Moralism is the tendency to convert every description of excellence into an accusation against those who fall short of it, and to convert every attention to one's own conduct into a form of performance. It is one of the recognizable diseases of the contemporary American public sphere, and it is present on both the left and the right, in different vocabularies. What we are describing is different from moralism in at least three ways. First, the demands of the role are specific and limited. The doctor is asked to be a good doctor, not a good person in the abstract; the parent is asked to parent well, not to achieve moral perfection; the citizen is asked to inhabit her civic role, not to become a theologian. Outside the specific demands of the specific positions one occupies, the person is free. Second, the attention is primarily directed at the self, not at others. The question is *how shall I act*, not *how should they act*. Third, the point of the attention is formation, not judgment. The work is slow, local, imperfect, and largely unobserved. It does not produce a satisfying affective payoff of the kind moralism thrives on. It produces, over time, a person who can do the job.

This is virtue ethics in the classical sense — attentive, local, role-bound, practiced — not in the sentimentalized contemporary sense that reduces virtue to good feelings and moral seriousness to the public expression of one's correctness. The sentimentalized version produces the cultural climate we have. The classical version, recovered one role at a time, produces something else.

We are also not preaching submission. The role has content, but the person is not dissolved into it. Alasdair MacIntyre's image of the *practice*, worked out in *After Virtue*, is useful here, and we think it is worth pausing on. A practice, MacIntyre writes, is a coherent and complex shared activity with standards of excellence internal to it. To enter the practice is to accept the authority of those standards, and excellence within the practice — in medicine, in teaching, in journalism, in the law, in farming, in fishing, in any practice that has such internal standards — forms the practitioner into a certain kind of person. This is the core of our argument. But MacIntyre is clear, and so are we, that a practice can itself be criticized, reformed, extended. The virtues of the practitioner include the virtues necessary to see when the practice has drifted, and to intervene in its reform. Role-fidelity is not role-captivity. The good journalist who sees her craft being hollowed out by a new set of incentives has an obligation arising *from the craft itself* to name the hollowing and to work against it. The good doctor who sees medicine drifting toward brand-management has an obligation that arises from medicine. The good citizen who sees the civic role being redefined in ways inimical to the republic has an obligation that arises from citizenship. The role forms the person; the person, so formed, is among the people capable of reforming the role. This is how practices stay alive, and how, when they die, they are sometimes resurrected.

The two confusions — moralism and submission — are, in our experience, the main barriers to a contemporary reader actually hearing a virtue-ethical argument. We have tried to set them aside. What remains, we think, is an ethic a modern person can use.

***

An individual, asking Levin's question with all the seriousness in the world, cannot succeed alone in an institution whose structures punish role-integrity. This has to be said hard, because the rest of the chapter collapses into exhortation if it is not. The chapter is not an invitation to a personal piety. It is an invitation to a practice that only works if shared, and whose sharing depends on institutional conditions we have to be honest about.

Consider first the question of incentives. Institutions transmit signals about what behavior pays, and most Americans, most of the time, respond to those signals. A legislature whose incentive structure rewards viral moments and punishes quiet substance will, on average, produce legislators who produce viral moments; the exceptions will be exceptional and few. A media economy that rewards outrage clicks and punishes careful corrections will, on average, produce reporters who maximize the first and minimize the second. A medical system that rewards throughput and penalizes the unbillable conversation will produce physicians who have stopped having the conversation. The platform pathology Volume II described is not a mystery. It is an incentive structure operating on persons who, by and large, are neither especially good nor especially bad. Changing behavior requires changing the incentives, and that is the slow structural work that neither side of our politics is particularly interested in, because both sides benefit, in different ways, from the incentives as they stand.

Consider second the question of formation. The virtues a role requires are not native to the persons who enter the role. They are transmitted by apprenticeship — by the relationship between the senior practitioner and the junior one, in which the norms of the practice are taught, not abstractly but through the thousand small corrections that accumulate over years of work done alongside. Medicine still has something of this, at its best, in residency. The law has it in the relationship between junior associates and senior partners in the firms that still do it properly. The academy has it in the dissertation advisor-student relationship. Journalism once had it in the editor-reporter relationship. The clergy, in most traditions, formally enshrines it. In each case, the relationship has eroded over the last generation. The senior practitioner, stretched thin and incentivized toward personal productivity, has less time for the junior. The junior, in turn, is less formed, and transmits less to those who follow her. Over two or three generations, a practice can lose the memory of its own standards without anyone noticing until the absence becomes catastrophic. Rebuilding apprenticeship is not glamorous. It is one of the most important forms of institutional repair available to us.

Consider third the question of social standing. The standing of a role depends on the role being seen, by the broader public, to do its work with integrity. When a profession ceases to do its work with integrity, its social standing erodes, and the erosion in turn degrades the incentives for entering the profession, and the degradation produces a worse cohort of entrants. The spiral is real. The doctor who treats her clinic as a personal brand does not only fail, personally, to do her job. She contributes to the public erosion of the social meaning of doctoring. The journalist who uses his reporting as a platform for his tribe's politics makes it harder for the journalist in the next cubicle over, who is still trying, to be trusted by readers. The legislator who treats the office as content teaches the public that legislating is not really what legislators do. Each individual defection is a tiny contribution to the ambient distrust, and the ambient distrust is one of the conditions under which future entrants to the profession cannot sustain the practice even if they want to. This is why the question of role-integrity is not merely a personal question. It is a civic one. A single person's fidelity to her role is a small public good. Enough single fidelities, visibly sustained over time, can reverse the spiral.

***

What, then, can be done at the structural level? We said in Chapter 15 that this volume would be honest about what is hard, and this is the chapter where that promise has to be kept. There is no policy lever that produces role-integrity in the abstract. But there are structural conditions that make role-integrity easier or harder to practice, and there are things that could be done, at various scales, to tilt those conditions.

Professional associations are among the most important and most neglected levers. The American Bar Association, the American Medical Association, the American Historical Association, the American Academy of Arts and Sciences, their peers in every profession — these are, at their origin and in their best form, *molding* institutions. They exist to maintain the substantive content of the professions they represent: to write and revise codes of ethics, to set standards of training, to articulate what it means to do the work well, to discipline the members who fall conspicuously short, and to represent the profession to the broader public with some measure of authority. Over the last two decades, many have been subject to the same pathologies we have described elsewhere: political capture, internal polarization, organizational drift, the substitution of public positioning for substantive practice-maintenance. Their rehabilitation is not a small matter. An organized profession is among the most powerful structural allies role-integrity has; a disorganized or captured one is among its most effective enemies. The reader who is a member of such an association and has long since stopped going to the meetings might consider that the meetings are now worth her time in a way they were not when she last attended. Internal reform, led by the members who actually practice the professions, is among the most available structural interventions we have.

Educational institutions are the second lever. Schools from K-12 through graduate professional training exist, in their classical self-understanding, to form persons. They do not only credential, and they do not only instruct; they form. In the current political environment, the formative mission of education is contested from all sides, and under contestation it has tended, defensively, to contract. Schools justify themselves in terms of measurable outcomes — test scores, credentials, employment rates — that are easier to defend than the older, harder claim that they are forming the persons who will inhabit a free polity. We understand the contraction. It is also a disaster, because if the educational institutions will not own the formative mission, no other institution in a secular, pluralist society is well-positioned to own it instead. Rebuilding the formative mission of education requires a long, unglamorous, cross-ideological coalition willing to defend the idea that the classroom is, among other things, a place where persons are made. The coalition does not yet exist. It could.

Regulatory and accountability structures are the third lever. Many of the structural features that protect role-performance against platform-performance are older institutional forms whose function is not always well understood. Peer review in science is one. Editorial structures in journalism — the editor, the fact-checker, the standards desk, the corrections policy — are another. Committee structures in legislatures, with their specialization, their institutional memory, their apprenticeship of junior members to senior ones, are another. Each imposes friction between the practitioner and the public performance of her practice, and the friction is what allows the practice to be itself. Each has been eroded by the logics of speed, publicity, and platform exposure. Re-engineering these structures — protecting peer review from political capture, re-funding editorial infrastructure, rebuilding committee systems hollowed out by leadership-office centralization — is technical work that does not produce viral content. It is the kind of work without which the roles we have been describing cannot be practiced well.

The fourth lever is the one we have undersold to this point in the book, and it requires its own paragraph. Religious institutions, in the United States, are among the most robust remaining *molding* institutions in American civic life. They are, at their best, exactly the kind of practice MacIntyre describes: shared activities with internal standards of excellence, sustained across generations by apprenticeship, anchored in traditions older than any individual member, organized to form persons rather than to credential or entertain them. We have not foregrounded this in earlier chapters, partly because we are not writing a theological book, and partly because we are writing for a readership that contains many members of traditions and many members of none. We think the omission was a mistake, and we want to correct it now. The preservation and renewal of religious institutions in this country is not only a theological matter. It is a civic matter. A secular polity that treats its religious institutions as hostile territory — as embarrassments to be tolerated or as adversaries to be constrained — is discarding, without quite meaning to, one of the most durable forms of character formation its members are likely ever to encounter. Friends of civic life on both the right and the left should take this seriously. The right's version of taking it seriously cannot be the capture of the state by one tradition's theology; the left's version cannot be the bureaucratic pressure against traditions whose substantive claims are out of step with the current progressive consensus. Both of those moves damage the formative institutions they are trying, respectively, to weaponize or to manage. What is needed instead is something harder and older: a civic recognition that the synagogue, the church, the mosque, the temple, the meeting-house, at their best, are among the places where the kind of person a republic needs is still being made, and that their maintenance is therefore a matter of common interest, whatever one's personal relationship to the traditions involved.

We make this argument without theological prejudice. The believer will read into it her own tradition's understanding of its mission. The reader who is not a believer at all is being asked to see something about the civic ecology of the country in which she lives, which depends in part on institutions whose members hold commitments she does not share. Pluralism is supposed to describe exactly this situation. We think it is time to mean it.

***

The structural levers are real, and some of the readers of this book will be in positions to pull them, and we hope those readers will. But the majority of readers will not be board members of professional associations, university presidents, media executives, or national religious leaders. The question for the majority is more modest, and more immediate: *what can I, in the positions I actually occupy, do?*

The answer begins with the question itself. Ask it, seriously and regularly. *Given my position here, how should I act?* Given that I am a manager, how do I treat my direct reports? Given that I am a sibling, how do I act in my family's conflicts? Given that I am an employee, what do I owe my employer beyond what is contractually required, and what do I owe my colleagues that my employer cannot reach? Given that I am a member of a congregation, how do I participate in its life in a way that serves the congregation rather than my own self-image? Given that I am a neighbor, what is the neighborhood owed from me this week, this month, this year? Given that I am a citizen, what is the small public within which I actually practice citizenship, and when am I next going to show up to it?

The questions are not dramatic. They do not lend themselves to social media posts. They are the questions that, asked and answered over a life, make a person into the kind of person a free society requires.

Treat your roles as sources of content for the moral life, not as interruptions of it. This is a specific inversion, and it is worth stating directly. The contemporary default framing — reinforced by almost every vector of contemporary culture — is that the "real self" is the self standing outside of roles, that roles are masks or obligations or constraints imposed on the authentic person by external circumstance, and that the task of the moral life is to realize the authentic self despite the roles. We think this framing is close to the reverse of true. The authentic self, in our experience, is almost always found in the careful inhabiting of the roles one actually occupies. The question "what kind of person should I be" is most often answered well not by constructing an abstract project of self-realization but by attending, seriously, to the positions one is in. The parent who attends carefully to the work of parenting discovers, over years, the kind of person she has become in doing it. The doctor who attends to medicine becomes a physician in a way no self-conception project could have produced. The citizen who shows up to the zoning meeting becomes, by the showing up, a citizen in a way no declaration of identity could have accomplished. Role-attention is not the enemy of self-realization. It is, for most people, its only reliable vehicle.

Resist the platform temptation within your own role. This is specific too. Do not use your workplace as a stage for an external audience. Do not use your family to perform authenticity online. Do not use your civic engagement as a source of content. The temptation is present in every role, because the platforms are present in every role; the discipline of not yielding to the temptation is one of the core moral disciplines of our moment. The discipline is not an ascetic rejection of all self-expression. It is the distinction between the role as a practice, which belongs to the persons and institutions it serves, and the role as a platform, which belongs to the performer and her audience. A parent who is still present to her actual children even while parenting is also documented is a different thing from a parent whose parenting is subordinated to its documentation. A professional who mentions her work on social media is a different thing from a professional whose work has been reorganized around what will play well on social media. The line between these is not always sharp, but the direction of drift is usually clear, and each of us, in each of our roles, has a sense of which side of the line we have been living on lately. The discipline is to notice and to correct.

Apprentice yourself, in some form. Find a more senior practitioner in your line of work or your calling, and learn from her. Cultivate the relationship. Ask her questions. Let yourself be corrected by her. Do not do this as a networking strategy; do it as a practice of formation, because formation is what apprenticeship is for. And, as you grow in the role, make yourself available to the juniors coming up behind you. Pass what you received. This is how practices stay alive across generations. It is also how, in the many roles where the formal apprenticeship structures have decayed, the informal ones can be rebuilt from the bottom up. We know of no more important form of voluntary civic work available to the ordinary reader of a book like this, and we know of almost no attention to it in the contemporary literature of civic renewal. It deserves more.

***

The person who is formed by her roles is the opposite of the atomized individual who is raw material for the mass movement. This is the connection, between this chapter and the book's larger thesis, that we have been building toward. It is worth stating now in its bare form.

Volume II, following Arendt and Hoffer, argued that the conditions of modern life produce a certain kind of person — hollow, unformed, unmoored from the particular associations that used to give a human life its texture — and that this person is the reservoir from which mass movements recruit. The atomized individual is not, as the nineteenth century sometimes hoped, a free person standing on his own reason. He is, in Arendt's harsh description, lonely raw material. His loneliness is itself the door through which the movement enters. He has no content of his own, so he is available to be filled by someone else's content. This is why cults, as Hoffer saw, are interchangeable at their psychological core. The content can be almost anything. The hollowness is the condition.

Role-formation is the reversal of this. A doctor formed by medicine over twenty years of practice is not a hollow vessel waiting for a tribal identity. She is too full of content to be captured.
